    Fork-based session management (preview):

    Fork-based sessions let you explore divergent approaches from a shared analysis baseline. This is covered in full in Lab 1.7, but here's the key concept:

    # After initial codebase analysis, fork to compare two approaches
> Fork this session to compare: (A) pytest unit tests vs (B) integration tests for the refund module

    Each fork inherits the shared analysis but explores independently. The coordinator (or developer) compares results without polluting either branch.

    Exam concept: fork_session creates independent branches from a shared baseline. Subagents can't share context between forks — each fork is isolated.

    Code Analysis

    Exercise 1 — What happens if you remove source attribution from the handoff format? Look at the build_research_handoff() function. Each finding includes "source" and "date" fields alongside the "claim". If you strip these out, the synthesis agent receives claims with no provenance — the final report becomes unverifiable. This is why structured handoffs separate content from metadata.

    Exercise 2 — Why do goal-based prompts outperform step-by-step prompts? Compare the two prompt styles in demonstrate_goal_based_prompt(). The step-by-step version prescribes exact searches ("search for EV market 2023"). If the search returns nothing, the agent is stuck. The goal-based version says "cover market size, growth rate, and key barriers" — the agent adapts its search queries based on what it finds.

    Exercise 3 — What is the latency difference between sequential and parallel subagent spawning? In the parallel pattern, all Task tool calls appear in a single coordinator response and execute concurrently. Sequential spawning takes the sum of all subagent runtimes; parallel takes only the longest single runtime. The exam tests whether you know that multiple Task calls in ONE response = parallel execution.

    fork_session — Independent Exploration Branches

    fork_session creates independent branches from a shared analysis baseline. Each branch explores a different approach without contaminating the other.

    # Shared baseline: both branches start from the same codebase analysis
# Branch A explores refactoring to class-based components
# Branch B explores refactoring to functional components with hooks
# Results are independent — Branch A's findings don't affect Branch B

# In Claude Code CLI:
# 1. Analyze the codebase in a named session
# 2. Fork to explore approach A
# 3. Fork again (from the original baseline) to explore approach B
# 4. Compare results and pick the better approach

    Why fork instead of just starting two separate sessions? Forks share the initial analysis baseline — you don't repeat the exploration phase. Each fork inherits the shared understanding but diverges independently from that point.

    When to fork vs when to start fresh: Fork when you want to compare approaches from the same starting point. Start fresh when the starting context itself is stale or degraded (see Lab 1.7 for the full decision framework).

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "subagent lacks context from prior agents" → pass findings explicitly in the prompt
    • Wrong answer trap: "Share a database between agents" — subagents have isolated context by design
    • Wrong answer trap: "Use a shared memory store" — the Agent SDK doesn't provide shared state
    • The correct pattern: Include complete findings from prior agents directly in the subagent's prompt

    ✅ Lab 1.3 Checkpoint

    • Subagents need Task in allowedTools to spawn sub-subagents
    • Multiple Task calls in ONE response = parallel execution
    • Synthesis prompt must include ALL prior findings explicitly
    • Structured handoff format separates content from metadata (sources/dates)
    • Goal-based prompts > step-by-step instructions for subagent adaptability
    • fork_session creates independent exploration branches from a shared baseline
    • Fork = same baseline, different approaches; Fresh = new baseline entirely

    LAB 1.4: PREREQUISITE GATES AND HANDOFF PATTERNS

    Domain 1 — Task Statement 1.4 Programmatic enforcement vs prompt guidance ~20 minutesAdvanced | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. How do you spawn parallel subagents? (Lab 1.3)
    2. Why are goal-based prompts better than step-by-step prompts for subagents? (Lab 1.3)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your support agent has three tools: get_customer, lookup_order, and process_refund. The system prompt says "always verify the customer first." But in 12% of cases, the agent skips get_customer and calls lookup_order using only the customer's stated name — occasionally refunding the wrong person.

    You can't fix this with a better prompt. Even "CRITICAL: ALWAYS verify first" fails sometimes. When mistakes have financial consequences, you need a guarantee, not a probability.

    The solution: A programmatic gate — code that blocks lookup_order and process_refund until get_customer has returned a verified customer ID. The model literally cannot skip the step.

    Exam connection (Sample Q1): This is the exam's signature question. Programmatic enforcement beats prompt-based enforcement when compliance matters. Every wrong answer on this topic uses prompts where gates are needed.

    What You'll Learn

    • Prerequisite gates: Code that blocks tool B until tool A has completed
    • Why prompts fail: Non-zero failure rate on required sequences
    • Escalation thresholds: Amounts > $500 → redirect to human, enforced in code
    • Multi-concern decomposition: Customer raises 3 issues → investigate each → unified response

    See It In Action

    Open Claude Code and try this:

    Before you make any changes to files in this project, first verify there are no uncommitted changes by running git status. Only proceed if the working directory is clean.

    Notice how Claude Code checks a prerequisite (clean working directory) before taking the main action. This is the same pattern as a prerequisite gate: tool B (make changes) is blocked until tool A (verify status) confirms it is safe to proceed. In production systems, this verification is enforced programmatically — not just suggested in a prompt.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d1-agentic-arch/prerequisite_gate.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

CUSTOMERS = {
    "jane@email.com": {"id": "CUST-001", "name": "Jane Smith", "tier": "gold"},
    "bob@email.com":  {"id": "CUST-002", "name": "Bob Jones",  "tier": "silver"}
}
ORDERS = {
    "ORD-555": {"id": "ORD-555", "customer_id": "CUST-001", "total": 149.99, "status": "shipped"},
    "ORD-777": {"id": "ORD-777", "customer_id": "CUST-002", "total": 29.99,  "status": "processing"}
}

verified_customer_id = None  # Gate state

def execute_tool(name, input_data):
    global verified_customer_id

    if name == "get_customer":
        c = CUSTOMERS.get(input_data.get("email", ""))
        if c:
            verified_customer_id = c["id"]
            print(f"    ✓ Customer verified: {verified_customer_id}")
            return json.dumps(c)
        return json.dumps({"error": "No customer found"})

    elif name == "lookup_order":
        # ── PROGRAMMATIC GATE ──
        if verified_customer_id is None:
            print(f"    ✗ BLOCKED: get_customer must succeed first")
            return json.dumps({
                "isError": True,
                "errorCategory": "validation",
                "isRetryable": False,
                "message": "Customer verification required before order lookup. Call get_customer first."
            })
        o = ORDERS.get(input_data.get("order_id", ""))
        return json.dumps(o) if o else json.dumps({"error": "Order not found"})

    elif name == "process_refund":
        # ── PROGRAMMATIC GATE ──
        if verified_customer_id is None:
            return json.dumps({
                "isError": True, "errorCategory": "validation",
                "isRetryable": False,
                "message": "Customer verification required. Call get_customer first."
            })
        amount = input_data.get("amount", 0)
        # ── POLICY GATE (PostToolUse hook pattern) ──
        if amount > 500:
            print(f"    ✗ BLOCKED: ${amount} exceeds $500 policy limit")
            return json.dumps({
                "isError": True, "errorCategory": "business",
                "isRetryable": False,
                "message": f"Refund of ${amount} exceeds $500 auto-approval limit.",
                "customer_friendly_message": "Your refund requires manager approval. You'll hear back within 24 hours.",
                "results_may_exist": False
            })
        return json.dumps({"success": True, "refund_amount": amount,
                           "customer_id": verified_customer_id})

    elif name == "escalate_to_human":
        summary = input_data.get("summary", "")
        customer_id = input_data.get("customer_id", verified_customer_id)
        print(f"    → ESCALATING to human agent")
        print(f"      Customer: {customer_id}")
        print(f"      Summary: {summary[:100]}")
        return json.dumps({
            "escalated": True,
            "ticket_id": "TKT-9999",
            "message": "Case transferred to human agent"
        })

    return json.dumps({"error": f"Unknown tool: {name}"})

tools = [
    {
        "name": "get_customer",
        "description": "Retrieves customer by email. MUST be called before any order or refund operations.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object", "properties": {"email": {"type": "string"}}, "required": ["email"]}
    },
    {
        "name": "lookup_order",
        "description": "Gets order details by order ID. Requires prior get_customer call.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object", "properties": {"order_id": {"type": "string"}}, "required": ["order_id"]}
    },
    {
        "name": "process_refund",
        "description": "Processes a refund. Requires get_customer first. Refunds over $500 go to human escalation.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"order_id": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}},
                         "required": ["order_id", "amount"]}
    },
    {
        "name": "escalate_to_human",
        "description": "Escalates to a human agent. Use when: customer requests human, policy gap exists, refund exceeds $500, or no progress after investigation.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {
                             "customer_id": {"type": "string"},
                             "summary": {"type": "string"},
                             "root_cause": {"type": "string"},
                             "recommended_action": {"type": "string"}
                         },
                         "required": ["summary"]}
    }
]

def run_agent(user_message):
    global verified_customer_id
    verified_customer_id = None  # Reset gate for each session
    print(f"\n{'='*60}\nUSER: {user_message}\n{'='*60}\n")
    messages = [{"role": "user", "content": user_message}]
    system = """You are a customer support agent.
ESCALATE when: customer explicitly requests human, policy gap, can't make progress, refund > $500.
Always verify customer identity with get_customer before any other operations."""

    while True:
        response = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=1024,
            system=system, tools=tools, messages=messages
        )
        print(f"    stop_reason: {response.stop_reason}")
        if response.stop_reason == "end_turn":
            text = "".join(b.text for b in response.content if hasattr(b, "text"))
            print(f"\nRESPONSE: {text}\n")
            return
        elif response.stop_reason == "tool_use":
            messages.append({"role": "assistant", "content": response.content})
            results = []
            for b in response.content:
                if b.type == "tool_use":
                    print(f"    Tool: {b.name}({json.dumps(b.input)})")
                    results.append({"type": "tool_result", "tool_use_id": b.id,
                                    "content": execute_tool(b.name, b.input)})
            messages.append({"role": "user", "content": results})
        else:
            break

if __name__ == "__main__":
    run_agent("I'm jane@email.com, I want a $50 refund for order ORD-555")
    run_agent("I'm jane@email.com, I want a $750 refund for order ORD-555")
    run_agent("I want to speak to a manager right now")

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    Create the file lab-d1-agentic-arch/prerequisite_gate.py with the code above and run it

    What you should see:

    • Test 1: get_customer ✓ → lookup_order ✓ → process_refund($50) ✓
    • Test 2: get_customer ✓ → lookup_order ✓ → process_refund($750) ✗ → escalate_to_human
    • Test 3: escalate_to_human immediately (no investigation)

    Why prompt-only enforcement fails: Consider what happens if you replace the programmatic gate with system="IMPORTANT: Always call get_customer before anything else." and remove the code-level check. Run it five times — it will NOT always call get_customer first. Prompt instructions have a non-zero failure rate. Financial operations need programmatic gates.

    Reference Implementation: Multi-concern request decomposition

    The exam tests: "Decomposing multi-concern customer requests into distinct items, then investigating each in parallel using shared context before synthesizing a resolution."

    import anthropic, json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

def handle_multi_concern_request(customer_email: str, concerns: list) -> str:
    """
    Multi-concern pattern:
    1. Identify distinct issues in the customer's request
    2. Investigate each concern independently (parallel tool calls)
    3. Synthesize a unified response covering all concerns

    The agent uses shared context (verified customer) across all investigations.
    """
    CUSTOMERS = {"jane@email.com": {"id": "CUST-001", "name": "Jane Smith"}}
    ORDERS = {
        "ORD-555": {"status": "shipped", "total": 149.99, "issue": "reported damaged"},
        "ORD-556": {"status": "delayed", "total": 89.99, "eta": "3 days late"}
    }

    # Simulate what the agent discovers per concern
    findings = []
    for concern in concerns:
        if "refund" in concern.lower() or "damaged" in concern.lower():
            findings.append({
                "concern": concern,
                "finding": "ORD-555 shipped damaged — eligible for full refund of $149.99",
                "action": "process_refund(ORD-555, 149.99)"
            })
        elif "delay" in concern.lower() or "late" in concern.lower():
            findings.append({
                "concern": concern,
                "finding": "ORD-556 delayed 3 days — carrier exception filed",
                "action": "apply_courtesy_credit(10.00)"
            })
        elif "billing" in concern.lower():
            findings.append({
                "concern": concern,
                "finding": "Billing query needs account review",
                "action": "escalate_billing_team"
            })

    # Synthesize unified response
    response_parts = [f"I've investigated all {len(concerns)} issues for your account:\n"]
    for i, f in enumerate(findings, 1):
        response_parts.append(f"{i}. {f['concern']}: {f['finding']} → {f['action']}")

    print(f"\nMulti-concern handling for {customer_email}:")
    print(f"Concerns identified: {len(concerns)}")
    for c in concerns:
        print(f"  - {c}")
    print(f"\nResolution:")
    print("\n".join(response_parts))
    return "\n".join(response_parts)


# Test: customer with 3 separate issues in one message
handle_multi_concern_request(
    "jane@email.com",
    [
        "My order ORD-555 arrived damaged — I want a refund",
        "My other order ORD-556 is 3 days late",
        "There's an unexpected charge on my bill"
    ]
)

print("""
KEY EXAM CONCEPT:
Multi-concern = identify all distinct issues first, THEN investigate each.
Not: handle first issue, respond, wait for next message.
Not: only handle the first issue mentioned.

The agent should:
1. Identify N distinct concerns
2. Investigate each (can be parallel tool calls in one response)
3. Synthesize ONE unified response covering all N concerns
""")

    Code Analysis

    Exercise 1 — What happens if you remove the prerequisite gate and rely only on the system prompt? Look at the gate check: if verified_customer_id is None. This is code-level enforcement — the tool literally cannot execute without prior verification. If you replace this with a system prompt instruction ("always verify customer first"), the agent will skip verification in some percentage of cases. Prompt enforcement is probabilistic; programmatic gates are deterministic.

    Exercise 2 — What happens if you remove the $500 threshold gate? The if amount > 500 check blocks high-value refunds programmatically. Without it, the agent processes a $750 refund automatically — a financial policy violation. The structured error response (errorCategory: "business", isRetryable: false) tells the agent to escalate rather than retry.

    Exercise 3 — What happens when a multi-concern request is handled without decomposition? Look at handle_multi_concern_request() — it iterates through ALL concerns and investigates each one. Without this pattern, the agent typically fixates on the first issue mentioned and forgets the others. The exam tests: identify all N concerns first, investigate each (parallel tool calls), then synthesize ONE unified response.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "agent skips a required step X% of the time" → programmatic gate, NOT prompt improvement
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add 'CRITICAL' to the system prompt" — still probabilistic, non-zero failure rate
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add few-shot examples of correct ordering" — still prompt-based, still can fail
    • The correct pattern: Programmatic prerequisite that blocks downstream tools until prerequisite completes

    ✅ Lab 1.4 Checkpoint

    • Programmatic gates block tool calls until prerequisites complete
    • Business policy violations return errorCategory: "business", isRetryable: false
    • Structured escalation handoff includes customer_id, summary, root_cause, recommended_action
    • Prompt-only enforcement is probabilistic — unacceptable for financial operations
    • Multi-concern requests: identify all concerns → investigate in parallel → unified response

    LAB 1.5: AGENT SDK HOOKS FOR TOOL INTERCEPTION

    Domain 1 — Task Statement 1.5 PostToolUse hooks, normalization, compliance enforcement ~20 minutesIntermediate

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. Why can't prompt instructions alone guarantee that get_customer is called before process_refund? (Lab 1.4)
    2. What should a structured escalation handoff include? (Lab 1.4)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your customer support agent calls 5 different MCP tools. Each returns dates in a different format: "3/15/2025", "2025-03-15", "March 15th, 2025". Claude gets confused and sometimes compares dates wrong. You could add "normalize dates" to the system prompt, but that's probabilistic — it works 95% of the time. For a production agent, 95% isn't good enough.

    The solution: hooks. A PostToolUse hook intercepts every tool result and normalizes dates to ISO 8601 format before Claude sees them. Claude never encounters inconsistent dates. A PreToolUse hook intercepts tool calls before they execute — you can block policy-violating operations (e.g., refunds over $500) with 100% reliability.

    The key insight (exam pattern): Hooks provide deterministic guarantees. Prompts provide probabilistic guidance. When compliance matters, use hooks.

    What You'll Learn

    • PostToolUse hooks: Transform tool results after execution but before the model processes them (normalization, filtering, enrichment)
    • PreToolUse hooks: Intercept and block tool calls before execution (policy enforcement, threshold checks)
    • When to use hooks vs prompts: Hooks = 100% enforcement for compliance/financial rules. Prompts = guidance for stylistic/behavioral preferences

    Reference Implementation: lab-d1-agentic-arch/hooks_demo.py

    import json
from datetime import datetime

# ── PostToolUse Hook: Data Normalization ──────────────────
# Different MCP tools return dates in different formats.
# This hook normalizes ALL date formats to ISO 8601
# before the model sees the results.

def post_tool_use_normalize(tool_name: str, tool_result: dict) -> dict:
    """
    PostToolUse hook: normalize heterogeneous data formats.

    Called AFTER a tool executes, BEFORE results go to the model.
    This is where you fix:
    - Unix timestamps → ISO 8601
    - Numeric status codes → human-readable strings
    - Currency cents → formatted dollars
    - Inconsistent field names across tools
    """

    # Normalize Unix timestamps
    if "created_at" in tool_result:
        raw = tool_result["created_at"]
        if isinstance(raw, int):  # Unix timestamp
            tool_result["created_at"] = datetime.fromtimestamp(raw).isoformat()
            print(f"  [HOOK] Normalized Unix timestamp: {raw} → {tool_result['created_at']}")

    # Normalize status codes
    STATUS_MAP = {1: "active", 2: "suspended", 3: "closed", 0: "pending"}
    if "status" in tool_result and isinstance(tool_result["status"], int):
        raw = tool_result["status"]
        tool_result["status"] = STATUS_MAP.get(raw, f"unknown({raw})")
        print(f"  [HOOK] Normalized status code: {raw} → {tool_result['status']}")

    # Normalize currency (cents to dollars)
    if "amount_cents" in tool_result:
        cents = tool_result.pop("amount_cents")
        tool_result["amount"] = f"${cents / 100:.2f}"
        print(f"  [HOOK] Normalized currency: {cents} cents → {tool_result['amount']}")

    return tool_result


# ── PreToolUse Hook: Policy Enforcement ──────────────────
# Intercepts OUTGOING tool calls before they execute.
# Returns None to allow, or an error dict to block.

def pre_tool_use_enforce(tool_name: str, tool_input: dict) -> dict | None:
    """
    PreToolUse hook: enforce compliance rules on outgoing tool calls.

    Return None → allow the call
    Return error dict → block the call (model sees the error response)

    WHY HOOKS, NOT PROMPTS:
    - Hooks provide guaranteed enforcement (100% compliance)
    - Prompts are probabilistic (99.x% at best)
    - For financial thresholds and compliance rules: always use hooks
    """

    if tool_name == "process_refund":
        amount = tool_input.get("amount", 0)
        if amount > 500:
            print(f"  [HOOK] BLOCKED process_refund: ${amount} > $500 limit")
            return {
                "isError": True,
                "errorCategory": "business",
                "isRetryable": False,
                "message": f"Refund of ${amount} exceeds auto-approval threshold of $500.",
                "customer_friendly_message": "This refund requires manager approval.",
                "redirect": "escalate_to_human"
            }
        if amount <= 0:
            print(f"  [HOOK] BLOCKED process_refund: invalid amount ${amount}")
            return {
                "isError": True,
                "errorCategory": "validation",
                "isRetryable": False,
                "message": "Refund amount must be greater than $0."
            }

    if tool_name == "delete_account":
        print(f"  [HOOK] BLOCKED delete_account: requires human review")
        return {
            "isError": True,
            "errorCategory": "permission",
            "isRetryable": False,
            "message": "Account deletion requires human agent authorization."
        }

    return None  # Allow all other calls


# ── Demonstration ─────────────────────────────────────────

def simulate_tool_pipeline():
    print("=== PostToolUse Hook: Data Normalization ===\n")

    # Simulate raw results from different MCP tools
    crm_result = {
        "customer_id": "CUST-001",
        "created_at": 1704067200,  # Unix timestamp
        "status": 1,               # Numeric status code
        "amount_cents": 14999      # Cents
    }
    print(f"Raw CRM result: {crm_result}")
    normalized = post_tool_use_normalize("get_customer", crm_result)
    print(f"Normalized:     {normalized}")

    print("\n=== PreToolUse Hook: Policy Enforcement ===\n")

    # Test cases
    test_calls = [
        ("process_refund", {"order_id": "ORD-555", "amount": 50}),
        ("process_refund", {"order_id": "ORD-555", "amount": 750}),
        ("process_refund", {"order_id": "ORD-555", "amount": -10}),
        ("delete_account", {"customer_id": "CUST-001"}),
        ("lookup_order",   {"order_id": "ORD-555"}),
    ]

    for tool_name, tool_input in test_calls:
        print(f"\nTool call: {tool_name}({json.dumps(tool_input)})")
        block = pre_tool_use_enforce(tool_name, tool_input)
        if block:
            print(f"  BLOCKED → model sees: {json.dumps(block)[:80]}...")
        else:
            print(f"  ALLOWED → tool executes normally")

    print("\n=== Key Principle ===")
    print("""
    Hooks vs Prompts:
    - Hooks:   guaranteed enforcement (programmatic, 100%)
    - Prompts: probabilistic guidance (~95-99%, not 100%)

    Use hooks for:
    - Financial thresholds ($500 refund limit)
    - Data format normalization across heterogeneous tools
    - Compliance-mandated blocking (delete requires human)
    - Audit logging of all tool calls

    Use prompts for:
    - Behavioral guidance ("be empathetic")
    - Decision-making ("escalate when customer is frustrated")
    - Output formatting preferences
    """)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    simulate_tool_pipeline()

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    Create lab-d1-agentic-arch/hooks_demo.py with the code above and run it

    What you should see:

    • PostToolUse: dates in 3 formats → all normalized to ISO 8601 (2025-03-15)
    • PreToolUse: refund of $50 → ALLOWED. Refund of $750 → BLOCKED with policy error
    • Summary showing hooks = deterministic (100%) vs prompts = probabilistic (<100%)

    Exam connection (Sample Q1 pattern): When a business rule requires 100% compliance (financial operations, identity verification), use hooks/gates — not prompt instructions. This is the #1 exam pattern.

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens without PostToolUse date normalization? If dates arrive in 3 formats ("3/15/2025", "2025-03-15", "March 15th"), Claude's comparisons become unreliable — inconsistent formats cause comparison errors.
    2. Can a system prompt replace a PreToolUse hook for refund limits? No — prompt enforcement ("Never process refunds over $500") has a non-zero failure rate. Over 10 requests, some will slip through. Hooks provide 100% enforcement.
    3. What's the difference between a hook and a gate? A gate blocks based on prior state — "has customer been verified?" A hook intercepts every call — "is this amount over $500?"

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "100% compliance required on a business rule" → hooks/gates, NOT prompts
    • Wrong answer trap: "Reduce temperature to 0" — temperature affects randomness, not compliance
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add the rule to system prompt with emphasis" — still probabilistic
    • The correct pattern: PreToolUse hook blocks policy-violating calls deterministically

    ✅ Lab 1.5 Checkpoint

    • PostToolUse hook normalizes data AFTER tool execution, BEFORE model sees results
    • PreToolUse hook can BLOCK outgoing tool calls that violate policy
    • Hooks provide guaranteed compliance; prompts are probabilistic
    • Use hooks for: financial thresholds, data normalization, audit logging

    LAB 1.6: TASK DECOMPOSITION STRATEGIES

    Domain 1 — Task Statement 1.6 Prompt chaining vs dynamic decomposition ~20 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What's the difference between a hook and a gate? (Lab 1.5)
    2. When should you use programmatic enforcement vs prompt guidance? (Lab 1.5)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: You need to review code for security, performance, and style. Do you: (A) Run three fixed passes in sequence (security → performance → style), or (B) Let the agent look at the code and decide what to focus on?

    The answer depends on the task. Prompt chaining (option A) works when steps are predictable and don't depend on what previous steps find. Dynamic decomposition (option B) works when the agent needs to adapt based on what it discovers.

    Getting this wrong means either wasting tokens on unnecessary fixed steps, or getting incomplete results because the agent didn't decompose thoroughly enough.

    Exam connection (Sample Q5): "Restructure a monolith into microservices" → plan mode (adaptive decomposition). "Fix an off-by-one error" → direct execution (fixed steps). The exam tests your judgment about WHICH strategy fits WHICH situation.

    What You'll Learn

    • Prompt chaining: Fixed sequential pipeline — each step always runs regardless of input. Best for predictable multi-aspect reviews.
    • Dynamic decomposition: Agent analyzes the input first, then decides what subtasks to create. Best for complex, unpredictable problems.
    • Decision rule: Known steps → chain. Unknown complexity → decompose.

    See It In Action

    Open Claude Code and try this:

    Analyze this project for potential improvements. Check for: (1) code quality issues, (2) missing tests, and (3) documentation gaps. For each category, give me specific findings.

    Watch how Claude Code breaks this into distinct passes — one for code quality, one for tests, one for documentation. This is prompt chaining: a fixed sequence of known analysis steps. Now try a more open-ended request:

    This codebase has been getting slower over the last month. Investigate why and suggest fixes.

    Notice how Claude Code adapts its investigation based on what it discovers — maybe it finds a database query issue first, then traces it to missing indexes, then checks for N+1 queries. This is dynamic decomposition: each step generates the next steps based on findings.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d1-agentic-arch/task_decomposition.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── PATTERN 1: PROMPT CHAINING (fixed sequential pipeline) ──
# Best for: predictable multi-aspect reviews, known steps
# Example: Code review → analyze each file → cross-file integration pass

def prompt_chaining_code_review(files: dict) -> dict:
    """
    Fixed pipeline: per-file analysis → cross-file integration pass.

    WHY SPLIT: "Lost in the middle" effect — reviewing 10 files in one
    prompt causes attention dilution. Dedicated passes per file are better.
    """
    print("\n=== PROMPT CHAINING: Code Review ===")

    per_file_findings = {}

    # Step 1: Per-file local analysis
    for filename, content in files.items():
        print(f"\n  Step 1: Analyzing {filename}...")

        response = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
            max_tokens=512,
            messages=[{
                "role": "user",
                "content": f"""Analyze this file for local issues only (bugs, security, logic errors).
Do NOT comment on cross-file integration issues.
File: {filename}
Content: {content}

Return JSON: {{"findings": [{{"line": N, "issue": "...", "severity": "high/medium/low"}}]}}"""
            }]
        )

        text = response.content[0].text
        # Parse findings (simplified)
        per_file_findings[filename] = {"raw": text[:100]}
        print(f"    Found local issues in {filename}")

    # Step 2: Cross-file integration pass
    print(f"\n  Step 2: Cross-file integration pass...")

    all_findings_summary = json.dumps(per_file_findings, indent=2)

    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=512,
        messages=[{
            "role": "user",
            "content": f"""Review these per-file findings for cross-file integration issues:
- Data flow inconsistencies between files
- Interface contract violations
- Circular dependencies

Per-file findings:
{all_findings_summary}

Return JSON: {{"integration_issues": [{{"files": [...], "issue": "...", "severity": "..."}}]}}"""
        }]
    )

    print(f"    Cross-file analysis complete")
    return {"per_file": per_file_findings, "integration": response.content[0].text[:100]}


# ── PATTERN 2: DYNAMIC ADAPTIVE DECOMPOSITION ──
# Best for: open-ended investigation, unknown structure
# Example: "Add comprehensive tests to this legacy codebase"

def dynamic_decomposition_demo():
    """
    Dynamic decomposition: each step generates the next steps.

    Phase 1: Map structure → discover what's there
    Phase 2: Identify high-impact areas → based on Phase 1 findings
    Phase 3: Create prioritized plan → based on Phase 2 findings

    The plan adapts based on what's discovered at each step.
    """
    print("\n=== DYNAMIC DECOMPOSITION: Legacy Test Coverage ===")

    # Phase 1: Structure mapping
    print("\n  Phase 1: Map codebase structure...")
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=512,
        messages=[{
            "role": "user",
            "content": """I have a legacy Python codebase.
Plan Phase 1 of adding comprehensive tests: structure mapping.
What questions should I answer first, before deciding what to test?
Return 3-5 specific investigation questions as JSON array."""
        }]
    )
    print(f"  Phase 1 questions: {response.content[0].text[:150]}...")

    # Phase 2 would be based on Phase 1 answers
    print("\n  Phase 2: Would investigate based on Phase 1 findings...")
    print("  (Each phase generates the tasks for the next phase)")

    print("""
    COMPARISON:

    Prompt Chaining (fixed):
    ✓ Predictable execution
    ✓ Easy to test and validate
    ✓ Good for code reviews, document processing
    ✗ Brittle if steps don't apply to all inputs

    Dynamic Decomposition:
    ✓ Adapts to what's discovered
    ✓ Good for open-ended investigation
    ✓ Handles unknown structure
    ✗ Harder to predict and test
    ✗ Can diverge in unexpected directions

    EXAM RULE: Predictable multi-aspect reviews → chaining.
               Open-ended investigation → dynamic decomposition.
    """)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    # Small sample for chaining demo
    files = {
        "auth.py": "def verify_token(token): return token == 'secret123'",
        "api.py":  "from auth import verify_token\ndef handle_request(req): verify_token(req.token)"
    }
    prompt_chaining_code_review(files)
    dynamic_decomposition_demo()

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    Create the file lab-d1-agentic-arch/task_decomposition.py with the code above and run it

    What you should see:

    • Prompt chaining: per-file analysis followed by a cross-file integration pass, each producing focused findings
    • Dynamic decomposition: agent analyzes the codebase first, then generates targeted investigation tasks
    • Comparison table showing trade-offs: chaining = predictable/testable, dynamic = adaptive/flexible

    Code Analysis

    Exercise 1 — Why does prompt chaining fail for open-ended tasks? Consider using the fixed pipeline (prompt_chaining_code_review) for an open-ended task like "investigate why users are churning." The pipeline runs per-file analysis then cross-file integration — but what if the root cause is in the database schema, not in any code file? Fixed steps cannot adapt to what is discovered. Chaining works only when the steps are known in advance.

    Exercise 2 — Why is dynamic decomposition wasteful for simple tasks? If the task is "lint, test, and format this file," the decomposition step (Phase 1: analyze structure, Phase 2: identify priorities) adds overhead with no benefit — the three steps are already known. The extra API call to plan the work is wasted tokens and latency.

    Exercise 3 — Exam decision rule: which pattern fits which situation? "Predictable multi-aspect review" (e.g., code review for security + performance + style) → prompt chaining. "Complex restructuring with unknown dependencies" (e.g., monolith to microservices) → dynamic decomposition. The key signal is whether you know the steps before you start.

    High-Impact Area Identification (Dynamic Decomposition Deep Dive)

    The exam specifically tests this pattern: "Decomposing open-ended tasks by first mapping structure, identifying high-impact areas, then creating a prioritized plan that adapts as dependencies are discovered."

    Here's the concrete workflow for an open-ended task like "add comprehensive tests to a legacy codebase":

    Phase 1 — Map structure (don't write any tests yet)

    • List all modules/directories and their file counts
    • Identify entry points (main functions, API endpoints, CLI commands)
    • Note which modules import which others (dependency graph)

    Phase 2 — Identify high-impact areas

    • Modules with the most callers — a bug here affects everything downstream
    • Modules with zero existing tests — highest risk, highest value to test
    • Modules with most recent bug fixes (git log) — historically unreliable code
    • Modules at system boundaries (database, external APIs) — where integration bugs hide

    Phase 3 — Create a prioritized plan that adapts

    • Start with the highest-impact module from Phase 2
    • After testing it, re-evaluate — did you discover new dependencies? New risks?
    • Adjust the plan: maybe module C turned out to be more critical than module B
    • This is why dynamic decomposition beats prompt chaining — the plan evolves

    Code Analysis — Why map structure first? Starting with the largest file (or a random file) wastes effort — you might test a utility function that nothing calls, while the untested payment module processes real money. Mapping first ensures effort goes where it matters.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "predictable multi-step review" → prompt chaining (fixed pipeline)
    • If the exam says "open-ended investigation with unknown dependencies" → dynamic decomposition
    • Wrong answer trap: "Use dynamic decomposition for everything" — adds overhead to known-step tasks
    • The correct pattern: Known steps → chain; unknown structure → decompose

    ✅ Lab 1.6 Checkpoint

    • Prompt chaining: fixed pipeline, each step output feeds next step input
    • Per-file + cross-file passes prevent attention dilution in large reviews
    • Dynamic decomposition: each phase generates the next phase's tasks
    • Map structure → identify high-impact areas → prioritize → adapt as you discover
    • Choose based on: known steps → chaining; unknown structure → dynamic

    LAB 1.7: SESSION STATE, RESUMPTION, AND FORKING

    Domain 1 — Task Statement 1.7 --resume, fork_session, stale tool results ~15 minutesGuided | Claude Code (no API key needed)

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When should you use prompt chaining vs dynamic decomposition? (Lab 1.6)
    2. What is the "map structure first" pattern for open-ended tasks? (Lab 1.6)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: You're 20 minutes into a complex debugging session with Claude Code. You find the root cause but need to test two different fix approaches. If you try both in the same session, the context from approach A pollutes your work on approach B. And if Claude Code crashes mid-session, you lose everything.

    The solutions:

    • --resume picks up where you left off (but beware: tool results from hours ago may be stale)
    • fork_session creates an independent branch — test approach A and approach B without cross-contamination
    • For really long sessions, starting fresh with an injected summary beats resuming with degraded context

    Exam connection: The exam tests whether you know when to resume vs fork vs start fresh. The rule: if tool results might be stale (files changed, APIs returned different data), start fresh with a structured summary of what you found — don't blindly resume.

    See It In Action

    Start a Claude Code session and explore your lab workspace:

    claude --resume my-first-session

    Ask Claude about a file in your workspace. Then close the session, reopen with --resume my-first-session, and ask a follow-up about the same file. Notice how context persists.

    Now try this: modify a file that Claude analyzed, then resume. Ask about the changed file — does Claude reference the old version or the new one? This is the stale context problem that Lab 1.7 teaches you to handle.

    What You'll Learn

    • Named sessions: Continue work across sessions with --resume
    • fork_session: Branch from a shared baseline for parallel exploration
    • Context degradation: Why long sessions produce worse results (Claude starts saying "typically" instead of citing specific code)
    • The decision rule: Stale tool results or degraded context → start fresh with injected summary. Recent, valid context → resume.

    Step 1: Create lab-d1-agentic-arch/session_demo.py

    This script demonstrates the core session management concepts with real code. It simulates what happens when tool results become stale and why injected summaries beat blind resumption.

    import json
from datetime import datetime

# ── SIMULATED SESSION STATE ────────────────────────────────
# This represents what Claude Code tracks across sessions

class SessionState:
    """Simulates Claude Code's session management."""

    def __init__(self, name):
        self.name = name
        self.findings = []
        self.tool_results = []
        self.created_at = datetime.now().isoformat()
        self.turn_count = 0

    def add_finding(self, finding, source_file, line_number):
        """Record a finding with its source location."""
        self.findings.append({
            "finding": finding,
            "file": source_file,
            "line": line_number,
            "recorded_at": datetime.now().isoformat()
        })
        self.turn_count += 1

    def add_tool_result(self, tool_name, result):
        """Record a tool result (these can go stale)."""
        self.tool_results.append({
            "tool": tool_name,
            "result": result,
            "captured_at": datetime.now().isoformat()
        })

    def get_summary(self):
        """Extract structured summary for injecting into a fresh session."""
        return {
            "session": self.name,
            "key_findings": [f["finding"] for f in self.findings],
            "files_examined": list(set(f["file"] for f in self.findings)),
            "turn_count": self.turn_count
        }


# ── SCENARIO 1: STALE TOOL RESULTS ────────────────────────
# Demonstrates why resuming blindly after code changes is dangerous

def demo_stale_results():
    print("=== Scenario 1: Stale Tool Results ===\n")

    # Session from yesterday
    session = SessionState("debug-auth")
    session.add_finding("SQL injection in login()", "auth.py", 42)
    session.add_tool_result("Read", {"file": "auth.py", "line_42": "query = f'SELECT * FROM users WHERE id={user_id}'"})

    print("Yesterday's session found:")
    print(f"  Finding: {session.findings[0]['finding']}")
    print(f"  At: {session.findings[0]['file']}:{session.findings[0]['line']}")
    print(f"  Tool result: {session.tool_results[0]['result']['line_42']}")

    # Overnight: developer fixed the bug and refactored
    print("\nOvernight: developer fixed auth.py (line 42 is now a comment)")
    print("  New line 42: '# Authentication refactored — see auth_v2.py'")

    # PROBLEM: if you --resume, Claude still thinks line 42 has SQL injection
    print("\n❌ BLIND RESUME: Claude references 'the SQL injection on line 42 of auth.py'")
    print("   But line 42 is now a comment. The bug was fixed AND the file was restructured.")
    print("   Claude is working with STALE tool results.")

    # SOLUTION: start fresh with injected summary
    summary = session.get_summary()
    print(f"\n✓ FRESH START with injected summary:")
    print(f"  'Previous analysis found: {summary['key_findings']}")
    print(f"   Files examined: {summary['files_examined']}")
    print(f"   Code has since been refactored. Re-analyze auth.py for remaining issues.'")
    print(f"\n  → Claude re-reads the CURRENT file and gives accurate findings.")


# ── SCENARIO 2: FORK SESSION ──────────────────────────────
# Demonstrates why forking prevents cross-contamination

def demo_fork_session():
    print("\n\n=== Scenario 2: Fork Session ===\n")

    # Shared baseline
    baseline = SessionState("perf-investigation")
    baseline.add_finding("API endpoint /users is slow (800ms p95)", "api.py", 15)
    baseline.add_finding("Database query scans full table", "models.py", 88)

    print("Shared baseline findings:")
    for f in baseline.findings:
        print(f"  - {f['finding']}")

    # Fork A: Try caching approach
    fork_a = SessionState("perf-investigation-cache")
    fork_a.findings = list(baseline.findings)  # Copy baseline
    fork_a.add_finding("Redis cache reduces /users to 50ms", "cache.py", 1)
    fork_a.add_finding("But adds 200MB memory overhead", "cache.py", 45)

    # Fork B: Try index approach
    fork_b = SessionState("perf-investigation-index")
    fork_b.findings = list(baseline.findings)  # Copy baseline
    fork_b.add_finding("Database index reduces query from 800ms to 12ms", "migrations/add_index.sql", 1)
    fork_b.add_finding("No additional memory overhead", "models.py", 88)

    print("\nFork A (caching):")
    for f in fork_a.findings[2:]:
        print(f"  - {f['finding']}")

    print("\nFork B (indexing):")
    for f in fork_b.findings[2:]:
        print(f"  - {f['finding']}")

    print("\n✓ KEY: Fork A and Fork B don't see each other's findings.")
    print("  Cache exploration doesn't pollute the index exploration.")
    print("  You can compare results objectively from a shared baseline.")


# ── SCENARIO 3: CONTEXT DEGRADATION ───────────────────────
# Demonstrates why long sessions produce worse results

def demo_context_degradation():
    print("\n\n=== Scenario 3: Context Degradation ===\n")

    session = SessionState("long-exploration")

    # Simulate 50 turns of exploration
    files = [f"module_{i}.py" for i in range(25)]
    for i, f in enumerate(files):
        session.add_finding(f"Found pattern X in {f}", f, i * 10)
        session.add_tool_result("Read", {"file": f, "size": 200 + i * 50})

    print(f"Session has {session.turn_count} turns, {len(session.tool_results)} tool results")
    print(f"Estimated token usage: ~{session.turn_count * 500 + len(session.tool_results) * 200} tokens")

    print("\nSYMPTOM: Ask 'What was the first finding?'")
    print("  Turn 50: 'Based on the analysis, the codebase typically uses pattern X...'")
    print("  ← WRONG. It said 'typically' instead of citing the specific finding from turn 1.")
    print("  This is context degradation — earlier findings are lost in the middle.")

    print("\nFIXES:")
    print("  1. /compact — summarizes context, preserving key findings but freeing tokens")
    print("  2. Start fresh with injected summary — extract what you found, start new session")
    print("  3. Scratchpad files — write findings to disk so they survive context limits")


# ── DECISION GUIDE ─────────────────────────────────────────

def print_decision_guide():
    print("\n\n=== Session Strategy Decision Guide ===\n")
    print("""
    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
    │ WHEN TO...                                              │
    ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ --resume:                                               │
    │   • Prior context is still valid                        │
    │   • No code changes since last session                  │
    │   • Short break, continuing same investigation          │
    ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ fork_session:                                           │
    │   • Compare two approaches from same baseline           │
    │   • Explore divergent solutions in isolation            │
    │   • A/B test different strategies                       │
    ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
    │ Start fresh with injected summary:                      │
    │   • Files changed since last session                    │
    │   • Tool results are stale (line numbers shifted)       │
    │   • Session ran too long (context degradation)          │
    │   • EXAM RULE: This beats --resume when results         │
    │     might be stale. Always prefer fresh + summary.      │
    └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
    """)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    demo_stale_results()
    demo_fork_session()
    demo_context_degradation()
    print_decision_guide()

    • Scenario 2: Shows two forks with independent findings that don't cross-contaminate

# Inside Claude Code, explore your lab directory

# Practice /compact to handle context degradation

    Break It — test your understanding

    1. Resume a session after changing a file that was analyzed. Does Claude reference the old version? (Answer: yes — stale tool results persist in the resumed context)
    2. Run 30+ turns without /compact. Ask Claude about a finding from turn 3. Does it cite the specific finding or say "typically"? (Answer: likely "typically" — context degradation)
    3. Why not always start fresh? (Answer: you lose the full conversation context. Resume is better when context is still valid and tool results haven't gone stale)

    Session Strategy Decision Framework

    The exam tests your ability to choose between three approaches. Use this table:

    Situation Approach Why
    Minor file changes since last session, context still valid --resume session-name Prior analysis and tool results are mostly accurate
    Exploring 2 competing approaches from the same analysis fork_session Creates independent branches from a shared baseline — no cross-contamination
    Significant time passed, code has changed substantially Start fresh + inject structured summary Stale tool results cause the agent to reference old code
    Agent says "typically uses X" instead of citing specific code Start fresh + inject structured summary Progressive summarization has lost specific details
    Need to re-analyze only the changed files --resume + inform about changes Tell the agent exactly which files changed for targeted re-analysis

    The exam trap: "Resume" sounds efficient but is wrong when tool results are stale. "Start fresh" sounds wasteful but is correct when context has degraded. The key question is: are the prior tool results still accurate?

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "agent references old code after files changed" → start fresh with injected summary, NOT resume
    • Wrong answer trap: "Resume the session — it preserves full context" — yes, including stale tool results
    • Wrong answer trap: "Use a larger context window" — more context doesn't fix stale data
    • The correct pattern: Are prior tool results still accurate? Yes → resume. No → fresh start with summary.

    ✅ Lab 1.7 Checkpoint

    • --resume <session-name> continues named sessions
    • fork_session creates parallel branches from shared baseline
    • Stale tool results (after code changes) → start fresh with injected summary
    • Context degradation → use /compact or start fresh with structured summary
    • Inform resumed sessions about changed files for targeted re-analysis
    • Decision rule: Are prior tool results still accurate? Yes → resume. No → fresh start.

    Quick Check — Module 1

    Q: Your agent skips get_customer in 12% of cases and calls lookup_order directly, occasionally refunding the wrong person. What's the best fix?

    A) Add "CRITICAL: ALWAYS call get_customer first" to the system prompt B) Add few-shot examples showing correct tool ordering C) Implement a programmatic gate that blocks lookup_order until get_customer returns a verified ID D) Add a routing classifier to pre-select tools

    Answer

    C. When a tool sequence is required for financial operations, programmatic enforcement provides deterministic guarantees. Prompts (A, B) have a non-zero failure rate. A routing classifier (D) addresses tool availability, not ordering. (Lab 1.4, Exam Sample Q1)

    Q: Your multi-agent research report covers only "visual arts" when the topic was "AI in creative industries." Each subagent completed its work correctly. What's the root cause?

    A) The synthesis agent lacks gap-detection instructions B) The coordinator's task decomposition was too narrow C) The search agent needs broader queries D) The analysis agent filtered out non-visual content

    Answer

    B. The coordinator decomposed "creative industries" into only visual arts subtasks. Subagents executed correctly within their assigned scope — the problem is what they were assigned. (Lab 1.2, Exam Sample Q7)

    MODULE 2: TOOL DESIGN & MCP INTEGRATION

    Domain 2 — 18% of exam Task Statements 2.1 – 2.5

    Key Terms for Module 2

    • MCP (Model Context Protocol): A standard for connecting Claude to external tools and data sources. An MCP server wraps your tool/API and exposes it to Claude through a standardized interface.
    • Tool description: The text Claude reads to decide which tool to call. Vague descriptions → wrong tool selection. Good descriptions include: purpose, input format, output format, when to use, when NOT to use.
    • isError flag: Boolean in tool responses. true = tool failed (timeout, permission denied). false = tool succeeded (even if no results found).
    • errorCategory: Classification of what went wrong: transient (retry), validation (fix input), business (policy violation), permission (access denied).
    • isRetryable: Boolean indicating if the error is worth retrying. Only true for transient errors.
    • tool_choice: Controls whether Claude must call a tool. "auto" = Claude decides. "any" = must call a tool (any of them). {"type": "tool", "name": "X"} = must call tool X specifically.
    • Scoped tool access: Giving each agent only 4-5 tools relevant to its role, not all 18 tools in the system.
    • .mcp.json: Project-scoped MCP config file (shared via git). Uses ${ENV_VAR} for credentials.
    • ~/.claude.json: User-scoped MCP config file (personal, not shared).
    • MCP resources: Content catalogs (like issue lists, documentation hierarchies) that agents can browse without making individual tool calls.
    • Grep: Built-in tool for searching file contents (patterns, function names, error messages).
    • Glob: Built-in tool for finding files by path patterns (e.g., **/*.test.tsx).
    • Edit: Built-in tool for targeted file modifications using unique text matching. Falls back to Read + Write when text isn't unique.

    LAB 2.1: TOOL DESCRIPTION QUALITY

    Domain 2 — Task Statement 2.1 Descriptions affect tool selection reliability ~20 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When should you use --resume vs fork_session vs starting fresh? (Lab 1.7)
    2. What causes context degradation in extended sessions? (Lab 1.7)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: You give Claude two tools — get_customer ("Retrieves customer information") and lookup_order ("Retrieves order details"). A user asks "check my order #12345." Claude calls get_customer instead of lookup_order. Why? Because both descriptions say "retrieves" and both accept ID-like inputs. Claude can't tell them apart.

    This is the #1 tool reliability issue in production agents, and it's the easiest to fix. You don't need routing classifiers or few-shot examples. You just need better descriptions.

    Exam connection (Sample Q2): "What's the most effective first step to improve tool selection reliability?" → Expand tool descriptions. Not routing layers, not consolidation, not examples. Descriptions are the lowest-effort, highest-leverage fix.

    What You'll Learn

    • Why minimal descriptions ("Retrieves X") cause tool confusion
    • The 5-part description template: purpose, input format, output format, when-to-use, when-NOT-to-use
    • How tool names that share keywords cause unintended associations in system prompts

    See It In Action

    Ask Claude Code:

    "I have two tools: one called analyze_content with description 'Analyzes content and returns results' and one called analyze_document with description 'Analyzes a document and returns results.' If I ask you to process a Google search result page, which tool would you pick and why? Now imagine the tools were renamed to extract_web_results (with a description specifying web pages, HTML input, returns title/url/snippet) and extract_document_data (specifying PDFs/invoices, returns type/title/key_facts). Which would you pick now, and why is it easier to decide?"

    Notice how Claude Code itself demonstrates the problem: vague, overlapping descriptions make tool selection ambiguous, while specific descriptions with input/output contracts make it obvious.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d2-tool-design/tool_descriptions.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── BAD DESCRIPTIONS: Vague, overlapping ──────────────────
bad_tools = [
    {
        "name": "analyze_content",
        "description": "Analyzes content and returns results",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"input": {"type": "string"}},
                         "required": ["input"]}
    },
    {
        "name": "analyze_document",
        "description": "Analyzes a document and returns results",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"input": {"type": "string"}},
                         "required": ["input"]}
    }
]

# ── GOOD DESCRIPTIONS: Specific, differentiated ───────────
good_tools = [
    {
        "name": "extract_web_results",
        "description": (
            "Extracts structured data from web search result pages. "
            "Input: raw HTML or text content from a web search results page. "
            "Returns: list of {title, url, snippet, date} objects. "
            "Use for: processing search engine results, news feeds, web-scraped content. "
            "Do NOT use for: PDFs, Word documents, or non-web text."
        ),
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"html_content": {"type": "string",
                                                         "description": "Raw HTML from a web page"}},
                         "required": ["html_content"]}
    },
    {
        "name": "extract_document_data",
        "description": (
            "Extracts structured data from business documents (PDFs, Word, spreadsheets). "
            "Input: text content from a document file. "
            "Returns: {type, title, date, author, key_facts, tables}. "
            "Use for: invoices, reports, contracts, research papers. "
            "Do NOT use for: web pages, search results, chat messages."
        ),
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"document_text": {"type": "string",
                                                          "description": "Extracted text from a document file"}},
                         "required": ["document_text"]}
    }
]

def test_tool_selection(tools_list, tools_name, test_message):
    """Test which tool Claude selects for a given input."""
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=256,
        tools=tools_list,
        tool_choice="any",
        messages=[{"role": "user", "content": test_message}]
    )

    selected = None
    for block in response.content:
        if block.type == "tool_use":
            selected = block.name

    print(f"  [{tools_name}] Message: '{test_message[:50]}...'")
    print(f"  → Selected: {selected}\n")
    return selected

if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("=== Tool Selection Comparison ===\n")

    test_cases = [
        "Process this Google search result: <html>Top 10 EV articles...</html>",
        "Extract data from this invoice PDF: INVOICE #998 Widget Co...",
        "Analyze this content I scraped from the web",
    ]

    for msg in test_cases:
        bad_selection  = test_tool_selection(bad_tools,  "BAD DESCRIPTIONS", msg)
        good_selection = test_tool_selection(good_tools, "GOOD DESCRIPTIONS", msg)

        expected = "extract_web_results" if "html" in msg.lower() or "search" in msg.lower() else "extract_document_data"
        print(f"  Expected: {expected}")
        print(f"  Bad desc got it right: {bad_selection == expected if bad_selection else 'N/A'}")
        print(f"  Good desc got it right: {good_selection == expected}")
        print(f"  {'─'*50}\n")

    print("\n=== Tool Splitting Pattern ===")

    # BEFORE: One generic tool that serves multiple purposes
    generic_tool = {
        "name": "analyze_document",
        "description": "Analyzes a document and returns results.",  # Too vague!
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"content": {"type": "string"}},
                         "required": ["content"]}
    }

    # AFTER: Split into three purpose-specific tools
    split_tools = [
        {
            "name": "extract_data_points",
            "description": (
                "Extracts structured data points from a document. "
                "Returns: values, units, dates, named entities. "
                "Use for: invoices, financial reports, data tables. "
                "Do NOT use for: summarization or claim verification."
            ),
            "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                             "properties": {"content": {"type": "string"}},
                             "required": ["content"]}
        },
        {
            "name": "summarize_content",
            "description": (
                "Creates a structured summary of a document. "
                "Returns: title, abstract, key_points list. "
                "Use for: research papers, articles, reports. "
                "Do NOT use for: extracting specific data values."
            ),
            "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                             "properties": {"content": {"type": "string"}},
                             "required": ["content"]}
        },
        {
            "name": "verify_claim_against_source",
            "description": (
                "Checks whether a specific claim is supported by a source document. "
                "Returns: verified (bool), evidence excerpt, confidence. "
                "Use for: fact-checking, citation verification. "
                "Do NOT use for: general summarization or data extraction."
            ),
            "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                             "properties": {
                                 "claim": {"type": "string"},
                                 "source_content": {"type": "string"}
                             },
                             "required": ["claim", "source_content"]}
        }
    ]

    print(f"  BEFORE: 1 generic tool → 'analyze_document' (vague)")
    print(f"  AFTER:  3 specific tools →")
    for t in split_tools:
        print(f"    - {t['name']}: {t['description'][:60]}...")
    print(f"\n  Each tool has a clear input/output contract.")
    print(f"  The model selects with high confidence because there's no ambiguity.")

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    "Create the file lab-d2-tool-design/tool_descriptions.py with the code above and run it."

    What you should see:

    • For each of 3 test messages, both BAD and GOOD description tool selections are printed, showing which tool Claude picked
    • BAD descriptions produce inconsistent or wrong selections (e.g., both queries route to the same vague tool), while GOOD descriptions correctly route web content to extract_web_results and documents to extract_document_data
    • A "Tool Splitting Pattern" section showing how 1 generic analyze_document tool becomes 3 specific tools (extract_data_points, summarize_content, verify_claim_against_source), each with a clear contract

    The keyword-sensitive system prompt problem

    System prompt wording can accidentally override good tool descriptions. This is a real failure mode — and easy to miss.

    # BAD system prompt — "document" keyword creates unintended tool association
bad_system = """You are a research assistant.
When users provide a document, analyze it thoroughly."""
# Problem: Now "document" queries route to "analyze_document" even when
# the user pasted a web search result. The word "document" in the system
# prompt creates an unintended association with that tool.

# FIX: Review system prompts for keywords that match tool names
good_system = """You are a research assistant.
Analyze content the user provides. Choose the right extraction tool based on
the content source (web page vs file document), not the word used to describe it."""

# Step: Audit your system prompt for tool-name keywords
def audit_system_prompt_for_tool_keywords(system_prompt: str, tool_names: list) -> list:
    """Find system prompt words that match tool names — potential misrouting risk."""
    warnings = []
    prompt_words = system_prompt.lower().replace("_", " ").split()
    for tool in tool_names:
        tool_words = tool.lower().replace("_", " ").split()
        for word in tool_words:
            if len(word) > 4 and word in prompt_words:  # skip short words
                warnings.append(f"System prompt contains '{word}' which matches tool '{tool}'")
    return warnings

# Test it
tools_list = ["analyze_document", "extract_web_results", "process_refund"]
warnings = audit_system_prompt_for_tool_keywords(bad_system, tools_list)
for w in warnings:
    print(f"⚠️  {w}")

    Run this: any overlap between your system prompt keywords and tool names is a potential misrouting risk.

    Code Analysis

    1. Why do the minimal descriptions ("Analyzes content and returns results" / "Analyzes a document and returns results") cause misrouting? Both descriptions use near-identical language ("analyzes" + "returns results") and accept the same input type (a generic string). Without differentiation in purpose, input format, or output format, the model has no reliable signal to distinguish them. It effectively picks at random.

    2. How does keyword overlap between system prompts and tool names bias selection? If your system prompt says "When users provide a document, analyze it thoroughly" and you have a tool named analyze_document, the repeated "document" keyword creates an unintended association. Queries that mention any kind of content may route to that tool, even for web search results that should go elsewhere.

    3. Is consolidating two tools into one always better than improving descriptions? No. Consolidation can work but is higher effort than just improving descriptions. The exam's correct answer for "most effective first step" is expanding tool descriptions — it is the lowest-effort, highest-leverage fix.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "agent calls wrong tool" or "tools have similar descriptions" → expand descriptions
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add a routing classifier" — over-engineered for a description problem
    • Wrong answer trap: "Consolidate tools into one" — valid but not the "first step" the question asks for
    • The correct pattern: Tool descriptions are the primary selection mechanism — improve them first

    ✅ Lab 2.1 Checkpoint

    • Vague/overlapping descriptions → unreliable tool selection
    • Good descriptions: purpose, input format, output format, when-to-use, when-NOT-to-use
    • Overlapping tools → rename to eliminate functional overlap
    • Generic tools → split into purpose-specific tools with defined contracts
    • Audit system prompts for keywords that match tool names → remove unintended associations

    LAB 2.2: STRUCTURED ERROR RESPONSES

    Domain 2 — Task Statement 2.2 Error categories, isRetryable, access failure vs empty result ~15 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What is the #1 first step to fix tool selection reliability? (Lab 2.1)
    2. Why is a routing classifier usually the wrong answer for tool selection problems? (Lab 2.1)

    Why This Matters

    Your agent calls a database tool that times out. It gets back {"error": "Operation failed"}. Should it retry? Is the data gone? Was the input wrong? A generic error message gives no clue — the agent either retries blindly or gives up. The fix: structured error responses with categories (transient/validation/business/permission) and isRetryable flags that tell the agent exactly what went wrong and what to do next.

    What You'll Learn

    4 error categories (transient, validation, business, permission) with isRetryable flags. The critical distinction: isError:true (access failure) vs isError:false with null results (valid empty). How subagents implement local error recovery before propagating to the coordinator.

    See It In Action

    Ask Claude Code:

    "Imagine you call a tool and get back {\"error\": \"Operation failed\"}. Should you retry? Is the data gone? Was the input wrong? Now imagine you get back {\"isError\": true, \"errorCategory\": \"transient\", \"isRetryable\": true, \"message\": \"Database timed out after 5 seconds\"}. How does the structured version change your decision? What would you do differently for a validation error vs a transient error vs a permission error?"

    Notice how Claude Code explains the difference: generic errors leave the agent guessing, while structured errors with categories and isRetryable flags enable intelligent recovery decisions.

    Challenge Mode (optional — try before looking at the solution)

    Design error response functions for these 4 categories without looking ahead:

    1. Transient (timeout, rate limit) — what fields? Is it retryable?
    2. Validation (bad input from the model) — what fields? Retryable?
    3. Business (policy violation, e.g., refund over limit) — what fields? What does the customer see?
    4. Permission (unauthorized access) — what fields?
    5. Empty result — is this an error? What does isError equal?

    Write your helper functions, then compare to the reference below.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d2-tool-design/structured_errors.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

def make_transient_error(operation: str) -> dict:
    """Timeout/unavailability — the service might work if you try again."""
    return {
        "isError": True,
        "errorCategory": "transient",
        "isRetryable": True,
        "message": f"{operation} timed out after 5 seconds",
        "attempted_query": operation,
        "suggestion": "Retry in a few seconds; this is a temporary service issue"
    }

def make_validation_error(field: str, issue: str) -> dict:
    """Invalid input — retrying with same input won't help."""
    return {
        "isError": True,
        "errorCategory": "validation",
        "isRetryable": False,
        "message": f"Invalid {field}: {issue}",
        "fix_hint": f"Correct the {field} before retrying"
    }

def make_business_error(message: str, customer_message: str) -> dict:
    """Policy violation — not a system error, a business rule."""
    return {
        "isError": True,
        "errorCategory": "business",
        "isRetryable": False,
        "message": message,
        "customer_friendly_message": customer_message,
        "results_may_exist": False
    }

def make_permission_error(resource: str) -> dict:
    """Access denied — the agent doesn't have rights to this resource."""
    return {
        "isError": True,
        "errorCategory": "permission",
        "isRetryable": False,
        "message": f"Access denied to {resource}",
        "escalation_required": True
    }

def make_empty_result(query: str) -> dict:
    """
    IMPORTANT: This is NOT an error. The search worked; there's just nothing there.
    Using isError: false tells Claude "the operation succeeded, with no results."
    """
    return {
        "isError": False,
        "results": None,
        "message": f"Search for '{query}' completed successfully. No matching records found.",
        "query_executed": query
    }

def simulate_tool_call(email: str) -> dict:
    """Simulates a customer lookup with various failure modes."""
    if email == "timeout@test.com":
        return make_transient_error("get_customer")
    if email == "bad-email":
        return make_validation_error("email", "not a valid email address format")
    if email == "locked@test.com":
        return make_business_error(
            "Account suspended due to policy violation",
            "Your account is temporarily unavailable. Please contact support."
        )
    if email == "restricted@test.com":
        return make_permission_error("restricted account tier")
    if email == "nobody@test.com":
        return make_empty_result(email)

    return {"id": "CUST-001", "name": "Jane Smith"}  # Success

tools = [{
    "name": "get_customer",
    "description": "Gets customer by email. Returns customer data or structured error/empty result.",
    "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                     "properties": {"email": {"type": "string"}},
                     "required": ["email"]}
}]

def run_error_test(email: str):
    """Show how Claude responds to each error type."""
    print(f"\n{'─'*50}")
    print(f"Testing: {email}")

    # Intercept the tool call to inject our error
    result = simulate_tool_call(email)
    print(f"Tool returns: {json.dumps(result)[:100]}")

    # Now show Claude's interpretation
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=256,
        messages=[
            {"role": "user", "content": f"Look up customer {email}"},
            {"role": "assistant", "content": [{"type": "tool_use", "id": "tu_001",
                                               "name": "get_customer",
                                               "input": {"email": email}}]},
            {"role": "user", "content": [{"type": "tool_result", "tool_use_id": "tu_001",
                                          "content": json.dumps(result)}]}
        ]
    )

    text = "".join(b.text for b in response.content if hasattr(b, "text"))
    print(f"Claude says: {text[:150]}")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("=== Error Category Demonstration ===")
    test_emails = [
        "timeout@test.com",    # transient → Claude may suggest retry
        "bad-email",           # validation → Claude explains input issue
        "locked@test.com",     # business → Claude gives customer-friendly message
        "restricted@test.com", # permission → Claude explains can't access
        "nobody@test.com",     # empty result (NOT error) → Claude says not found
    ]
    for email in test_emails:
        run_error_test(email)

    print("\n\n=== KEY DISTINCTION ===")
    print("""
    Access failure (isError: true):
      Tool threw an exception, timed out, or was blocked.
      The query did NOT complete successfully.
      Claude should: retry (transient), fix input (validation), or escalate (permission/business)

    Valid empty result (isError: false, results: null):
      The tool worked perfectly. There just isn't any matching data.
      Claude should: report "not found" to user, NOT say "an error occurred"

    This distinction prevents Claude from saying "There was an error looking you up"
    when it should say "No account found with that email address."
    """)

    # === Local error recovery within a subagent ===
    print("\n=== Subagent Local Error Recovery Pattern ===")

    def subagent_with_local_recovery(query: str, max_local_retries: int = 2) -> dict:
        """
        Subagent attempts local recovery for transient errors.
        Only propagates to coordinator what it CANNOT resolve locally.
        Returns partial results + error metadata if recovery fails.
        """
        partial_results = []
        attempts = 0

        for attempt in range(max_local_retries + 1):
            attempts += 1
            # Simulate a tool call that may fail transiently
            result = simulate_tool_call("timeout@test.com")  # simulates transient

            if result.get("isError") and result.get("isRetryable"):
                print(f"    Attempt {attempts}: Transient error — retrying locally...")
                continue  # Local retry — don't bother coordinator
            elif result.get("isError") and not result.get("isRetryable"):
                # Non-retryable: propagate to coordinator immediately
                return {
                    "status": "failed",
                    "error": result,
                    "partial_results": partial_results,
                    "attempts": attempts,
                    "message": "Non-retryable error — coordinator must decide"
                }
            else:
                # Success
                partial_results.append(result)
                return {"status": "success", "results": partial_results, "attempts": attempts}

        # All retries exhausted — propagate with partial results
        return {
            "status": "partial_failure",
            "error": {"errorCategory": "transient", "message": "All retries exhausted"},
            "partial_results": partial_results,
            "attempts": attempts,
            "message": "Transient error persisted after local retries — coordinator should try alternative approach"
        }

    recovery_result = subagent_with_local_recovery("search for EV market data")
    print(f"  Recovery result: {json.dumps(recovery_result, indent=2)}")
    print("""
    Key pattern:
    1. Subagent retries LOCALLY for transient errors (don't burden coordinator)
    2. Non-retryable errors → propagate IMMEDIATELY with error + partial results
    3. Exhausted retries → propagate with what was attempted + partial results
    4. Coordinator gets enough context to decide: retry differently, skip, or fail gracefully
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    "Create the file lab-d2-tool-design/structured_errors.py with the code above and run it."

    What you should see:

    • timeout@test.com: Claude suggests retrying or waiting (transient error)
    • bad-email: Claude explains the input was invalid (validation error)
    • locked@test.com: Claude gives a customer-friendly message (business error)
    • restricted@test.com: Claude explains it can't access the resource (permission error)
    • nobody@test.com: Claude says "no customer found" — NOT "an error occurred" (valid empty result)

    Exam connection (Sample Q8): When a subagent times out, return structured error context — not generic "search unavailable."

    Code Analysis

    1. Why does replacing make_transient_error with a generic {"error": "Operation failed"} break intelligent recovery?
