Without the errorCategory and isRetryable fields, the agent cannot distinguish between a temporary timeout (retry), invalid input (fix and resend), a policy violation (show customer-friendly message), or a permissions issue (escalate). It gives the same vague response for all failures.

    2. What happens if make_empty_result returns {"isError": False, "results": []} with no message? Claude silently moves on without telling the user "no results found." The critical distinction is that isError: false with results: null and a clear message tells Claude "the operation succeeded, there is just nothing there" — so it can report "no customer found" instead of either erroring or silently dropping the result.

    3. Why should subagents retry transient errors locally instead of propagating every error to the coordinator? The coordinator gets flooded with recoverable errors that the subagent could have handled itself. The correct pattern: subagents retry locally for transient (retryable) errors, propagate immediately for non-retryable errors, and propagate with partial results when all local retries are exhausted.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "coordinator can't decide whether to retry" → structured error context, NOT generic errors
    • Wrong answer trap: "Automatic retry with backoff" — eventual generic status still hides context
    • Wrong answer trap: "Return empty result as success" — silent suppression is the worst anti-pattern
    • The correct pattern: Structured error with failure_type, attempted_query, partial_results, is_retryable

    ✅ Lab 2.2 Checkpoint

    • 4 error categories: transient (retry), validation (fix input), business (policy), permission (escalate)
    • isRetryable: true only for transient errors
    • isError: false = success with no results (NOT an error)
    • Structured errors enable intelligent agent recovery decisions
    • Generic "Operation failed" errors prevent smart agent recovery

    LAB 2.3: TOOL CHOICE AND TOOL DISTRIBUTION

    Domain 2 — Task Statement 2.3 tool_choice options, scoped tool access, constrained tool replacement ~15 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. Name the 4 error categories for structured error responses. (Lab 2.2)
    2. What's the difference between an access failure and a valid empty result? (Lab 2.2)

    Why This Matters

    Your extraction pipeline needs guaranteed structured output — but tool_choice='auto' sometimes returns text instead of calling the tool. Your parser crashes. The fix: tool_choice='any' forces a tool call, and forced selection ({"type": "tool", "name": "X"}) guarantees a SPECIFIC tool runs. No more hoping the model cooperates — you control when tools are mandatory.

    What You'll Learn

    tool_choice options: "auto" (model decides), "any" (must call a tool), forced (must call specific tool). Why giving agents 18 tools degrades reliability — scope to 4-5 per role. How to replace generic tools with constrained alternatives.

    See It In Action

    Ask Claude Code:

    "When I use you in Claude Code, you have access to tools like Bash, Read, Edit, Grep, and Glob. How do you decide which tool to use for a given request? If I gave you 20 additional MCP tools all with vague descriptions, would your tool selection get better or worse? Why does scoping tools to 4-5 per agent role improve reliability?"

    Observe how Claude Code explains its own tool selection process. This directly illustrates why scoped tool access (giving each agent only tools relevant to its role) outperforms giving every agent access to every tool.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d2-tool-design/tool_choice.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

extraction_tools = [
    {
        "name": "extract_metadata",
        "description": "Extracts document metadata (title, date, author, type). Always call this first before any enrichment.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"document": {"type": "string"}},
                         "required": ["document"]}
    },
    {
        "name": "extract_financials",
        "description": "Extracts financial data (amounts, totals, line items) from invoices or reports.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"document": {"type": "string"}},
                         "required": ["document"]}
    }
]

doc = "INVOICE #998 from Widget Co, dated June 1 2024. Items: Widget A $100, Widget B $200. Total: $300."

def demo_tool_choice():
    print("=== tool_choice: auto ===")
    r = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=256,
        tools=extraction_tools,
        tool_choice={"type": "auto"},  # May return text OR call a tool
        messages=[{"role": "user", "content": f"What do you think about this doc? {doc}"}]
    )
    print(f"stop_reason: {r.stop_reason}")
    for b in r.content:
        if b.type == "tool_use": print(f"Tool called: {b.name}")
        if hasattr(b, "text"): print(f"Text returned: {b.text[:80]}")

    print("\n=== tool_choice: any ===")
    r = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=256,
        tools=extraction_tools,
        tool_choice="any",  # MUST call a tool, can choose which
        messages=[{"role": "user", "content": f"Process this document: {doc}"}]
    )
    print(f"stop_reason: {r.stop_reason}")
    for b in r.content:
        if b.type == "tool_use": print(f"Tool called: {b.name}")

    print("\n=== tool_choice: forced (extract_metadata first) ===")
    r = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=256,
        tools=extraction_tools,
        tool_choice={"type": "tool", "name": "extract_metadata"},  # MUST call this specific tool
        messages=[{"role": "user", "content": f"Extract everything from: {doc}"}]
    )
    print(f"stop_reason: {r.stop_reason}")
    for b in r.content:
        if b.type == "tool_use": print(f"Tool called: {b.name} (forced)")

def demo_scoped_tool_access():
    print("\n\n=== Scoped Tool Access ===")
    print("""
    ANTI-PATTERN: Give all agents access to all 18 tools
    Problem: Model decision complexity increases, wrong tool selections multiply

    CORRECT PATTERN: Scope tools to agent role

    Search Agent:
      allowed_tools: ["web_search", "fetch_url", "extract_web_results"]
      NOT: document analysis tools, synthesis tools

    Analysis Agent:
      allowed_tools: ["read_document", "extract_data_points", "summarize_content"]
      NOT: web search tools

    Synthesis Agent:
      allowed_tools: ["verify_fact"]  ← scoped cross-role tool
      NOT: web_search (synthesis doesn't search independently)

    Coordinator Agent:
      allowed_tools: ["Task"]  ← spawning only
      (delegates all actual work to subagents)

    RESULT: Each agent makes correct tool selections because
            every tool in its list is relevant to its role.
    """)

def demo_constrained_tool_replacement():
    """Replace generic tools with constrained alternatives to improve reliability."""
    print("\n\n=== Constrained Tool Replacement ===")

    # BEFORE: Generic fetch_url — can be misused to fetch anything
    generic_tool = {
        "name": "fetch_url",
        "description": "Fetches any URL and returns its content.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {"url": {"type": "string"}},
                         "required": ["url"]}
    }

    # AFTER: Constrained load_document — validates input, scoped to purpose
    constrained_tool = {
        "name": "load_document",
        "description": (
            "Loads a document from an approved document URL. "
            "Input: url (must be from docs.internal.com or arxiv.org). "
            "Returns: document title, text content, page count. "
            "Do NOT use for: web pages, APIs, or non-document URLs. "
            "If the URL is not from an approved domain, returns a validation error."
        ),
        "input_schema": {
            "type": "object",
            "properties": {
                "url": {"type": "string",
                        "description": "URL from docs.internal.com or arxiv.org only"}
            },
            "required": ["url"]
        }
    }

    print(f"  BEFORE: fetch_url — any URL, no validation, easily misused")
    print(f"  AFTER:  load_document — approved domains only, validates input")
    print(f"")
    print(f"  Benefits of constrained replacement:")
    print(f"  1. Model knows exactly what URLs are valid (approved domains)")
    print(f"  2. Description says when NOT to use it → reduces misuse")
    print(f"  3. Server-side validation rejects bad URLs before they execute")
    print(f"  4. Synthesis agent can't accidentally use it for web scraping")

    # Scoped cross-role tool example
    print(f"\n  Scoped cross-role tool example:")
    print(f"  Synthesis Agent gets: ['verify_fact'] — just simple lookups")
    print(f"  NOT: ['web_search', 'fetch_url', 'verify_fact'] — too many options")
    print(f"  Complex verification → route through coordinator to Search Agent")


if __name__ == "__main__":
    demo_tool_choice()
    demo_scoped_tool_access()
    demo_constrained_tool_replacement()

    print("\n=== tool_choice Reference ===")
    print("""
    "auto"                           → model decides: text OR tool
    "any"                            → model MUST call a tool (any of them)
    {"type": "tool", "name": "X"}    → model MUST call tool X specifically

    When to use each:
    auto:   Normal conversational agents; let model decide
    any:    When you need structured output but don't care which schema
            (e.g., unknown document type — let model pick invoice vs contract schema)
    forced: When a specific tool MUST run first before other steps
            (e.g., force extract_metadata before any enrichment)
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    "Create the file lab-d2-tool-design/tool_choice.py with the code above and run it."

    What you should see:

    • tool_choice: "auto" → Claude may return text OR call a tool (stop_reason varies)
    • tool_choice: "any" → Claude ALWAYS calls a tool (stop_reason always = tool_use)
    • tool_choice: forced → Claude calls the SPECIFIC named tool (extract_metadata)
    • Scoped access printout shows 4 agent roles, each with 3-4 tools max
    • Constrained replacement shows fetch_url (generic) → load_document (scoped to approved domains)

    Code Analysis

    1. Why does giving one agent all 18 tools degrade selection accuracy compared to scoping to 4-5? More tools means more ambiguity in selection. With 18 tools, many will have overlapping descriptions or purposes, and the model must evaluate more candidates. Scoping to 4-5 per role ensures every tool in the list is relevant, reducing confusion and improving selection accuracy.

    2. What goes wrong if you use tool_choice: "any" when you want text output? Claude is forced to call a tool even when a text response would be more appropriate. The "any" setting guarantees a tool call — use it only when you need structured output, not for conversational responses.

    3. Why does tool_choice: "auto" fail when you NEED structured data? With "auto", Claude may return text instead of calling a tool. If your downstream parser expects structured output from a tool call, a text response will break it. Use "any" (must call some tool) or forced {"type": "tool", "name": "X"} (must call a specific tool) when structured output is required.

    Exam connection (Sample Q9): Give the synthesis agent a scoped verify_fact tool for simple lookups — principle of least privilege.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "agent has 18 tools and misuses them" → scope tools to agent role (4-5 per agent)
    • Wrong answer trap: "Improve all 18 tool descriptions" — too many tools is the root cause
    • Wrong answer trap: "Give full tool access for flexibility" — violates principle of least privilege
    • The correct pattern: Each agent gets only tools for its role + scoped cross-role tools for frequent needs

    ✅ Lab 2.3 Checkpoint

    • auto: model may return text or call a tool
    • any: model MUST call a tool (guarantees structured output)
    • forced: model MUST call this specific named tool
    • Too many tools → use scoped tool access (4-5 per agent, not 18)
    • Each agent gets only tools relevant to its role
    • Replace generic tools with constrained alternatives scoped to purpose

    LAB 2.4: MCP SERVER CONFIGURATION

    Domain 2 — Task Statement 2.4 Project vs user scope, environment variables, resources ~15 minutesGuided | Claude Code (no API key needed)

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. How many tools should a single agent ideally have access to? (Lab 2.3)
    2. What is tool_choice "any" vs "auto" vs forced selection? (Lab 2.3)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your team uses a shared Postgres database and a private GitHub repo. You want Claude Code to query both. But if you hardcode the database credentials in a config file, they end up in git. And if each developer configures their own MCP setup, new team members can't use Claude Code until someone explains the setup.

    The solution: Two scopes of configuration:

    • .mcp.json (project root) — shared via git, every team member gets it automatically
    • ~/.claude.json (home dir) — personal servers (experimental tools, personal APIs), not shared

    Both support ${ENV_VAR} expansion for credentials — the config file says which env var to read, not the actual secret. Secrets stay in each developer's environment, never in git.

    Exam connection: The exam tests whether you know which scope to use for team-shared tools (project) vs personal tools (user), and that ${ENV_VAR} is the credential pattern.

    What You'll Learn

    • How to configure MCP servers in .mcp.json (project) and ~/.claude.json (user)
    • Why ${ENV_VAR} expansion matters for team security
    • How MCP resources expose content catalogs (reducing exploratory tool calls)
    • How to verify which MCP servers are loaded

    See It In Action

    In Claude Code, run:

    /mcp

    This shows all configured MCP servers and their available tools. If you have no servers configured yet, you'll see only the built-in tools. After this lab, you'll have project and personal MCP servers configured and visible here.

    How MCP Works

    MCP (Model Context Protocol) is a standard for connecting Claude to external tools. An MCP server is a small program that wraps your tool/API and exposes it to Claude.

    Example: The GitHub MCP server wraps GitHub's API. Once configured, Claude can read issues, create PRs, and search repositories — all via tools.

    You configure MCP servers in JSON files. At startup, Claude Code discovers all configured servers and makes their tools available alongside built-in tools.

    Two types of MCP capabilities:

    • Tools: Actions Claude can take (search_issues, create_pr, run_query)
    • Resources: Content catalogs Claude can browse (issue lists, documentation hierarchies, database schemas) — reduces exploratory tool calls

    Step 1: Create the project MCP config

    In your Claude Code project folder, create .mcp.json:

    {
  "mcpServers": {
    "github": {
      "command": "npx",
      "args": ["-y", "@modelcontextprotocol/server-github"],
      "env": {
        "GITHUB_TOKEN": "${GITHUB_TOKEN}"
      }
    },
    "postgres": {
      "command": "npx",
      "args": ["-y", "@modelcontextprotocol/server-postgres", "${DATABASE_URL}"],
      "env": {
        "DATABASE_URL": "${DATABASE_URL}"
      }
    }
  }
}

    This is project-scoped — committed to version control, shared with all teammates. Note: ${GITHUB_TOKEN} expands from your environment — the secret is never in the file.

    Step 2: User-level config for personal/experimental servers

    Edit ~/.claude.json (or ~/.config/claude/claude.json) to add personal servers:

    {
  "mcpServers": {
    "my-experimental-tool": {
      "command": "python",
      "args": ["/home/me/tools/my_mcp_server.py"]
    }
  }
}

    This is user-scoped — not shared with teammates via version control. Use for: personal tools, experimental integrations, work-in-progress servers.

    Step 3: Understand the scoping decision

    Create lab-d2-tool-design/mcp_config_guide.md:

    # MCP Server Scoping Guide

## Use .mcp.json (project-scoped) when:
- The tool is needed by all team members
- It integrates with shared systems (GitHub, Jira, production database)
- You want it version-controlled alongside the code
- Example: GitHub integration, database schema tool, CI/CD tool

## Use ~/.claude.json (user-scoped) when:
- Personal/experimental integration not ready to share
- User-specific credentials that differ per developer
- Testing a custom MCP server before team rollout
- Example: personal Notion integration, dev-machine-specific tools

## Environment Variables for Credentials:
CORRECT: "GITHUB_TOKEN": "${GITHUB_TOKEN}"
WRONG:   "GITHUB_TOKEN": "ghp_abc123..."  ← never commit real tokens

## MCP Resources vs Tools:
Tools: execute actions (search, fetch, write)
Resources: expose content catalogs (list of issues, documentation index, schema)

Example resource: expose a list of open GitHub issues
→ Agent can browse the catalog WITHOUT making individual search tool calls
→ Reduces exploratory tool calls when agent needs to know "what's available"

## Tool Descriptions Matter for MCP:
If your MCP tool description is weak, Claude will prefer
built-in tools (like Grep) over your more capable MCP tool.
Write descriptions that explain WHY the MCP tool is better for certain tasks.

Example:
WEAK: "Searches the codebase"
STRONG: "Searches codebase with semantic understanding of code structure.
         Returns: file path, line number, function context, and related imports.
         Better than text search for finding all places a pattern is used."

    Step 4: Test your MCP config

    # In Claude Code, check which MCP tools are available:
> /mcp

# Or check the memory/context:
> What tools are available to you?

    Step 5: Verify your configs (runs without Claude Code)

    Create lab-d2-tool-design/verify_mcp_config.py:

    import json
import os
import re

def validate_mcp_config(path, scope_name):
    """Validate an MCP config file for correctness."""
    print(f"\n=== Validating {scope_name}: {path} ===")

    if not os.path.exists(path):
        print(f"  ⚠ File not found — create it first (see steps above)")
        return False

    with open(path) as f:
        try:
            config = json.load(f)
        except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
            print(f"  ✗ Invalid JSON: {e}")
            return False

    print(f"  ✓ Valid JSON")

    # Check for mcpServers key
    servers = config.get("mcpServers", {})
    if not servers:
        print(f"  ⚠ No 'mcpServers' found — add server definitions")
        return False

    print(f"  ✓ Found {len(servers)} server(s): {', '.join(servers.keys())}")

    # Check each server
    for name, server in servers.items():
        issues = []

        if "command" not in server:
            issues.append("missing 'command' field")

        # Check for hardcoded secrets (common mistake)
        config_str = json.dumps(server)
        if re.search(r'(ghp_|sk-|token_)[a-zA-Z0-9]{20,}', config_str):
            issues.append("⚠ HARDCODED SECRET DETECTED — use ${ENV_VAR} instead!")

        # Check for env var expansion pattern
        env_vars = re.findall(r'\$\{(\w+)\}', config_str)
        if env_vars:
            for var in env_vars:
                if os.environ.get(var):
                    print(f"  ✓ Server '{name}': ${{{var}}} is set in environment")
                else:
                    print(f"  ⚠ Server '{name}': ${{{var}}} is NOT set — export it before starting Claude Code")
        else:
            if "env" not in server:
                print(f"  ℹ Server '{name}': No env vars used (OK if no credentials needed)")

        if issues:
            for issue in issues:
                print(f"  ✗ Server '{name}': {issue}")
        else:
            print(f"  ✓ Server '{name}': config looks good")

    return True


if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("MCP Configuration Validator")
    print("=" * 40)

    # Validate project-scoped config
    validate_mcp_config(".mcp.json", "Project-scoped")

    # Validate user-scoped config
    home = os.path.expanduser("~")
    user_config = os.path.join(home, ".claude.json")
    validate_mcp_config(user_config, "User-scoped")

    print("\n\n=== MCP Scoping Rules ===")
    print("""
    .mcp.json (project root)     → Shared via git. All teammates get these servers.
    ~/.claude.json (home dir)    → Personal only. Not in git. Your experiments.

    BOTH load at startup. Tools from ALL servers are available simultaneously.

    NEVER hardcode secrets. Use ${ENV_VAR} expansion:
      "args": ["--token", "${GITHUB_TOKEN}"]
    Then: export GITHUB_TOKEN="ghp_xxx" in your shell profile.
    """)

    python lab-d2-tool-design/verify_mcp_config.py

    What you should see:

    • Validation of .mcp.json (or "not found" if you haven't created it yet)
    • Validation of ~/.claude.json (or "not found")
    • Warning if any hardcoded secrets detected
    • Status of each env var (set or not set)
    • Scoping rules summary

    Break It

    Try these misconfigurations and observe the consequences:

    1. Hardcode a token in .mcp.json — put a real "GITHUB_TOKEN": "ghp_abc123..." value directly in the file. Now run git diff — your secret is in version control, visible to every teammate and anyone who clones the repo. Use ${GITHUB_TOKEN} expansion instead.
    2. Put a personal server in .mcp.json instead of ~/.claude.json — add your experimental my-local-tool to the project config. Every teammate now gets a broken server they can't run. Personal/experimental servers belong in ~/.claude.json.
    3. Forget to set the env var — remove GITHUB_TOKEN from your environment and start Claude Code. The MCP server fails silently at startup — the tool simply doesn't appear. Run /mcp to confirm it's missing, then export GITHUB_TOKEN="..." and restart.

    MCP Resources — Content Catalogs That Reduce Tool Calls

    The exam tests MCP resources as distinct from MCP tools. Resources expose data catalogs so the agent knows what's available without making exploratory tool calls.

    Example: Instead of the agent calling list_endpoints(), list_tables(), list_docs() to discover what's available, expose a resource:

    {
  "resources": [
    {
      "uri": "docs://api/endpoints",
      "name": "API Endpoint Catalog",
      "description": "All REST endpoints with methods, auth, and rate limits"
    },
    {
      "uri": "docs://database/schema",
      "name": "Database Schema",
      "description": "All tables, columns, types, and relationships"
    }
  ]
}

    The agent sees the catalog immediately and calls specific tools only when it needs to act. This saves token budget and reduces tool call round-trips.

    Key distinction: Resources = read-only content discovery. Tools = actions that change state.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "teammate doesn't see my Claude instructions" → check if instructions are in ~/.claude/ (personal) vs project-level
    • Wrong answer trap: "The MCP server isn't configured" — this is a CLAUDE.md hierarchy issue
    • The correct pattern: .mcp.json (project, shared) vs ~/.claude.json (personal, not shared). Use ${ENV_VAR} for secrets.

    ✅ Lab 2.4 Checkpoint

    • .mcp.json = project-scoped, version-controlled, shared with team
    • ~/.claude.json = user-scoped, personal, not shared
    • Use ${ENV_VAR} for credentials — never commit actual tokens
    • MCP resources expose content catalogs to reduce exploratory tool calls
    • Resources = read-only content discovery; Tools = actions
    • Strong MCP tool descriptions prevent Claude from preferring built-in tools

    LAB 2.5: BUILT-IN TOOLS — READ, WRITE, GREP, GLOB, BASH

    Domain 2 — Task Statement 2.5 Right tool for each operation ~20 minutesGuided | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What's the difference between .mcp.json and ~/.claude.json? (Lab 2.4)
    2. How do you handle credentials in MCP config without committing secrets? (Lab 2.4)

    Why This Matters

    The key mental model: Claude Code has 6 built-in tools, and each does ONE thing well:

    • Grep = search file contents ("find all functions that call db.execute")
    • Glob = search file paths ("find all *.test.tsx files")
    • Read = view a specific file's contents
    • Write = create or completely replace a file
    • Edit = surgically modify part of a file (find-and-replace)
    • Bash = run shell commands (install packages, run tests, git operations)

    Using the wrong tool wastes context and produces bad results. Grep for a filename? Fails. Bash for reading a file? Works but doesn't show Claude the content properly.

    Exam connection: The exam tests whether you know which tool to reach for in each situation. The pattern: content search → Grep, path search → Glob, read file → Read, modify file → Edit.

    See It In Action

    Watch Claude Code select the right tool automatically. Try these three prompts in sequence:

    Find all Python test files in this project

    (Claude uses Glob — file path pattern matching)

    Find everywhere that "stop_reason" is referenced in the code

    (Claude uses Grep — content search across files)

    Show me the contents of lab-d1-agentic-arch/basic_loop.py

    (Claude uses Read — viewing a specific file)

    Notice how Claude picks a different built-in tool for each task without being told which to use. The tool descriptions guide the selection — the same principle Lab 2.1 taught about custom tools.

    Step 1: Practice the right tool for each task

    Open Claude Code and run these in order. Notice which tool Claude selects.

    Content search (use Grep, not Bash grep):

    > Find all places where "process_refund" is called in the lab code

    Claude should use Grep. Watch: it returns file paths + line numbers + context.

    > Search for all Python files that import "anthropic"

    Again Grep — searches content across files.

    File path search (use Glob, not find):

    > What .py files exist in the lab-d1-agentic-arch folder?

    Claude uses Glob with pattern lab-d1-agentic-arch/**/*.py. Returns file paths sorted by modification time.

    > Find all test files in the project

    Glob with **/*.test.* or **/*_test.*.

    File reading and modification:

    > Read the basic_loop.py file and summarize what it does

    Claude uses Read — gets full file with line numbers.

    > In basic_loop.py, change the model name to claude-sonnet-4-6

    Claude uses Edit with unique text matching. If the text isn't unique, it falls back to Read + Write.

    Incremental codebase exploration:

    > How does the run_agent function work across all the lab files?

    Watch Claude:

    1. Grep for def run_agent → finds locations
    2. Read specific files → traces the implementation
    3. Does NOT read all files upfront

    This is the correct pattern: Grep to find entry points → Read to trace flows.

    Step 2: Understand Edit vs Read+Write

    Create a test file:

    echo "x = 1
x = 1
x = 2" > /tmp/test_edit.py

    > In /tmp/test_edit.py, change x = 1 on line 2 to x = 99

    Edit will fail because x = 1 appears twice — not unique. Claude should fall back to Read + Write with the full file.

    Step 3: Create the tool selection reference

    Create lab-d2-tool-design/builtin_tools_guide.md:

    # Built-in Tool Selection Guide

## Grep — Content search
Use for: finding function definitions, error messages, import statements,
         pattern matching across file contents
Example: "Find all callers of verify_token"
→ Grep pattern: "verify_token"

## Glob — File path matching
Use for: finding files by name/extension, listing files in directories
Example: "Find all test files"
→ Glob pattern: "**/*.test.tsx" or "**/*_test.py"

## Read — Full file contents
Use for: reading a complete file to understand its structure
         loading file content for analysis or processing

## Write — Full file creation/overwrite
Use for: creating new files, replacing entire file content
         fallback when Edit can't find unique anchor text

## Edit — Targeted file modification
Use for: changing specific text within a file
Requires: unique text match (only appears once in the file)
Fallback: if not unique → Read + Write the whole file

## Bash — System commands
Use for: running programs, git commands, npm/pip, anything that
         needs actual shell execution
NOT for: file search (use Glob), content search (use Grep),
         reading files (use Read)

## Incremental exploration pattern:
1. Grep to find entry points (don't read all files upfront)
2. Read specific files to trace flows
3. Grep again to follow imports/references
4. Build understanding incrementally

    Step 4: Test your tool selection knowledge (runs without Claude Code)

    Create lab-d2-tool-design/tool_selection_quiz.py:

    import random

SCENARIOS = [
    {"task": "Find all functions that call db.execute()",
     "correct": "Grep", "why": "Searching file CONTENTS for a pattern"},
    {"task": "Find all .test.tsx files in the project",
     "correct": "Glob", "why": "Searching file PATHS by extension pattern"},
    {"task": "View the contents of config.py",
     "correct": "Read", "why": "Loading a specific file's content"},
    {"task": "Change 'localhost' to '0.0.0.0' in server.py (appears once)",
     "correct": "Edit", "why": "Targeted modification with unique text match"},
    {"task": "Run the test suite with pytest",
     "correct": "Bash", "why": "Executing a shell command"},
    {"task": "Find all files named 'CLAUDE.md' in any directory",
     "correct": "Glob", "why": "Searching for files by name pattern"},
    {"task": "Find where the error 'ConnectionTimeout' is raised",
     "correct": "Grep", "why": "Searching file contents for a string"},
    {"task": "Replace line 42 with new code, but 'x = 1' appears 3 times",
     "correct": "Read+Write", "why": "Edit fails on non-unique text — use Read+Write fallback"},
    {"task": "Install the anthropic package",
     "correct": "Bash", "why": "Running pip install requires shell execution"},
    {"task": "Find all Python files that import 'json'",
     "correct": "Grep", "why": "Searching file contents for import statements"},
    {"task": "List all directories under src/",
     "correct": "Glob", "why": "Path-based discovery of directory structure"},
    {"task": "Create a new file called helper.py with specific content",
     "correct": "Write", "why": "Creating a new file from scratch"},
]

def run_quiz():
    print("=== Built-in Tool Selection Quiz ===")
    print("For each task, pick the right tool: Grep, Glob, Read, Write, Edit, Bash, or Read+Write\n")

    random.shuffle(SCENARIOS)
    score = 0

    for i, s in enumerate(SCENARIOS):
        print(f"\n{i+1}. Task: {s['task']}")
        answer = input("   Your answer: ").strip()

        if answer.lower() == s["correct"].lower():
            print(f"   ✓ Correct! {s['why']}")
            score += 1
        else:
            print(f"   ✗ Wrong. Correct: {s['correct']} — {s['why']}")

    print(f"\n\nScore: {score}/{len(SCENARIOS)}")
    if score == len(SCENARIOS):
        print("Perfect! You know your tools.")
    elif score >= len(SCENARIOS) * 0.8:
        print("Good — review the ones you missed.")
    else:
        print("Review the tool selection guide above and try again.")

    print("\n=== Quick Reference ===")
    print("Grep  = search file CONTENTS (patterns, functions, errors)")
    print("Glob  = search file PATHS (names, extensions, directories)")
    print("Read  = view a specific file")
    print("Write = create or completely replace a file")
    print("Edit  = modify specific text (must be unique in file)")
    print("Bash  = run shell commands (git, pip, tests)")
    print("Read+Write = fallback when Edit can't find unique text")


if __name__ == "__main__":
    run_quiz()

    python lab-d2-tool-design/tool_selection_quiz.py

    What you should see: An interactive 12-question quiz testing your tool selection knowledge. Immediate feedback on each answer with the reasoning.

    Break It — test your understanding

    1. Use Bash to read a file instead of Read. Does the content appear in Claude's context properly? (Answer: it works but doesn't integrate with Claude Code's file tracking — Read is preferred)
    2. Use Grep to find a file by name instead of Glob. Does it work? (Answer: no — Grep searches CONTENTS, Glob searches PATHS. Wrong tool for the job)
    3. Use Edit on text that appears 3 times. What happens? (Answer: Edit fails because it can't determine which occurrence to change — fall back to Read + Write)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "find all callers of function X" → Grep (content search), NOT Glob
    • If the exam says "find all *.test.tsx files" → Glob (path pattern), NOT Grep
    • Wrong answer trap: Confusing Grep (searches CONTENT) with Glob (searches PATHS)
    • The correct pattern: Grep = what's inside files. Glob = which files exist. Read = view a specific file.

    ✅ Lab 2.5 Checkpoint

    • Grep: searches file CONTENTS (patterns, function names, error messages)
    • Glob: searches file PATHS (by name or extension pattern)
    • Edit: targeted modification — requires unique text match
    • Read + Write: fallback when Edit can't find unique anchor text
    • Build codebase understanding incrementally: Grep → Read → Grep

    Quick Check — Module 2

    Q: Your agent has two tools with similar descriptions. Users report wrong tool selection 30% of the time. What's the most effective first step?

    A) Add 8 few-shot examples showing correct tool selection B) Expand each tool's description with input formats, examples, and when-NOT-to-use guidance C) Build a routing classifier to pre-select the correct tool D) Consolidate both tools into one

    Answer

    B. Tool descriptions are the primary mechanism for selection. Expanding them is the lowest-effort, highest-leverage fix. Few-shot examples (A) add token overhead. A classifier (C) is over-engineered as a first step. Consolidation (D) requires more effort. (Lab 2.1, Exam Sample Q2)

    Q: A subagent's database query times out. It returns {"error": "Operation failed"}. The coordinator can't decide whether to retry or proceed. What's wrong?

    A) The subagent should retry automatically with exponential backoff B) The error should include failure type, attempted query, partial results, and alternatives C) The coordinator should terminate the entire workflow D) The error should be suppressed and an empty result returned

    Answer

    B. Structured error context enables intelligent recovery decisions. Generic errors (A's output) hide context. Suppression (D) and termination (C) are both anti-patterns. (Lab 2.2, Exam Sample Q8)

    MODULE 3: CLAUDE CODE CONFIGURATION & WORKFLOWS

    Domain 3 — 20% of exam Task Statements 3.1 – 3.6

    Key Terms for Module 3

    • CLAUDE.md: Configuration file that gives Claude Code project context, coding standards, and instructions. Loaded automatically at session start.
    • CLAUDE.md hierarchy: Three levels of configuration that merge together:
      • User-level (~/.claude/CLAUDE.md): Personal preferences, NOT shared via git
      • Project-level (root CLAUDE.md or .claude/CLAUDE.md): Team standards, shared via git
      • Directory-level (subdirectory CLAUDE.md): Local overrides for specific packages
    • @import: Directive in CLAUDE.md that loads rules from another file. Keeps CLAUDE.md modular instead of one giant file.
    • .claude/rules/: Directory for topic-specific rule files (e.g., testing.md, api-conventions.md). Alternative to a monolithic CLAUDE.md.
    • Path-specific rules: Rule files with YAML frontmatter paths: field containing glob patterns. Rules load ONLY when editing files matching those patterns.
    • Slash command: A /command you type in Claude Code to invoke a reusable workflow. Stored in .claude/commands/ (project-shared) or ~/.claude/commands/ (personal).
    • Skill: A more advanced command stored in .claude/skills/ with a SKILL.md file supporting frontmatter configuration.
    • context: fork: Skill frontmatter that runs the skill in an isolated sub-session. Prevents verbose output from polluting your main conversation context.
    • allowed-tools: Skill frontmatter restricting which tools the skill can use (e.g., only Read, Write, Glob — no Bash).
    • argument-hint: Skill frontmatter that prompts users for input when they invoke the skill. Referenced as $ARGUMENTS in the skill body.
    • Plan mode (/plan): Mode where Claude explores and designs before making changes. Use for complex, multi-file, architectural tasks.
    • Direct execution: Normal mode where Claude acts immediately. Use for simple, well-scoped changes.
    • Explore subagent: Isolated sub-session for verbose file discovery. Returns only a summary to keep your main context clean.
    • -p flag: Runs Claude Code in non-interactive mode (no stdin). Required for CI/CD pipelines.
    • --output-format json: Returns Claude Code output as JSON instead of text.
    • --json-schema: Enforces a JSON schema on the output. Combined with --output-format json for machine-parseable CI results.
    • /memory: Command to see which CLAUDE.md and rule files are currently loaded. Use to diagnose configuration issues.
    • /compact: Summarizes context, removing verbose tool outputs while preserving key findings.

    Platform note: Labs 3.1-3.6 ask you to create configuration files by telling Claude Code what to create. The file contents are shown in code blocks so you can see exactly what goes in each file. You can also create these files manually if you prefer. Each lab includes a verification step to confirm your config files are correct.

    LAB 3.1: CLAUDE.MD HIERARCHY AND MODULAR ORGANIZATION

    Domain 3 — Task Statement 3.1 User/project/directory hierarchy, @import, .claude/rules/ ~20 minutesGuided | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When should you use Grep vs Glob? (Lab 2.5)
    2. What is the incremental codebase exploration pattern? (Lab 2.5)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your team has coding standards — "use async/await for API handlers, use functional components in React, always write tests." You put them all in one giant CLAUDE.md. It works, but:

    • New devs add personal preferences that apply to everyone
    • The file grows to 500 lines and Claude starts ignoring parts of it ("lost in the middle")
    • Some rules only apply to /src/api/ but Claude applies them everywhere

    The solution: a hierarchy.

    • User-level (~/.claude/CLAUDE.md) — your personal preferences. NOT in git. Only you see it.
    • Project-level (root CLAUDE.md) — team standards. In git. Everyone shares it.
    • Directory-level — subdirectory-specific overrides. A /src/api/CLAUDE.md with async rules.
    • @import — splits a huge CLAUDE.md into modular files (@import ./standards/testing.md)
    • .claude/rules/ — auto-loaded rules with glob patterns for path-specific conventions

    Exam connection (Sample Q6): "Test files spread throughout the codebase need the same conventions" → .claude/rules/ with paths: ["**/*.test.*"]. NOT directory-level CLAUDE.md (can't cover files scattered across dirs).

    What You'll Learn

    • The 3-level hierarchy and what belongs at each level (personal vs team vs directory)
    • @import for modular configuration that doesn't hit context limits
    • .claude/rules/ with YAML frontmatter for path-scoped auto-loading
    • How to verify what's loaded with /memory

    See It In Action

    Run this in Claude Code to see what configuration is currently loaded:

    /memory

    You'll see which CLAUDE.md files and rules are active. Right now it may be empty or minimal. By the end of this lab, you'll have a full hierarchy — user-level, project-level, and path-specific rules — and /memory will show all of them.

    Step 1: Create the full CLAUDE.md hierarchy

    Ask Claude Code to create the project-level CLAUDE.md and directory structure:

    "Create the directories .claude/rules, .claude/commands, and .claude/skills. Then create a project-level CLAUDE.md with the content below."

    # Claude Architect Labs — Project Configuration

## Project Overview
This is a hands-on lab project for the Claude Certified Architect exam.
All Python files use the anthropic library with model claude-sonnet-4-6.

## Standards
@import .claude/rules/testing.md
@import .claude/rules/api-conventions.md

## Key Patterns
- All agents use while True loop with stop_reason control
- Tool results always appended as role "user" with type "tool_result"
- Error responses always include: isError, errorCategory, isRetryable

    Step 2: Create modular rule files

    Ask Claude Code to create these two rule files:

    "Create the file .claude/rules/testing.md with the content below."

    # Testing Standards

## Test file naming: test_<module>.py
## Always test both success and failure paths
## Error category tests: transient, validation, business, permission
## Test gate blocking: verify prerequisite gates block unauthorized calls

    "Create the file .claude/rules/api-conventions.md with the content below."

    # API Conventions

## Model: always use claude-sonnet-4-6 (not claude-sonnet-4-20250514)
## max_tokens: 1024 for agents, 512 for extraction, 256 for classification
## Always check stop_reason before processing response content
## Tool definitions: include purpose, input format, output, when-to-use, when-NOT-to-use

    Step 3: Create path-specific rules (Task Statement 3.3)

    Path-specific rules load ONLY when editing matching files. This is better than directory-level CLAUDE.md when conventions span multiple dirs.

    Ask Claude Code to create these two path-specific rule files:

    "Create the file .claude/rules/python-agents.md with the content below."

    ---
paths:
  - "lab-d1-agentic-arch/**/*.py"
  - "lab-d5-context-reliability/**/*.py"
---
# Agentic Python Rules (loads only for agent lab files)

## Always include: while True loop, stop_reason check, tool result appending
## Error handling: structured errors with isError, errorCategory, isRetryable
## Client: always anthropic.Anthropic() (not OpenAI client)

    "Create the file .claude/rules/config-files.md with the content below."

    ---
paths:
  - "**/*.json"
  - ".mcp.json"
---
# Config File Rules (loads only when editing JSON/config files)

## MCP configs: use ${ENV_VAR} syntax for all credentials
## Never commit actual API tokens or secrets
## Include both project-scoped (.mcp.json) and note user-scoped (~/.claude.json)

    Step 4: Verify the hierarchy with /memory

    In Claude Code, run:

    /memory

    Expected output (example):

    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ Memory files loaded:                                     │
│   ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md               (user-level)         │
│   ./CLAUDE.md                       (project-level)      │
│   ./.claude/rules/testing.md        (path-scoped rule)   │
│   ./.claude/rules/api-conventions.md (path-scoped rule)  │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

    How to interpret /memory output:

    • See which files are actually loaded (vs which you expected)
    • If a teammate isn't getting conventions → check if rules are in user-level ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md (not shared) vs project-level CLAUDE.md (shared via git)
    • If a path-specific rule isn't loading → verify the glob pattern matches the file being edited
    • If rules load inconsistently across sessions → use /memory to diagnose which config files the session sees

    Step 5: Create a user-level CLAUDE.md (personal, not shared)

    This file is NOT committed to version control. Ask Claude Code to create it:

    "Create the file ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md with the content below."

    # Personal Claude Configuration

## My preferences (not shared with team):
## - Prefer concise responses
## - Use my personal shortcuts
## - My development machine has Python 3.13 at /usr/local/bin/python3

    Key insight: If a new team member isn't getting project conventions, it's because the instructions are in their user-level ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md instead of the project-level CLAUDE.md.

    Step 6: Verify your setup

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this verification script:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/verify_config.py with the code below and run it."

    import os
import re
import glob as glob_module

def check_file(path, label, required_content=None):
    """Check if a config file exists and contains expected content."""
    if os.path.exists(path):
        with open(path) as f:
            content = f.read()
        size = len(content)
        print(f"  ✓ {label}: {path} ({size} chars)")
        if required_content:
            for item in required_content:
                if item in content:
                    print(f"    ✓ Contains: {item}")
                else:
                    print(f"    ✗ Missing: {item}")
        return True
    else:
        print(f"  ✗ {label}: {path} — NOT FOUND (create it first)")
        return False


def verify_lab_3_1():
    """Verify CLAUDE.md hierarchy and modular organization."""
    print("\n=== Lab 3.1: CLAUDE.md Hierarchy ===\n")

    # Project-level CLAUDE.md
    check_file("CLAUDE.md", "Project CLAUDE.md",
               ["@import"])

    # Rules directory
    rules = glob_module.glob(".claude/rules/*.md")
    if rules:
        print(f"  ✓ .claude/rules/: {len(rules)} rule file(s)")
        for r in rules:
            check_file(r, f"  Rule file", None)
    else:
        print("  ✗ .claude/rules/: No rule files found")

    # User-level
    home = os.path.expanduser("~")
    check_file(os.path.join(home, ".claude", "CLAUDE.md"), "User-level CLAUDE.md")


def verify_lab_3_2():
    """Verify custom commands and skills."""
    print("\n=== Lab 3.2: Commands & Skills ===\n")

    # Commands
    cmds = glob_module.glob(".claude/commands/*.md")
    if cmds:
        print(f"  ✓ .claude/commands/: {len(cmds)} command(s)")
        for c in cmds:
            print(f"    - {os.path.basename(c)}")
    else:
        print("  ✗ .claude/commands/: No commands found")

    # Skills
    skills = glob_module.glob(".claude/skills/*.md")
    if skills:
        print(f"  ✓ .claude/skills/: {len(skills)} skill(s)")
        for s in skills:
            with open(s) as f:
                content = f.read()
            has_fork = "context: fork" in content
            has_tools = "allowed-tools:" in content
            has_hint = "argument-hint:" in content
            features = []
            if has_fork: features.append("context:fork")
            if has_tools: features.append("allowed-tools")
            if has_hint: features.append("argument-hint")
            feat_str = ", ".join(features) if features else "no frontmatter"
            print(f"    - {os.path.basename(s)} [{feat_str}]")
    else:
        print("  ✗ .claude/skills/: No skills found")


def verify_lab_3_3():
    """Verify path-specific rules with YAML frontmatter."""
    print("\n=== Lab 3.3: Path-Specific Rules ===\n")

    rules = glob_module.glob(".claude/rules/*.md")
    path_rules = 0
    for r in rules:
        with open(r) as f:
            content = f.read()
        if content.startswith("---"):
            # Extract YAML frontmatter
            parts = content.split("---", 2)
            if len(parts) >= 3:
                frontmatter = parts[1]
                if "paths:" in frontmatter:
                    path_rules += 1
                    patterns = re.findall(r'- "([^"]+)"', frontmatter)
                    print(f"  ✓ {os.path.basename(r)}: paths = {patterns}")

    if path_rules == 0:
        print("  ✗ No path-specific rules found (need YAML frontmatter with 'paths:' field)")
    else:
        print(f"\n  Total path-specific rules: {path_rules}")


if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("Claude Code Configuration Verifier")
    print("=" * 45)
    verify_lab_3_1()
    verify_lab_3_2()
    verify_lab_3_3()
    print("\n✓ Verification complete. Fix any ✗ items above, then run /memory in Claude Code to confirm loading.")

    If Claude Code created the file but did not run it, ask:

    "Run the verify_config.py script you just created."

    What you should see:

    • Lab 3.1: ✓/✗ for project CLAUDE.md, rule files, user-level CLAUDE.md, @import directives
    • Lab 3.2: List of commands and skills with their frontmatter features (fork, tools, hint)
    • Lab 3.3: Path-specific rules with their glob patterns extracted from YAML frontmatter

    Break It

    1. Put team conventions in ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md instead of project CLAUDE.md — new team members don't get them.
    2. Remove @import and put everything in one giant CLAUDE.md — context fills up, Claude ignores rules at the bottom.
    3. Create .claude/rules/ without YAML frontmatter paths: field — rule loads for ALL files, not just matching ones.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "new teammate doesn't get conventions" → check if rules are in ~/.claude/ (personal) vs project-level (shared)
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add rules to each directory's CLAUDE.md" — doesn't scale for cross-directory patterns
    • The correct pattern: Project-level CLAUDE.md + .claude/rules/ with glob patterns for shared conventions

    ✅ Lab 3.1 Checkpoint

    • CLAUDE.md hierarchy: user-level → project-level → directory-level
    • User-level (~/.claude/CLAUDE.md) is NOT shared via version control
    • @import keeps CLAUDE.md modular — imports topic-specific rule files
    • .claude/rules/ with path-specific YAML frontmatter for conditional loading
    • /memory shows which config files are currently loaded

    LAB 3.2: CUSTOM SLASH COMMANDS AND SKILLS

    Domain 3 — Task Statement 3.2 Project vs user commands, skills with frontmatter, context:fork ~20 minutesGuided | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What are the three levels of the CLAUDE.md hierarchy? (Lab 3.1)
    2. Why don't teammates see your ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md settings? (Lab 3.1)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your team builds a /review command that runs a standard code review checklist. It works great — but it generates 2,000 tokens of output that clutter your main conversation. Next time you ask Claude a question, it's distracted by the review output still in context.

    Worse: The review command calls Bash to run tests, but someone modifies it to also run rm -rf as a cleanup step. Now every /review invocation can delete files.

    The solutions:

    • Commands (.claude/commands/) — simple reusable prompts, shared via git. No isolation.
    • Skills (.claude/skills/) — prompts with superpowers: context: fork runs in isolated context (output doesn't pollute your session), allowed-tools restricts which tools it can use (no accidental rm -rf), argument-hint prompts the user for input.

    Exam connection (Sample Q4): "Where should you create a shared /review command?" → .claude/commands/ (project-scoped, version-controlled). NOT ~/.claude/commands/ (personal).

    What You'll Learn

    • Commands vs Skills: When to use each, and why skills have capabilities commands don't
    • context: fork: Run in isolation so output doesn't pollute your main session
    • allowed-tools: Restrict which tools a skill can access (security boundary)
    • Project vs personal scope: .claude/ (shared via git) vs ~/. claude/ (personal only)

    See It In Action

    Try listing what commands are already available:

    /commands

    You'll see built-in commands like /help, /memory, /compact. After this lab, your custom project commands and skills will appear here too — shared with your team through version control.

    Commands vs Skills — What's the Difference?

    Both are invoked with /name in Claude Code. The difference is in capabilities:

    Feature Command (.claude/commands/) Skill (.claude/skills/)
    Invocation /command-name /skill-name
    Content Simple prompt text Prompt text with YAML frontmatter
    context:fork No Yes — runs in isolated context
    allowed-tools No Yes — restricts available tools
    argument-hint No Yes — prompts user for input
    Best for Simple reusable prompts Complex workflows needing isolation/restrictions

    Exam concept (Sample Q4): Project-scoped commands go in .claude/commands/ (shared via git). Personal commands go in ~/.claude/commands/ (not shared). Skills go in .claude/skills/.

    Step 1: Create a project-scoped slash command

    Ask Claude Code to create this command file:

    "Create the file .claude/commands/review-agent.md with the content below."

    Review the Python agent file at $ARGUMENTS for:
1. Correct stop_reason handling (tool_use and end_turn)
2. Tool results properly appended as role "user"
3. Structured error responses (isError, errorCategory, isRetryable)
4. No anti-patterns (text parsing for loop termination, arbitrary iteration caps)

Report: PASS or FAIL for each check, with specific line numbers for failures.

    Usage in Claude Code: /review-agent lab-d1-agentic-arch/basic_loop.py

    Step 2: Create a skill with frontmatter

    Ask Claude Code to create this skill file:

    "Create the file .claude/skills/explore-codebase.md with the content below."

    ---
context: fork
allowed-tools: Read, Grep, Glob
argument-hint: "Enter the module name or question to explore"
---
Explore the codebase to answer: $ARGUMENTS

Use Grep to find relevant code, Read to examine files, Glob to discover structure.
Return a concise summary of findings — do not modify any files.

    Why context: fork?

    • Exploration produces verbose output (dozens of file reads)
    • Without fork: all that output floods your main conversation context
    • With fork: runs in isolated sub-agent, returns only the summary
    • Use for: codebase exploration, brainstorming, verbose analysis

    Step 3: Create a skill with allowed-tools restriction

    Ask Claude Code to create this skill file:

    "Create the file .claude/skills/generate-tests.md with the content below."

    ---
context: fork
allowed-tools: Read, Write, Glob
argument-hint: "Enter the Python file path to generate tests for"
---
Generate a comprehensive test suite for: $ARGUMENTS

Read the target file. Write a test file named test_<filename>.py
in the same directory.

Do NOT run tests. Do NOT install packages. Do NOT modify source files.

    The allowed-tools: Read, Write, Glob prevents the skill from running Bash commands during test generation — no accidental pip install or rm.

    Step 4: Create a personal variant

    Ask Claude Code to create this personal skill (not shared with team):

    "Create the file ~/.claude/skills/my-explore.md with the content below."

    ---
context: fork
allowed-tools: Read, Grep, Glob
argument-hint: "What do you want to explore?"
---
Personal exploration skill (not shared with team).
Explore: $ARGUMENTS

Return findings as a bulleted list. Be concise.

    This won't affect teammates — it's in ~/.claude/skills/, not .claude/skills/.

    Step 5: Understand skills vs CLAUDE.md

    CLAUDE.md: Always-loaded universal standards
  → Use for: project-wide conventions, always-on rules

Skills: On-demand invocation for specific workflows
  → Use for: tasks you run sometimes (not always)
  → Example: /generate-tests, /review-agent, /explore-codebase

    What you should see when you run verify_config.py: Your commands listed under ".claude/commands/" and skills listed with their frontmatter features ([context:fork], [allowed-tools], [argument-hint]).

    Break It — test your understanding

    1. Run a skill WITHOUT context: fork. Does its output appear in your main conversation? (Answer: yes — without fork, all the skill's verbose output pollutes your context)
    2. Remove allowed-tools from the skill. Can it now call Bash, Write, and other tools? (Answer: yes — without restrictions, the skill has full access, which is a security risk)
    3. Put a personal command in .claude/commands/ instead of ~/.claude/commands/. Does it get committed to git? (Answer: yes — personal commands should go in ~/.claude/commands/ to stay out of version control)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "share a command with the team" → .claude/commands/ (project, version-controlled)
    • Wrong answer trap: "Put it in ~/.claude/commands/" — personal, not shared via git
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add it to CLAUDE.md" — CLAUDE.md is for instructions, not command definitions
    • The correct pattern: Commands in .claude/commands/ (shared). Skills in .claude/skills/ with frontmatter (isolation, tool restrictions).

    ✅ Lab 3.2 Checkpoint

    • .claude/commands/ → project-scoped, version-controlled, team-shared
    • ~/.claude/commands/ → user-scoped, personal, not shared
    • context: fork → isolated sub-agent, prevents context pollution
    • allowed-tools → restricts skill's tool access (security boundary)
    • argument-hint → prompts user for required parameters

    LAB 3.3: PATH-SPECIFIC RULES

    Domain 3 — Task Statement 3.3 YAML frontmatter paths, glob patterns, conditional loading ~15 minutesGuided | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What does context:fork do in a skill's frontmatter? (Lab 3.2)
    2. Where do project-scoped commands go vs personal commands? (Lab 3.2)

    Why This Matters

    Your test files are scattered across 12 directories. You put test conventions in a directory-level CLAUDE.md — but now you need 12 copies. When conventions change, you update one and forget the others. Path-specific rules with glob patterns (paths: [**/.test.]) apply automatically regardless of directory.

    Exam connection (Sample Q6): Test files spread throughout the codebase → .claude/rules/ with glob patterns. NOT directory-level CLAUDE.md.

    What You'll Learn

    Path-specific rules load ONLY when editing matching files. Better than directory-level CLAUDE.md when conventions apply to files spread across dirs.

    See It In Action

    Open a test file in your project and ask Claude Code:

    What conventions or rules apply to this file?

    Then open a non-test source file and ask the same question. Right now, the answer is the same for both. After this lab, path-specific rules will automatically load different conventions based on the file you're editing — test files get testing rules, API files get API conventions.

    Step 1: Create path-scoped rule files

    Ask Claude Code to create these three path-scoped rule files:

    "Create the file .claude/rules/test-conventions.md with the content below."

    ---
paths:
  - "**/*.test.py"
  - "**/*_test.py"
  - "**/test_*.py"
---
# Test File Conventions
# (Only loads when editing test files — not loaded for source files)

## Test structure: Arrange-Act-Assert pattern
## Each test function tests exactly ONE behavior
## Test names: test_<method>_<condition>_<expected_result>
## For agent tests: always reset gate state between tests
## Mock at system boundaries only — don't mock internal functions

    "Create the file .claude/rules/terraform-conventions.md with the content below."

    ---
paths:
  - "terraform/**/*"
  - "**/*.tf"
  - "**/*.tfvars"
---
# Terraform Conventions
# (Only loads when editing Terraform files)

## Always use remote state backend
## Tag all resources with: environment, team, cost-center
## Never hardcode credentials — use variables

    "Create the file .claude/rules/api-routes.md with the content below."

    ---
paths:
  - "src/api/**/*.py"
  - "src/routes/**/*.py"
---
# API Route Conventions
# (Only loads when editing API route files)

## Always validate input at entry point
## Return structured error responses (same format as MCP tools)
## Authentication check before any data access

    Step 2: Compare to directory-level CLAUDE.md

    Path-specific rules (.claude/rules/test-conventions.md with paths: ["**/*.test.py"]):
✓ Applies to ALL test files regardless of directory
✓ Single file to maintain
✓ Doesn't load for non-test files (saves tokens)
✗ Requires YAML frontmatter syntax

Directory-level CLAUDE.md (in src/tests/CLAUDE.md):
✓ Simple — just a file in the directory
✗ Only applies to files IN that directory
✗ Test files scattered across the repo don't get the rules
✗ Multiple copies needed for multiple test directories

EXAM RULE: When conventions apply to files spread across the codebase
(e.g., test files, config files, API routes) → use path-specific rules.
When conventions apply to one specific directory → use directory CLAUDE.md.

    Step 3: Verify rules load correctly

    In Claude Code, open a test file and ask:

    > What testing conventions apply to this file?

    Claude should mention the test-conventions.md rules. Then open a regular source file and ask the same — those rules should NOT appear.

    What you should see: When editing a test file (e.g., test_basic.py), Claude cites the Arrange-Act-Assert pattern and naming convention from test-conventions.md. When editing a regular source file (e.g., basic_loop.py), those test rules do NOT appear — only rules matching that file’s path load.

    Break It - test your understanding

    1. Remove the paths: frontmatter from test-conventions.md. Now the rule loads for ALL files, not just test files. You’ve lost the scoping benefit — every file gets test conventions cluttering its context.
    2. Use directory CLAUDE.md for scattered test files — put test conventions in src/tests/CLAUDE.md. Files in src/api/tests/, src/models/tests/, etc. don’t get the rules. You need 12 copies.
    3. Use wrong glob pattern (e.g., paths: ["*.test.py"] without **/) — rule never loads because glob doesn’t match files in subdirectories.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "conventions for files spread across directories" → .claude/rules/ with glob patterns
    • Wrong answer trap: "Put a CLAUDE.md in each directory" — doesn't handle files spread throughout the codebase
    • The correct pattern: paths: ["**/*.test.tsx"] in YAML frontmatter loads rules for matching files regardless of location

    ✅ Lab 3.3 Checkpoint

    • .claude/rules/ files with paths: frontmatter = path-specific rules
    • Rules load ONLY when editing files matching the glob patterns
    • glob patterns apply regardless of directory (e.g., **/*.test.py)
    • Better than directory CLAUDE.md for cross-directory conventions
    • Reduces irrelevant context and token usage

    LAB 3.4: PLAN MODE VS DIRECT EXECUTION

    Domain 3 — Task Statement 3.4 When to plan first, when to act directly, Explore subagent ~15 minutesGuided | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When are path-specific rules better than directory-level CLAUDE.md? (Lab 3.3)
    2. What YAML frontmatter field activates conditional rule loading? (Lab 3.3)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your manager asks you to "restructure the monolith into microservices." You open Claude Code and start making changes directly. Three files in, you realize the service boundaries are wrong — the payment module depends on the user module in ways you didn't see. Now you have to undo everything and start over.

    The other problem: Someone asks you to "fix the off-by-one error on line 42 of parser.py." You enter plan mode, spend 5 minutes exploring the codebase and designing an approach... for a one-line fix. Total waste of time.

    The rule: Complex tasks with multiple valid approaches → plan first, then execute. Clear, well-scoped tasks → execute directly. The Explore subagent lets you investigate without polluting your main conversation with verbose discovery output.

    Exam connection (Sample Q5): "Restructure a monolith" → plan mode. "Fix an off-by-one error" → direct execution. The exam tests your judgment about when each mode is appropriate.

    See It In Action

    Try both modes in Claude Code:

    Direct execution — give a simple, specific task:

    Add a comment to the top of lab-d1-agentic-arch/basic_loop.py explaining what this file does

    Plan mode — give a complex, open-ended task:

    /plan How should I restructure the lab files to separate production code from test utilities?

    Notice the difference: direct execution acts immediately. Plan mode explores first, considers approaches, then presents a plan for your approval before making changes. The decision of which to use is an exam-tested concept.

    What You'll Learn

    • Plan mode: Explore and design before committing — prevents costly rework on complex tasks
    • Direct execution: Act immediately on clear, well-scoped changes
    • Explore subagent: Isolates verbose discovery output so your main context stays clean
    • Combined pattern: Plan → execute → /compact when context fills up

    Step 1: Decision guide

    Create lab-d3-claude-code/plan_vs_direct.md:

    # Plan Mode vs Direct Execution Decision Guide

## Use Plan Mode (/plan) when:
- Task has architectural implications
  Example: "Migrate from SQLite to PostgreSQL" — multiple files, schema changes, ORM config
- Multiple valid approaches exist and you need to evaluate tradeoffs
  Example: "Add authentication" — JWT vs sessions vs OAuth2
- Large-scale changes affecting many files (5+ files)
  Example: "Rename the Customer class to Client throughout the codebase"
- Infrastructure changes with different requirements
  Example: "Add caching" — Redis vs in-memory vs CDN have different infra needs
- Refactoring with unclear blast radius
  Example: "Restructure the module hierarchy"

## Use Direct Execution when:
- Simple, well-scoped single-file change
  Example: "Fix the off-by-one error on line 47 of auth.py"
- Clear stack trace pointing to specific location
  Example: "KeyError: 'user_id' at api.py:83 — add a check"
- Adding a simple conditional or validation
  Example: "Add a null check before accessing order.tracking"
- Change has no cross-file effects
  Example: "Update the error message in process_refund"

## The Explore Subagent (for plan mode):
When exploration generates verbose output (reading 20+ files):
→ Use Explore subagent to isolate discovery output
→ Explore returns a SUMMARY, not all the raw tool call outputs
→ Main conversation context stays clean

In Claude Code: "Use an Explore subagent to map the codebase structure,
then report back with a summary before we plan the migration."

## Pattern: Plan → Execute
1. Enter plan mode for architectural decisions
2. Get Claude's proposed plan + tradeoffs
3. Review and approve (or modify) the plan
4. Exit plan mode → execute the approved approach

    Step 2: Practice in Claude Code

    # SCENARIO A: Direct execution (simple fix)
> The validate_email function in lab-d2-tool-design/tool_descriptions.py
  doesn't handle None input. Fix it.

    Claude should act directly — no planning needed, clear scope, single file.

    # SCENARIO B: Plan mode (architectural decision)
> I want to add persistent storage to my lab agents so they can
  save and reload conversation history between runs. Plan this out.

    Claude should enter plan mode. You'll see it explore options:

    • File-based (JSON) vs SQLite vs Redis
    • Where to store the history
    • How to handle backward compatibility

    Step 3: Using the Explore subagent

    The Explore subagent isolates verbose discovery output so your main context stays clean.

    # Request an Explore subagent for verbose discovery
> Use an Explore subagent to map the entire lab codebase structure:
  find all Python files, identify which domain each belongs to,
  count test cases per module. Return only a summary.

    What happens:

    1. Claude spawns an Explore subagent (isolated context)
    2. The subagent reads 20+ files, runs Grep/Glob — verbose output stays in its context
    3. Only the summary comes back to your main session
    4. Your main conversation context stays clean for the next task

    Without Explore: All those file reads fill your context → model degrades. With Explore: You get just the summary → context stays clean for planning.

    Step 4: Combining plan mode + direct execution

    The real power is using plan mode for investigation, then switching to direct execution:

    # Phase 1: Plan mode — investigate and design
> /plan
> I need to add a caching layer to all tool calls in the agent.
  Explore the codebase first, then propose an approach.

    Claude investigates without making changes. You review the plan.

    # Phase 2: Direct execution — implement the approved plan
> /exitplan
> Implement option A from the plan: add an in-memory LRU cache
  to execute_tool() in basic_loop.py with a 5-minute TTL.

    Now Claude executes the specific, well-scoped implementation.

    Exam concept: Plan mode prevents costly rework on complex tasks. Direct execution is efficient for clear, well-scoped changes. Combining them = best of both worlds.

    Step 5: Observe context preservation with /compact

    In a long exploration session:

    > Let's explore the entire lab codebase — read every file and understand all the patterns.

    Watch context fill up with verbose tool outputs. Then try:

    > /compact

    Claude summarizes what's in context, clearing verbose tool outputs. The key findings are preserved; the noise is removed.

    Step 6: Test your judgment

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this quiz script:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/plan_vs_direct_quiz.py with the code below and run it."

    SCENARIOS = [
    {"task": "Fix the off-by-one error on line 42 of parser.py",
     "answer": "direct", "why": "Clear scope, single file, known location"},
    {"task": "Restructure the monolith into microservices",
     "answer": "plan", "why": "Multiple valid approaches, architectural decisions, many files"},
    {"task": "Add a null check to the validate_email function",
     "answer": "direct", "why": "Simple fix, single function, well-scoped"},
    {"task": "Migrate from SQLite to PostgreSQL across the project",
     "answer": "plan", "why": "45+ files affected, multiple migration strategies, need to explore dependencies"},
    {"task": "Update the README with the new API endpoint",
     "answer": "direct", "why": "Single file, known content, straightforward"},
    {"task": "Add caching to all API endpoints with appropriate invalidation",
     "answer": "plan", "why": "Multiple cache strategies, need to understand data flow first"},
    {"task": "Rename 'userId' to 'user_id' in models.py",
     "answer": "direct", "why": "Mechanical change, clear scope"},
    {"task": "Investigate why tests are flaky and fix root causes",
     "answer": "plan", "why": "Open-ended investigation, unknown root causes, need exploration"},
]

def run_quiz():
    print("=== Plan Mode vs Direct Execution Quiz ===")
    print("For each task, answer: 'plan' or 'direct'\n")

    score = 0
    for i, s in enumerate(SCENARIOS):
        print(f"\n{i+1}. {s['task']}")
        answer = input("   plan or direct? ").strip().lower()

        if answer == s["answer"]:
            print(f"   ✓ Correct! {s['why']}")
            score += 1
        else:
            print(f"   ✗ Wrong. Answer: {s['answer']} — {s['why']}")

    print(f"\nScore: {score}/{len(SCENARIOS)}")
    print("\nRule: Known steps + clear scope → direct. Unknown complexity + multiple approaches → plan.")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    run_quiz()

    If Claude Code created the file but did not run it, ask:

    "Run the plan_vs_direct_quiz.py script you just created."

    What you should see: 8 real-world scenarios where you decide plan vs direct. Immediate feedback with reasoning.

    Break It — test your understanding

    1. Use direct execution for a monolith-to-microservices migration. What happens? (Answer: costly rework — you discover dependencies 3 files in and have to undo everything)
    2. Use plan mode for a one-line bug fix. What happens? (Answer: 5 minutes wasted exploring when you could have fixed it in 30 seconds)
    3. Skip the Explore subagent and do verbose file discovery in your main session. What fills up? (Answer: your context — 20+ file reads crowd out your actual work. Explore keeps main context clean)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "restructure a monolith" or "migration affecting 45+ files" → plan mode
    • If the exam says "fix off-by-one error" or "add one validation check" → direct execution
    • Wrong answer trap: "Start with direct execution and switch if needed" — for known-complex tasks, plan mode upfront prevents costly rework
    • The correct pattern: Architectural decisions and multi-file changes → plan mode. Single-file, clear-scope fixes → direct.

    ✅ Lab 3.4 Checkpoint

    • Plan mode: complex/architectural tasks, multiple valid approaches, 5+ files
    • Direct execution: simple, well-scoped, clear location, single file
    • Explore subagent: isolates verbose discovery output → returns summary to main context
    • Combining plan + execute: investigate in plan mode → implement in direct execution
    • /compact: reduces context during extended exploration sessions

    LAB 3.5: ITERATIVE REFINEMENT TECHNIQUES

    Domain 3 — Task Statement 3.5 Examples > descriptions, test-driven iteration, interview pattern ~20 minutesIntermediate | Claude Code or API key

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When should you use plan mode vs direct execution? (Lab 3.4)
    2. What does the Explore subagent prevent? (Lab 3.4)

    Why This Matters

    You tell Claude handle edge cases gracefully. It produces code that crashes on null input. You say be more robust. It adds try/except everywhere. The problem: prose instructions are ambiguous. The fix: show concrete input/output examples — Claude replicates the pattern exactly.

    What You'll Learn

    When prose instructions produce inconsistent results, examples fix it. Test-driven iteration: write tests first, share failures to guide improvement. Interview pattern: have Claude ask questions before implementing.

    See It In Action

    Try these two prompts in Claude Code and compare the results:

    Prompt 1 (vague):

    "Normalize these customer names from a CRM export. Make them properly formatted: JONES, BOB / dr. LISA PARK / MCDONALD, RONALD"

    Prompt 2 (examples-based):

    "Normalize these customer names. Follow this pattern: 'SMITH, JOHN' becomes 'John Smith', 'mary-jane watson' becomes 'Mary-Jane Watson', 'DR. CHEN, ALICE' becomes 'Dr. Alice Chen', 'O'BRIEN, PATRICK' becomes 'Patrick O'Brien'. Now apply the same transformation to: JONES, BOB / dr. LISA PARK / MCDONALD, RONALD"

    Notice how the vague prompt produces inconsistent formatting, while the examples-based prompt produces consistent results that match the demonstrated pattern exactly. This is the core principle: 2-3 concrete examples beat verbose prose descriptions.

    Reference Implementation: Examples beat descriptions

    lab-d3-claude-code/refinement_demo.py:

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

def demo_examples_vs_description():
    """
    When a transformation produces inconsistent results,
    2-3 concrete input/output examples fix it better than more prose.
    """

    # VAGUE INSTRUCTION (produces inconsistent results):
    vague_prompt = """
    Normalize customer names from our CRM export.
    Make them properly formatted.
    """

    # EXAMPLES-BASED INSTRUCTION (consistent results):
    examples_prompt = """
    Normalize customer names from our CRM export.

    Examples:
    Input:  "SMITH, JOHN"      → Output: "John Smith"
    Input:  "mary-jane watson" → Output: "Mary-Jane Watson"
    Input:  "DR. CHEN, ALICE"  → Output: "Dr. Alice Chen"
    Input:  "O'BRIEN, PATRICK" → Output: "Patrick O'Brien"

    Apply the same transformation to: $NAMES
    """

    test_names = ["JONES, BOB", "dr. LISA PARK", "MCDONALD, RONALD"]

    print("=== Vague instruction ===")
    r = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=256,
        messages=[{"role": "user",
                   "content": vague_prompt.replace("$NAMES", str(test_names))}]
    )
    print(r.content[0].text[:200])

    print("\n=== Examples-based instruction ===")
    r = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=256,
        messages=[{"role": "user",
                   "content": examples_prompt.replace("$NAMES", str(test_names))}]
    )
    print(r.content[0].text[:200])


def demo_test_driven_iteration():
    """
    Write tests first → share failures → guide model to fix issues.
    More effective than describing the problem in prose.
    """
    print("\n=== Test-Driven Iteration Pattern ===")

    # Step 1: Write tests that define expected behavior
    tests = [
        {"input": None,         "expected": None,       "case": "null input"},
        {"input": "",           "expected": None,       "case": "empty string"},
        {"input": "2024-01-15", "expected": "Jan 15, 2024", "case": "ISO date"},
        {"input": "01/15/2024", "expected": "Jan 15, 2024", "case": "US format"},
        {"input": "15-01-2024", "expected": "Jan 15, 2024", "case": "EU format"},
    ]

    # Step 2: Run tests against current implementation
    # (Simulate failures — in practice, run your test suite)
    failures = [
        t for t in tests
        if t["input"] in [None, ""]  # pretend null handling is broken
    ]

    if failures:
        # Step 3: Share specific test failures with Claude
        failure_msg = "\n".join([
            f"FAIL: input={t['input']!r} → got exception, expected {t['expected']!r}"
            for t in failures
        ])

        print("Sharing test failures with Claude...")
        r = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=512,
            messages=[{
                "role": "user",
                "content": f"""My date normalization function has failing tests:

{failure_msg}

The function currently does: return datetime.strptime(date_str, '%Y-%m-%d').strftime('%b %d, %Y')

Fix the function to handle these cases. Return just the fixed Python function."""
            }]
        )
        print(r.content[0].text[:300])


def demo_interview_pattern():
    """
    Interview pattern: Claude asks YOU questions before implementing.
    Surfaces design considerations you haven't thought of.
    """
    print("\n=== Interview Pattern ===")

    r = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=512,
        messages=[{
            "role": "user",
            "content": """I want you to implement a caching layer for my customer API calls.
Before you start implementing, ask me 3-5 questions about requirements and constraints
that will significantly affect the design. Do not implement anything yet."""
        }]
    )
    print("Claude's questions:")
    print(r.content[0].text[:400])

    print("""
    Why the interview pattern matters:
    - Cache invalidation strategies (TTL? Event-driven? Manual?)
    - Failure modes (what if cache is down? Fall through or error?)
    - Consistency requirements (is stale data acceptable?)
    - Scale (single process? Distributed?)

    These questions surface requirements you may not have specified.
    Answering them BEFORE implementation prevents costly rework.
    """)
