if __name__ == "__main__":
    demo_examples_vs_description()
    demo_test_driven_iteration()
    demo_interview_pattern()

    print("\n=== Multi-issue Message Strategy ===")
    print("""
    Multiple INTERACTING issues → one message with all issues:
    "Fix these three issues together: [issue1], [issue2], [issue3].
     Note: fixing issue1 affects issue2 — handle them as a unit."

    Multiple INDEPENDENT issues → sequential messages:
    Message 1: "Fix the null handling in validate_email"
    Message 2: (after fix confirmed) "Now fix the date parsing in normalize_date"

    Why: Interacting issues need to be seen together to be fixed correctly.
         Independent issues are safer to fix one at a time — easier to review.
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/refinement_demo.py with the code above and run it."

    What you should see:

    • "Vague instruction" output shows inconsistent name formatting, then "Examples-based instruction" output shows names consistently transformed (e.g., "JONES, BOB" becomes "Bob Jones")
    • A "Test-Driven Iteration Pattern" section where specific test failures (null input, empty string) are shared with Claude, and Claude returns a fixed Python function that handles those edge cases
    • An "Interview Pattern" section where Claude asks 3-5 clarifying questions about caching requirements (TTL, failure modes, consistency, scale) before implementing anything
    • A "Multi-issue Message Strategy" summary explaining when to combine interacting issues vs send independent issues sequentially

    Code Analysis

    1. Why do prose instructions ("handle null input gracefully") produce inconsistent results across runs? Prose is ambiguous -- "gracefully" could mean return None, return an empty string, raise a custom exception, log and skip, or any number of interpretations. Each time the model generates, it may pick a different interpretation. Concrete input/output examples (e.g., input: None -> output: None) eliminate ambiguity by showing the exact expected behavior.

    2. Why does the interview pattern surface better requirements than jumping straight to implementation? Without the interview pattern, Claude guesses at requirements instead of surfacing them. For a caching layer, critical questions include: cache invalidation strategy (TTL? event-driven?), failure mode (fall through or error if cache is down?), consistency requirements (is stale data acceptable?), and scale (single process or distributed?). These questions prevent costly rework by catching missing requirements before implementation begins.

    3. Why share failing tests rather than passing tests when iterating? Failing tests show the EXACT gap between current behavior and expected behavior. Passing tests confirm what already works but do not reveal what is broken. Sharing FAIL: input=None -> got exception, expected None gives Claude a precise target to fix.

    Exam connection: The exam tests whether you know that concrete examples beat prose for consistency, test-driven iteration surfaces exact gaps, and the interview pattern surfaces requirements you haven't considered.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "output format is inconsistent across runs" → add 2-3 concrete input/output examples
    • Wrong answer trap: "Write more detailed prose instructions" — prose is interpreted inconsistently
    • The correct pattern: Examples beat descriptions for format consistency and ambiguous case handling

    ✅ Lab 3.5 Checkpoint

    • 2-3 examples beat verbose prose descriptions for transformation tasks
    • Test-driven: write tests first → run → share specific failures → iterate
    • Interview pattern: ask questions BEFORE implementing in unfamiliar domains
    • Interacting issues → one message; independent issues → sequential messages

    LAB 3.6: CI/CD INTEGRATION

    Domain 3 — Task Statement 3.6 -p flag, --output-format json, independent review instances ~20 minutesAdvanced | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What are the three iterative refinement techniques? (Lab 3.5)
    2. When should you provide all issues in one message vs sequentially? (Lab 3.5)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: You add Claude to your CI pipeline: claude "Review this PR for security issues". The build hangs forever. Logs show Claude Code is waiting for interactive input — but there's no human at the keyboard in CI. Your PR is blocked, developers are waiting.

    The second problem: Claude reviews the PR and outputs prose text. Your automation script needs to parse it into structured comments for the GitHub PR. Parsing prose is fragile and breaks when Claude changes its formatting.

    The solutions:

    • -p flag → non-interactive mode (processes prompt, outputs result, exits)
    • --output-format json → structured output your scripts can parse reliably
    • --json-schema → guarantees the output matches your exact schema
    • Independent review instances → catch bugs that self-review misses (the generator's reasoning makes it blind to its own mistakes)

    Exam connection (Sample Q10): "Pipeline hangs" → -p flag. This is directly tested. (Sample Q11): "Reduce costs" → Batch API for overnight reports, synchronous for blocking checks.

    What You'll Learn

    • -p flag: Non-interactive mode for CI/CD — the #1 pipeline integration fix
    • --output-format json + --json-schema: Machine-parseable, schema-validated output
    • Independent review: Why a separate instance catches what self-review misses
    • Re-review pattern: Pass prior findings to avoid duplicate comments

    See It In Action

    Try non-interactive mode right now:

    claude -p "What is the -p flag used for in Claude Code?"

    Notice how it processes the prompt, outputs the answer, and exits — no interactive session. This is exactly what CI/CD pipelines need. Without -p, the job would hang waiting for interactive input (the #1 CI integration bug).

    Step 1: Create the CI review script

    Ask Claude Code to create the file lab-d3-claude-code/ci_review.sh with the following content:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/ci_review.sh with the content below and make it executable."

    #!/bin/bash
# CI Code Review Script
# Usage: ./ci_review.sh <file_to_review>

FILE=${1:-"lab-d1-agentic-arch/basic_loop.py"}

echo "=== Running Claude Code Review in CI Mode ==="
echo "File: $FILE"
echo ""

# -p flag: non-interactive mode (no stdin needed — won't hang in CI)
# --output-format json: machine-parseable for automated processing
claude -p "Review this Python file for:
1. Security issues (hardcoded credentials, injection vulnerabilities)
2. Bug risks (unhandled exceptions, off-by-one errors)
3. API misuse (wrong model name, missing required parameters)

File to review: $FILE

Return ONLY a JSON array of findings:
[{\"file\": \"...\", \"line\": N, \"severity\": \"high/medium/low\",
  \"issue\": \"...\", \"suggestion\": \"...\"}]
Skip style/formatting issues." \
  --output-format json \
  < /dev/null

echo ""
echo "=== Review complete ==="

    To try it (requires Claude Code CLI installed):

    "Run ./lab-d3-claude-code/ci_review.sh"

    What you should see: A JSON array of findings with file, line, severity, issue, and suggestion fields. The -p flag causes Claude to process the prompt and exit without waiting for input. The --output-format json wraps the response in a machine-parseable JSON structure.

    Step 2: Create the CLAUDE.md for CI context

    Create this file in your project to give CI reviews the right context:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/CLAUDE.md with the content below."

    # CI Review Context

## Review Criteria
Flag ONLY: bugs, security vulnerabilities, API misuse
SKIP: style, formatting, minor naming issues

## Severity Definitions
high:   Will cause errors in production (wrong model name, missing error handling)
medium: May cause errors in edge cases (unhandled None, missing validation)
low:    Best practice violations that could cause future issues

## Testing Standards
Available fixtures: CUSTOMERS dict, ORDERS dict, execute_tool function
Don't suggest tests that duplicate: basic_loop_test.py (already exists)

## Prior Review Findings (for re-review after commits)
Include these when re-reviewing to avoid duplicate comments.

    Step 3: Practice the independent review pattern

    This pattern uses two separate Claude Code sessions. Study how the -p flag enables this:

    # Generate code with one Claude instance
claude -p "Write a Python function that validates customer email addresses" > generated_code.py

# Review with an INDEPENDENT instance (different session, no prior context)
claude -p "Review this code for bugs and security issues: $(cat generated_code.py)" \
  --output-format json

    Why independent? The generator's reasoning context makes it less likely to question its own decisions. An independent reviewer has no attachment to the original approach.

    Step 4: The re-review pattern (avoid duplicate comments)

    Ask Claude Code to create this re-review script:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/re_review.sh with the content below and make it executable."

    #!/bin/bash
# Re-review after new commits — only report NEW or STILL-UNADDRESSED issues

PRIOR_FINDINGS=$1
NEW_CODE=$2

claude -p "Review this updated code.

PRIOR REVIEW FINDINGS (already reported — do NOT repeat these unless still present):
$PRIOR_FINDINGS

UPDATED CODE:
$NEW_CODE

Report ONLY:
1. New issues not in the prior findings
2. Prior issues that are STILL present (unaddressed)
Do NOT repeat prior issues that have been fixed." \
  --output-format json < /dev/null

    Step 5: Understand the CI pipeline config

    # .github/workflows/claude-review.yml
# name: Claude Code Review
# on: [pull_request]
# jobs:
#   review:
#     runs-on: ubuntu-latest
#     steps:
#       - uses: actions/checkout@v4
#       - name: Run Claude Code Review
#         run: |
#           claude -p "$(cat .claude/commands/ci-review.md)" \
#             --output-format json \
#             < /dev/null > findings.json
#       - name: Post PR Comments
#         run: python scripts/post_review_comments.py findings.json

    Key CI patterns:

    • -p flag: prevents interactive input hang in CI
    • < /dev/null: ensures no stdin in automated environment
    • --output-format json: machine-parseable for automated processing
    • CLAUDE.md in project: provides testing standards, fixtures, review criteria
    • Independent instance: generates code in one run, reviews in separate run

    Prerequisite: This lab requires the Claude Code CLI (claude command). Install it first: npm install -g @anthropic-ai/claude-code (or see https://docs.anthropic.com/en/docs/claude-code). If you can't install it, study the bash scripts and YAML configs — the exam tests the CONCEPTS (when to use -p, what --json-schema does), not the exact CLI output.

    Break It - test your understanding

    1. Remove the -p flag in CI — pipeline hangs waiting for interactive input. No human at the keyboard. Build times out.
    2. Use the same instance for generate + review — Claude’s reasoning context makes it blind to its own mistakes. It defends its choices instead of finding bugs.
    3. Skip --json-schema — output format varies between runs. Your parser breaks when Claude changes its response structure.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "CI job hangs" → -p flag for non-interactive mode
    • If the exam says "review misses issues the generator should have caught" → independent review instance
    • Wrong answer trap: "CLAUDE_HEADLESS=true" — doesn't exist
    • Wrong answer trap: "Self-review with extended thinking" — same session context makes self-review blind
    • The correct pattern: Separate generation and review into independent sessions

    ✅ Lab 3.6 Checkpoint

    • -p flag runs Claude Code non-interactively (required for CI)
    • --output-format json produces machine-parseable findings
    • --json-schema <file> enforces a specific JSON schema on the output (use with --output-format json)
    • CLAUDE.md provides project context (test standards, fixtures, review criteria) to CI
    • Include existing test files in context so Claude doesn't suggest duplicate test scenarios
    • Independent review instances: generator and reviewer are separate sessions
    • Re-review: pass prior findings to avoid duplicate PR comments

    Step 6: The --json-schema flag

    The --json-schema flag enforces a specific output schema in CI — guarantees machine-parseable structure:

    First, create a schema file. Ask Claude Code:

    "Create the file lab-d3-claude-code/review-schema.json with the content below."

    {
  "type": "array",
  "items": {
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "file":       {"type": "string"},
      "line":       {"type": "integer"},
      "severity":   {"type": "string", "enum": ["high", "medium", "low"]},
      "issue":      {"type": "string"},
      "suggestion": {"type": "string"}
    },
    "required": ["file", "severity", "issue"]
  }
}

    Then run Claude Code with schema enforcement (requires CLI installed):

    claude -p "Review lab-d1-agentic-arch/basic_loop.py for bugs and security issues.
Return ONLY a JSON array of findings. Empty array if no issues." \
  --output-format json \
  --json-schema lab-d3-claude-code/review-schema.json \
  < /dev/null

    The --json-schema flag validates that output conforms to the schema. Your CI pipeline can safely parse the result without try/except for malformed JSON. The schema is your contract between Claude Code and downstream tooling.

    Exam key: --output-format json alone = JSON output (best-effort). --output-format json --json-schema <file> = JSON output validated against schema.

    Quick Check — Module 3

    Q: Your codebase has test files spread throughout (Button.test.tsx next to Button.tsx). You want all tests to follow the same conventions. What's the most maintainable approach?

    A) Create .claude/rules/ files with glob patterns like paths: ["**/*.test.tsx"] B) Put test conventions in the root CLAUDE.md under a "Testing" header C) Create a .claude/skills/ test-generation skill D) Put a CLAUDE.md in every directory that has test files

    Answer

    A. .claude/rules/ with glob patterns applies conventions automatically based on file path, regardless of directory. Option D can't cover files scattered across many dirs. Option B relies on inference. Option C requires manual invocation. (Lab 3.3, Exam Sample Q6)

    Q: Your CI pipeline runs claude "Review this PR" but hangs indefinitely. What's the fix?

    A) Use claude -p "Review this PR" B) Set CLAUDE_HEADLESS=true C) Redirect stdin with < /dev/null D) Add --batch flag

    Answer

    A. The -p flag runs Claude Code in non-interactive mode. B, C, D reference non-existent features. (Lab 3.6, Exam Sample Q10)

    MODULE 4: PROMPT ENGINEERING & STRUCTURED OUTPUT

    Domain 4 — 20% of exam Task Statements 4.1 – 4.6

    Key Terms for Module 4

    • False positive: A finding the model reports that isn't actually an issue. High false positive rates erode developer trust in ALL findings.
    • Explicit criteria: Specific, categorical rules for what to report vs skip (e.g., "flag comments only when claimed behavior contradicts actual code"). Beats vague instructions like "be conservative."
    • Few-shot prompting: Providing 2-4 concrete input/output examples in the prompt. The most effective technique for achieving consistent, formatted output.
    • tool_use + JSON schema: When Claude calls a tool, it MUST conform to the tool's JSON schema. This eliminates JSON syntax errors but NOT semantic errors (wrong values).
    • Nullable fields: Schema fields typed as ["string", "null"]. Tells the model "it's OK if this data is missing — return null instead of guessing."
    • Enum with "other" + detail: Schema pattern for extensible categories. "enum": ["invoice", "receipt", "other"] with an adjacent detail field for unexpected types.
    • tool_choice: Controls tool calling behavior. "auto" = model decides. "any" = must call a tool. {"type": "tool", "name": "X"} = must call tool X.
    • Retry-with-feedback: When extraction fails validation, send a follow-up with: original document + failed extraction + specific errors. Claude can self-correct.
    • detected_pattern: Field added to findings that records which code construct triggered it. Enables analysis of which patterns produce false positives.
    • Pydantic: Python library for schema validation. Catches type/schema errors (wrong types, missing fields). Does NOT catch semantic errors (values don't sum correctly).
    • Message Batches API: Anthropic API for batch processing. 50% cost savings, up to 24-hour processing window, no latency SLA guarantee. Does NOT support multi-turn tool calling.
    • custom_id: Your identifier in batch requests to correlate requests with responses.
    • Self-review limitation: When the same Claude session generates and reviews code, it retains reasoning context — making it less likely to question its own decisions.
    • Independent review instance: A fresh API call with no prior context. More effective at catching issues than self-review.
    • Multi-pass review: Splitting reviews into per-file local analysis + separate cross-file integration pass. Prevents attention dilution.
    • Confidence score: Model-reported certainty (0.0-1.0) alongside each finding. Must be calibrated against labeled validation data before trusting.

    LAB 4.1: EXPLICIT CRITERIA TO REDUCE FALSE POSITIVES

    Domain 4 — Task Statement 4.1 Specific criteria > vague instructions, false positive impact ~20 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What flag makes Claude Code run non-interactively in CI? (Lab 3.6)
    2. Why do independent review instances beat self-review? (Lab 3.6)

    Why This Matters

    You tell Claude "review this code and be conservative." It flags 47 issues — 30 are false positives. Developers ignore ALL findings, including the 17 real bugs. The problem: "be conservative" means different things each run. The fix: explicit categorical criteria ("flag SQL injection and hardcoded secrets; skip style issues and naming conventions").

    Exam connection (Sample Q3): Explicit escalation criteria + few-shot examples is the correct first step for improving calibration — not sentiment analysis, not confidence scores.

    What You'll Learn

    "Be conservative" doesn't reduce false positives. Specific categorical criteria does. High false positive rates undermine trust in accurate categories.

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > Here are 4 code snippets. Review them for issues using TWO different approaches:
> First, use a VAGUE instruction: "Review this code and be conservative — only flag high-confidence issues."
> Second, use SPECIFIC criteria: "Flag ONLY SQL injection and hardcoded credentials. DO NOT flag style issues, TODO comments, or comments that are slightly imprecise but not contradicting actual behavior."
>
> Snippet 1: def get_user(id): return db.query(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE id = {id}")
> Snippet 2: # TODO: refactor this later
>            def process(x): result = x * 2; return result
> Snippet 3: def get_order(order_id: str) -> dict:
>                '''Retrieves order from local cache.'''
>                return cache.get(order_id)
> Snippet 4: def verify_signature(data, sig):
>                # Checks that signature is valid
>                return hmac.compare_digest(expected_sig(data), sig)
>
> Compare the results. Which approach produces fewer false positives?

    Watch how the vague prompt flags safe code (TODO comments, accurate docstrings) as issues, while the specific criteria correctly identifies only the SQL injection in Snippet 1.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d4-prompt-eng/explicit_criteria.py

    First, we'll create code samples that are a mix of real issues and false-positive-prone patterns. The key: only 2 of 4 samples have real issues. A good prompt should flag those 2 and skip the rest.

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# Test code samples — mix of real issues and false-positive-prone patterns
CODE_SAMPLES = [
    {
        "id": "sample1",
        "code": """
def get_user(id):
    # Returns user by ID
    return db.query(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE id = {id}")
""",
        "real_issue": True,
        "issue_type": "SQL injection"
    },
    {
        "id": "sample2",
        "code": """
# TODO: refactor this later
def process(x):
    result = x * 2  # multiply by 2
    return result
""",
        "real_issue": False,
        "issue_type": "Style comment (not a bug)"
    },
    {
        "id": "sample3",
        "code": """
def get_order(order_id: str) -> dict:
    '''Retrieves order from local cache.'''
    return cache.get(order_id)
""",
        "real_issue": False,
        "issue_type": "Accurate docstring (matches behavior)"
    },
    {
        "id": "sample4",
        "code": """
def verify_signature(data, sig):
    # Checks that signature is valid
    return hmac.compare_digest(expected_sig(data), sig)
""",
        "real_issue": False,
        "issue_type": "Comment is accurate"
    },
]

    Reference Implementation: two prompts — vague vs specific

    Now the experiment: we'll test the SAME code samples with two different prompts. The vague prompt says "be conservative." The specific prompt defines EXACTLY what to report.

    # ── VAGUE REVIEW PROMPT (produces false positives) ────────
vague_system = """You are a code reviewer.
Review the code and flag any issues you find.
Be conservative — only report high-confidence findings."""

# ── SPECIFIC CRITERIA PROMPT (precise) ────────────────────
specific_system = """You are a code reviewer.

REPORT these issue types:
- Bugs: code that will throw exceptions or produce wrong results
- Security: SQL injection, hardcoded credentials, missing auth checks
- API misuse: wrong parameters, deprecated methods

DO NOT REPORT these:
- Comments that are slightly imprecise but not misleading
- Style preferences (variable naming, formatting)
- TODO comments
- Code patterns that work correctly even if non-idiomatic

For comments: ONLY flag when the comment claims behavior that CONTRADICTS
what the code actually does. An incomplete comment is NOT a flag.

Return JSON: [{"issue": "...", "severity": "high/medium/low", "category": "bug/security/api_misuse"}]
If no issues found, return: []"""

def review_code(system_prompt, code_sample):
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=512,
        system=system_prompt,
        messages=[{"role": "user",
                   "content": f"Review this code:\n```python{code_sample['code']}```"}]
    )
    text = response.content[0].text.strip()
    # Try to parse JSON from response
    try:
        start = text.find("[")
        end = text.rfind("]") + 1
        if start >= 0 and end > start:
            return json.loads(text[start:end])
    except:
        pass
    return text[:100]

    Reference Implementation: run the comparison and observe

    This is where you see the difference. The vague prompt will flag accurate comments as issues. The specific prompt will correctly skip them and only flag the real bugs.

    if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("=== False Positive Comparison ===\n")

    for sample in CODE_SAMPLES:
        print(f"Sample {sample['id']} (real issue: {sample['real_issue']}, type: {sample['issue_type']})")
        print(f"Code: {sample['code'].strip()[:80]}...")

        vague_result   = review_code(vague_system, sample)
        specific_result = review_code(specific_system, sample)

        print(f"  VAGUE criteria:    {vague_result}")
        print(f"  SPECIFIC criteria: {specific_result}")
        print()

    print("\n=== Key Insights ===")
    print("""
    1. "Be conservative" doesn't help — it's still vague.
       The model doesn't know WHAT to be conservative about.

    2. Specific categories fix it:
       "Flag comments ONLY when claimed behavior CONTRADICTS actual code"
       Not: "flag when comments might be inaccurate"

    3. False positive categories hurt trust:
       If style issues are flagged alongside real bugs,
       developers start ignoring ALL findings — including real bugs.

    4. Fix high-FP categories by:
       a. Temporarily DISABLE the category (restore trust)
       b. Improve the criterion with specific examples
       c. Re-enable with better criteria
    """)

    # === Category disable/enable pattern ===
    print("=== Temporarily Disabling High-FP Categories ===\n")

    # Track which categories produce too many false positives
    DISABLED_CATEGORIES = ["comment_accuracy", "naming_convention"]  # high FP in production
    ENABLED_CATEGORIES = ["security_vulnerability", "logic_bug", "error_handling"]

    def build_criteria_with_category_control(disabled: list, enabled: list) -> str:
        """Build review criteria that only include enabled categories."""
        criteria = "REPORT these issue types:\n"
        for cat in enabled:
            criteria += f"- {cat.replace('_', ' ').title()}: flag when found\n"

        if disabled:
            criteria += "\nDO NOT REPORT these types (temporarily disabled for improvement):\n"
            for cat in disabled:
                criteria += f"- {cat.replace('_', ' ').title()}: SKIP — do not flag\n"

        return criteria

    criteria = build_criteria_with_category_control(DISABLED_CATEGORIES, ENABLED_CATEGORIES)
    print(criteria)
    print("Why this works:")
    print("  1. Developers stop seeing false positives → trust rebuilds")
    print("  2. You improve the disabled category criteria offline")
    print("  3. Re-enable with better criteria → precision is higher")
    print("  4. Trust is restored instead of eroded by noise")

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d4-prompt-eng/explicit_criteria.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • Sample 1 (SQL injection): Both prompts flag it — it's a real bug
    • Sample 2 (style comment): VAGUE flags it as inaccurate → false positive. SPECIFIC correctly skips it
    • Sample 3 (accurate docstring): VAGUE may flag it → false positive. SPECIFIC correctly skips it
    • Sample 4 (accurate comment): Same pattern — SPECIFIC is more precise

    The key insight: "be conservative" is vague. "Flag ONLY when comment contradicts actual code behavior" is specific.

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens if you replace specific criteria with "be conservative"? Run the same code 5 times. Is the output consistent? (Answer: no — "conservative" means different things each run)
    2. What happens if you keep a high-false-positive category active (e.g., "comment accuracy") while it has a 60% FP rate? Do developers trust the other categories? (Answer: no — high FP in one category undermines trust in ALL categories)
    3. Why doesn't confidence-based filtering ("only report high-confidence findings") work as a replacement for explicit criteria? (Answer: the model is often confidently wrong — confidence alone doesn't reduce false positives)

    Interim Trust Restoration Strategy

    Exam-tested pattern: What do you do when one review category has an 80% false positive rate?

    The trap: you might think "add confidence scores" or "improve the prompt for that category." But here's the problem — while category X is producing false positives, developers stop reading ALL findings, including accurate ones from other categories.

    The correct fix (two-phase):

    1. Phase 1 — Disable the high-FP category entirely. Developers immediately see that remaining findings are trustworthy. Trust is restored. They start reading and acting on feedback again.

    2. Phase 2 — Fix category X's criteria separately. Test on a sample set. When FP rate drops below your threshold, re-enable it.

    Why this beats other approaches:

    • Confidence scores don't help — the model is often confidently wrong
    • "Improve the prompt" takes time — meanwhile developers are ignoring everything
    • Disabling is fast and immediately restores trust in the remaining categories

    Code Analysis: Why is disabling a high-FP category better than adding confidence scores? Because the model's confidence doesn't correlate with accuracy for false positives — it's often confident about wrong findings. Disabling is a deterministic fix; confidence filtering is probabilistic.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "developers ignore all review findings" → high-FP category undermining trust
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add confidence scores" — model is confidently wrong on false positives
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add sentiment analysis" — sentiment doesn't correlate with finding accuracy
    • The correct pattern: Disable high-FP category → restore trust → fix criteria → re-enable

    ✅ Lab 4.1 Checkpoint

    • Vague instructions ("be conservative") don't reduce false positives
    • Specific categorical criteria: define WHAT to report vs skip
    • "Flag comments only when claimed behavior contradicts actual code"
    • High FP categories undermine trust in all categories → disable to restore trust
    • Interim strategy: disable high-FP category → restore trust → fix prompts → re-enable
    • Explicit severity criteria with code examples → consistent classification

    LAB 4.2: FEW-SHOT PROMPTING

    Domain 4 — Task Statement 4.2 Examples for consistency, ambiguous case handling ~20 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. Why doesn't "be conservative" reduce false positives? (Lab 4.1)
    2. What is the interim trust restoration strategy for high-FP categories? (Lab 4.1)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: You tell Claude "review this code for security issues." It returns findings in different formats every time — sometimes JSON, sometimes bullets, sometimes prose. Your automation script breaks because it can't parse inconsistent output.

    Worse: Claude flags logging.info(user_email) as a "data exposure" issue. Is it? Depends on context. Without guidance on ambiguous cases, Claude either over-flags (floods developers with false positives) or under-flags (misses real issues).

    The solution: few-shot examples. Show Claude 2-4 examples of the EXACT output format you want, including at least one ambiguous case with the correct decision. Claude generalizes the pattern to new inputs without needing hundreds of examples.

    The key insight: Few-shot fixes FORMAT consistency and AMBIGUOUS CASE handling. It does NOT fix the underlying problem if your criteria are vague — that's Lab 4.1's job (explicit criteria).

    Exam connection (Sample Q3): Explicit criteria + few-shot examples = the correct first step for improving escalation calibration.

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > Review this code for security issues. Return findings in JSON format with
> fields: file, line, severity, issue, suggestion.
>
> def login(user, pw): return db.query(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE user='{user}'")
> x = value or 0  # default to zero
> def get_count(): return len(items)  # Returns number of active users

    Run it twice. Notice the output format changes each time? Now try again with few-shot examples prepended:

    > Review code using this EXACT format. Here are examples:
> Example 1 input: def get_user(id): return db.execute(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE id={id}")
> Example 1 output: {"file": "api.py", "line": 1, "severity": "high", "issue": "SQL injection", "suggestion": "Use parameterized query"}
> Example 2 input: result = value or default_value  # use default if None
> Example 2 output: []  (No issues — valid Python pattern, accurate comment)
>
> Now review: def login(user, pw): return db.query(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE user='{user}'")

    Compare the consistency. The few-shot version produces structured, predictable output every time.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d4-prompt-eng/few_shot.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── WITHOUT FEW-SHOT (inconsistent output format) ─────────
no_shot_system = """You are a code review assistant.
Review code and report findings with location, issue, severity, and suggested fix."""

# ── WITH FEW-SHOT (consistent, actionable format) ──────────
few_shot_system = """You are a code review assistant.

Here are examples of the EXACT output format required:

Example 1:
Code: `def get_user(id): return db.execute(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE id={id}")`
Output: {"file": "api.py", "line": 1, "severity": "high",
         "issue": "SQL injection: user input interpolated directly into query",
         "suggestion": "Use parameterized query: db.execute('SELECT * FROM users WHERE id=?', (id,))"}

Example 2:
Code: `# Returns user by ID\ndef get_user(id): return cache[id]`
Output: {"file": "api.py", "line": 1, "severity": "low",
         "issue": "Comment mentions 'user' but function returns cache entry (may be stale)",
         "suggestion": "Update comment: '# Returns user from cache by ID'"}

Example 3 (ambiguous — local pattern, not a real bug):
Code: `result = value or default_value  # use default if None`
Output: []  (No issues — this is a valid Python pattern, comment is accurate)

Why Example 3 returns empty: The `or` pattern is idiomatic Python.
The comment accurately describes the behavior. This is NOT a false positive.

Now review the provided code using this exact format."""

def review_code(system, code, label):
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=512,
        system=system,
        messages=[{"role": "user",
                   "content": f"Review:\n```python\n{code}\n```"}]
    )
    print(f"\n[{label}]")
    print(response.content[0].text[:300])

if __name__ == "__main__":
    test_cases = [
        'def login(user, pw): return db.query(f"SELECT * FROM users WHERE user=\'{user}\'")',
        'x = value or 0  # default to zero',
        'def get_count(): return len(items)  # Returns number of active users',
    ]

    for code in test_cases:
        print(f"\nCode: {code[:70]}...")
        review_code(no_shot_system, code, "NO FEW-SHOT (inconsistent)")
        review_code(few_shot_system, code, "FEW-SHOT (consistent)")

    print("\n\n=== Few-Shot Design Principles ===")
    print("""
    2-4 targeted examples:
    - Show the DESIRED output format exactly
    - Include ambiguous cases with explanation of why they go each way
    - Include at least one example of "no finding" (not every code has bugs)
    - Show reasoning for ambiguous decisions (why example 3 returns empty)

    Where few-shot works best:
    - Ambiguous tool selection (show: for X type of request, choose tool Y)
    - Varied document structures (show: inline citations vs bibliography extraction)
    - Reducing hallucination (show: when information is absent, return null not a guess)
    - Output format consistency (show the exact JSON structure required)

    Few-shot generalizes:
    The model applies the PATTERN to novel cases — it doesn't just match examples.
    3 good examples → consistent handling of 100+ novel patterns.
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d4-prompt-eng/few_shot.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • For each of 3 code snippets (SQL injection, or pattern, misleading comment), both NO FEW-SHOT and FEW-SHOT reviews are printed side by side
    • The NO FEW-SHOT reviews produce inconsistent formats and may over-flag safe code, while the FEW-SHOT reviews use the exact [severity] description (evidence) format from the examples
    • The few-shot reviewer correctly returns empty findings for the safe or pattern (Example 3 calibration), while the no-shot reviewer may flag it unnecessarily
    • A "Few-Shot Design Principles" summary explaining why 2-4 targeted examples beat verbose instructions

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens if you remove Example 3 (the ambiguous case) from the few-shot prompt? Does Claude now over-flag or under-flag ambiguous code? (Answer: yes — without ambiguous case guidance, Claude defaults to inconsistent decisions)
    2. Why do 10+ examples perform worse than 2-4? (Answer: diminishing returns + token waste. 2-4 targeted examples outperform large volumes because the model generalizes from the pattern, not from volume)
    3. Can few-shot examples fix decision quality if the criteria are vague (e.g., "be conservative")? (Answer: format improves but decision quality doesn't — few-shot fixes format, not judgment. You need explicit criteria from Lab 4.1 for that)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "inconsistent output format" or "wrong handling of ambiguous cases" → few-shot examples
    • Wrong answer trap: "More detailed instructions" — prose is interpreted differently each run
    • The correct pattern: 2-4 targeted examples including an ambiguous case with reasoning

    ✅ Lab 4.2 Checkpoint

    • 2-4 targeted examples beat detailed instructions for consistency
    • Include examples of ambiguous cases with decision rationale
    • Include at least one "no finding" example (prevent over-flagging)
    • Few-shot examples enable generalization to novel patterns
    • Show exact output format — don't describe it, demonstrate it

    LAB 4.3: STRUCTURED OUTPUT WITH TOOL USE AND JSON SCHEMAS

    Domain 4 — Task Statement 4.3 tool_use for guaranteed schema compliance, nullable fields, enums ~25 minutesAdvanced | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What do few-shot examples fix that descriptions alone can't? (Lab 4.2)
    2. How many examples are typically needed? (Lab 4.2)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: You ask Claude to extract invoice data and return JSON. Sometimes it wraps the JSON in markdown code fences. Sometimes it adds a chatty preamble. Sometimes the JSON has trailing commas. Your parser crashes on all three.

    The solution: tool_use with JSON schema. When you define a tool with an input_schema, Claude MUST return data matching that schema. No code fences, no preamble, no syntax errors. The schema is enforced by the API, not by Claude's good behavior.

    But here's the catch: Schema compliance eliminates SYNTAX errors (missing fields, wrong types). It does NOT eliminate SEMANTIC errors (fabricated vendor IDs, line items that don't sum to total). For semantic validation, you need custom code — that's Lab 4.4.

    Two critical schema patterns:

    • Nullable fields ("type": ["string", "null"]): Use when the source document might not contain the information. Claude returns null instead of making something up.
    • Enum with "other" + detail: "category": {"enum": ["electronics", "clothing", "other"]} plus "category_detail": {"type": "string"}. Handles unexpected values without forcing a wrong choice.

    Exam connection (Exercise 3): "Process documents where some fields are absent and verify the model returns null rather than fabricating values."

    Challenge Mode (optional — try before looking at the solution)

    Design a tool_use extraction schema for employee records with these requirements:

    1. Required fields: employee_id (string), name (string), department (enum: engineering, sales, marketing, other)
    2. Optional fields: salary (nullable number — some records don't include it), start_date (nullable string)
    3. A department_detail field for when department is "other"
    4. Use tool_choice to force the extraction tool

    Write the tool definition and API call. Does the model return null for missing fields or fabricate values? Then compare to the reference below.

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > I have this invoice text. Extract the data into structured JSON:
>
> INVOICE #INV-2024-042
> Vendor: Acme Supply Co.
> Date: March 15, 2024
> Line Items:
>   Office Supplies: $250.00
>   Printer Paper: $89.99
>   Ink Cartridges: $145.00
> TOTAL DUE: $484.99
> Status: UNPAID
>
> Now try a second invoice that has MISSING fields:
> INVOICE
> Vendor: Unknown Supplier
> Various items provided as discussed. Please remit payment.
>
> For the second invoice, which fields would you return as null vs fabricate?
> Explain why nullable schema fields ("type": ["string", "null"]) prevent hallucination.

    Notice how Claude handles the incomplete invoice. Without nullable fields in a schema, the model would be forced to invent values. With them, it can honestly return null.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d4-prompt-eng/structured_output.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── EXTRACTION TOOL DEFINITION ────────────────────────────
# Tool use + JSON schema = guaranteed schema compliance
# (Eliminates JSON syntax errors — model MUST conform to schema)

extraction_tool = {
    "name": "extract_invoice",
    "description": "Extracts structured data from an invoice document.",
    "input_schema": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
            "invoice_number": {
                "type": "string",
                "description": "Invoice ID or number"
            },
            "vendor_name": {
                "type": "string",
                "description": "Name of the vendor/supplier"
            },
            "invoice_date": {
                "type": ["string", "null"],
                "description": "Invoice date in ISO format (YYYY-MM-DD) or null if not found"
            },
            "total_amount": {
                "type": ["number", "null"],
                "description": "Total amount due. null if not present in document."
            },
            "line_items": {
                "type": "array",
                "items": {
                    "type": "object",
                    "properties": {
                        "description": {"type": "string"},
                        "amount": {"type": "number"}
                    },
                    "required": ["description", "amount"]
                }
            },
            "payment_status": {
                "type": "string",
                "enum": ["paid", "unpaid", "partial", "overdue", "unclear", "other"],
                "description": "Payment status. Use 'unclear' if document is ambiguous."
            },
            "payment_status_detail": {
                "type": ["string", "null"],
                "description": "Required when payment_status is 'other'. Details."
            },
            "calculated_total": {
                "type": ["number", "null"],
                "description": "Sum of line item amounts (for validation). null if no line items."
            },
            "conflict_detected": {
                "type": "boolean",
                "description": "True if stated total doesn't match sum of line items."
            }
        },
        "required": [
            "invoice_number", "vendor_name", "payment_status",
            "conflict_detected", "line_items"
        ]
    }
}

def extract_invoice(document_text: str) -> dict:
    """Extract invoice data using tool_use for guaranteed schema compliance."""
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=1024,
        tools=[extraction_tool],
        # Forced tool selection: MUST call extract_invoice
        tool_choice={"type": "tool", "name": "extract_invoice"},
        messages=[{
            "role": "user",
            "content": (
                "Extract all invoice data from this document.\n"
                "Format rules:\n"
                "- Dates: ISO format (YYYY-MM-DD)\n"
                "- Amounts: numbers without currency symbols\n"
                "- Missing fields: null (never fabricate values)\n"
                "- Calculate sum of line items for conflict detection\n\n"
                f"Document:\n{document_text}"
            )
        }]
    )

    for block in response.content:
        if block.type == "tool_use" and block.name == "extract_invoice":
            return block.input

    return None

# Test documents
GOOD_INVOICE = """
INVOICE #INV-2024-042
Vendor: Acme Supply Co.
Date: March 15, 2024

Line Items:
  Office Supplies: $250.00
  Printer Paper:   $89.99
  Ink Cartridges:  $145.00

TOTAL DUE: $484.99
Status: UNPAID
"""

CONFLICTING_INVOICE = """
INVOICE #INV-2024-099
Vendor: Widget Corp

Items:
  Widget A: $100.00
  Widget B: $200.00
  Widget C: $150.00

Total Due: $500.00  (note: line items sum to $450, not $500)
"""

INCOMPLETE_INVOICE = """
INVOICE
Vendor: Unknown Supplier
Various items provided as discussed.
Please remit payment.
"""

def validate_extraction(data: dict) -> list:
    """Validate semantic correctness (schema compliance is guaranteed by tool_use)."""
    errors = []

    if data.get("conflict_detected") and data.get("calculated_total") and data.get("total_amount"):
        calc = data["calculated_total"]
        stated = data["total_amount"]
        if abs(calc - stated) > 0.01:
            errors.append(f"Total mismatch: line items sum to ${calc:.2f} but stated total is ${stated:.2f}")

    if data.get("payment_status") == "other" and not data.get("payment_status_detail"):
        errors.append("payment_status is 'other' but payment_status_detail is missing")

    return errors

if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("=== Structured Extraction with Tool Use ===\n")

    for label, doc in [("Good invoice", GOOD_INVOICE),
                        ("Conflicting totals", CONFLICTING_INVOICE),
                        ("Incomplete invoice", INCOMPLETE_INVOICE)]:
        print(f"\n--- {label} ---")
        result = extract_invoice(doc)
        print(json.dumps(result, indent=2))

        errors = validate_extraction(result or {})
        if errors:
            print(f"Semantic validation errors: {errors}")
        else:
            print("Semantic validation: PASS")

    print("\n\n=== Key Schema Design Patterns ===")
    print("""
    Nullable fields: "type": ["string", "null"]
    → Use when source document may not contain the information
    → Prevents model from fabricating values to satisfy required fields

    Enum with "unclear" and "other":
    → "unclear": ambiguous in source document
    → "other" + detail field: extensible without schema changes

    Conflict detection: calculated_total + conflict_detected boolean
    → Schema compliance ≠ semantic correctness
    → Tool use eliminates syntax errors, not logic errors
    → Add cross-field validation for semantic checks

    tool_choice forced vs any:
    → forced: specific tool MUST run (single schema)
    → any: model picks which schema (multiple schemas for different doc types)
    """)

    # === Demo: tool_choice "any" with multiple schemas ===
    print("\n=== tool_choice 'any' — Multiple Schema Selection ===\n")

    receipt_tool = {
        "name": "extract_receipt",
        "description": "Extract data from retail receipts. Use for store purchases.",
        "input_schema": {
            "type": "object",
            "properties": {
                "store_name": {"type": "string"},
                "items": {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object",
                    "properties": {"name": {"type": "string"}, "price": {"type": "number"}}}},
                "tax": {"type": ["number", "null"]},
                "total": {"type": "number"}
            },
            "required": ["store_name", "items", "total"]
        }
    }

    # Use tool_choice "any" — model picks the right schema
    unknown_doc = "INVOICE #INV-2024-042\nVendor: Acme Supply\nItems: Paper $50\nTOTAL: $50.00"

    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=1024,
        tools=[extraction_tool, receipt_tool],  # Two schemas available
        tool_choice="any",  # Model MUST call a tool, picks which one
        messages=[{"role": "user", "content": f"Extract data from this document:\n\n{unknown_doc}"}]
    )

    for block in response.content:
        if block.type == "tool_use":
            print(f"  Model chose: {block.name}")
            print(f"  (Correctly selected invoice schema over receipt schema)")
            print(f"  Data: {json.dumps(block.input, indent=2)[:200]}")

    print("""
    When to use tool_choice "any":
    - Unknown document type → let model pick the right extraction schema
    - Multiple valid output formats → model selects the best match
    - Guarantees structured output (won't return text) but with flexibility
    """)

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d4-prompt-eng/structured_output.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • Good invoice: All fields extracted correctly, conflict_detected = false
    • Conflicting totals: Line items don't match stated total, conflict_detected = true
    • Incomplete invoice: Missing fields return null (not fabricated values)
    • Multi-schema: Model correctly picks extract_invoice over extract_receipt

    Code Analysis

    Study the schema and validation code, then answer:

    1. What does making vendor_name nullable ("type": ["string", "null"]) change? If you ran the conflicting invoice with this change, the schema validates fine — but the SEMANTIC error (line items don't sum to total) is still caught only by the validate_extraction() function.
    2. What is the key distinction between schema compliance and semantic correctness? (Answer: tool_use + JSON schema catches syntax errors — missing fields, wrong types, malformed JSON. Custom validation catches semantic errors — values that don't add up, fabricated data. You need both.)
    3. When would you use tool_choice: "any" vs forced tool selection? (Answer: "any" when you have multiple schemas and want the model to pick the right one based on document type. Forced when you know exactly which schema applies.)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "schema validates but values are wrong" → schema catches syntax, NOT semantics
    • If the exam says "model fabricates values for missing fields" → make fields nullable
    • Wrong answer trap: "Use stricter schema" — stricter syntax doesn't fix semantic errors
    • The correct pattern: tool_use + nullable fields + custom semantic validation

    ✅ Lab 4.3 Checkpoint

    • tool_use + JSON schema = guaranteed schema syntax compliance
    • Schema compliance does NOT prevent semantic errors (wrong field values)
    • Nullable fields: ["type", "null"] for potentially absent data
    • "unclear" enum value for ambiguous cases
    • "other" + detail field for extensible categorization
    • Add cross-field validation for semantic correctness checks
    • tool_choice "any" with multiple schemas: model picks the right extraction tool

    LAB 4.4: VALIDATION AND RETRY LOOPS

    Domain 4 — Task Statement 4.4 Retry with specific feedback, when retries won't help ~20 minutesAdvanced | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What does JSON schema enforce — syntax or semantics? (Lab 4.3)
    2. Why should fields be nullable when source data might be missing? (Lab 4.3)

    Why This Matters

    Your extraction pipeline uses tool_use with a JSON schema — no syntax errors. But line items sum to $300 while the stated total is $350. And for documents that don't mention a vendor ID, Claude fabricates one. Schema compliance ≠ correctness. You need custom validation + nullable fields.

    Exam connection (Exercise 3): "Implement a validation-retry loop: when validation fails, send a follow-up including the document, failed extraction, and specific validation error."

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > Extract invoice data from this document:
>
> INVOICE #INV-999
> Vendor: Widget Co
> Items:
>   Widget A: $100.00
>   Widget B: $200.00
> Total: $350.00
>
> Notice the line items sum to $300 but the stated total is $350.
> Walk me through: if a validation check caught this mismatch,
> how would you construct a retry prompt that includes the original
> document, the failed extraction, and the SPECIFIC error
> ("line items sum to $300 but total is $350")?
> Why is specific error feedback more effective than just saying "try again"?

    This demonstrates the core pattern: retry-with-specific-feedback enables targeted correction, while vague retries produce random changes.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d4-prompt-eng/retry_loop.py

    First, we define the tool schema and a validator that catches semantic errors (things JSON schema alone can't catch — like line items not summing to total).

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

extraction_tool = {
    "name": "extract_invoice",
    "description": "Extract invoice data",
    "input_schema": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
            "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
            "vendor_name": {"type": "string"},
            "total_amount": {"type": ["number", "null"]},
            "line_items": {
                "type": "array",
                "items": {"type": "object",
                          "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"},
                                         "amount": {"type": "number"}},
                          "required": ["description", "amount"]}
            },
            "calculated_total": {"type": ["number", "null"]},
            "conflict_detected": {"type": "boolean"}
        },
        "required": ["invoice_number", "vendor_name", "line_items", "conflict_detected"]
    }
}

def validate_extraction(data: dict) -> list:
    """Returns list of validation error messages."""
    errors = []

    if not data.get("invoice_number"):
        errors.append("invoice_number is empty")

    items = data.get("line_items", [])
    if items:
        calc = sum(item.get("amount", 0) for item in items)
        stated = data.get("total_amount")
        if stated and abs(calc - stated) > 0.01:
            errors.append(
                f"Line items sum to ${calc:.2f} but total_amount is ${stated:.2f}. "
                f"Set calculated_total={calc:.2f} and conflict_detected=true."
            )

    return errors

    Reference Implementation: the retry-with-feedback loop

    This is the core pattern: when validation fails, send a follow-up that includes the original document + the failed extraction + SPECIFIC error messages. Claude can self-correct because it knows exactly what went wrong.

    def extract_with_retry(doc_text: str, max_retries: int = 2) -> dict:
    """
    Extract invoice data with retry-on-validation-failure.

    KEY PATTERN: Each retry includes the SPECIFIC validation errors.
    "Your previous extraction had these errors: [specific errors]"
    This is far more effective than vague retries.
    """
    messages = [{"role": "user",
                 "content": f"Extract invoice data from:\n\n{doc_text}"}]

    for attempt in range(max_retries + 1):
        print(f"\n  Attempt {attempt + 1}/{max_retries + 1}...")

        response = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
            max_tokens=1024,
            tools=[extraction_tool],
            tool_choice={"type": "tool", "name": "extract_invoice"},
            messages=messages
        )

        data = None
        for block in response.content:
            if block.type == "tool_use":
                data = block.input

        if not data:
            print("  No extraction returned")
            continue

        errors = validate_extraction(data)

        if not errors:
            print(f"  ✓ Valid on attempt {attempt + 1}")
            return data

        print(f"  ✗ Errors: {errors}")

        if attempt < max_retries:
            # Retry with SPECIFIC error feedback
            error_list = "\n".join(f"- {e}" for e in errors)
            messages = [{
                "role": "user",
                "content": (
                    f"Extract invoice data from:\n\n{doc_text}\n\n"
                    f"Your previous extraction had these specific errors:\n{error_list}\n\n"
                    f"Re-examine the document carefully and correct these issues."
                )
            }]

    print(f"  Returning best attempt after {max_retries + 1} tries")
    return data

    Reference Implementation: test cases where retries help vs don't help

    Two test cases demonstrate the critical distinction:

    • Conflicting totals → retry CAN fix it (information is there, just miscalculated)
    • Missing data → retry CAN'T fix it (information isn't in the document at all)
    # Test cases
CONFLICTING_DOC = """
INVOICE #INV-999
Vendor: Widget Co
Items:
  Widget A: $100.00
  Widget B: $200.00
Total: $350.00
"""

MISSING_DATA_DOC = """
INVOICE #INV-888
Vendor: Supply Plus
Contact billing@supplyplus.com for pricing details.
"""

if __name__ == "__main__":
    print("=== Test 1: Conflicting totals (retry should fix it) ===")
    result = extract_with_retry(CONFLICTING_DOC)
    print(f"\nFinal: conflict_detected={result.get('conflict_detected')}, "
          f"calculated_total={result.get('calculated_total')}")

    print("\n\n=== Test 2: Missing data (retry won't help) ===")
    result = extract_with_retry(MISSING_DATA_DOC)
    print(f"\nFinal: total_amount={result.get('total_amount')} (should be null — data isn't in doc)")

    print("\n\n=== When Retries Help vs Don't Help ===")
    print("""
    Retries WILL help:
    - Format errors (date in wrong format → specify the correct format)
    - Arithmetic errors (calculated_total doesn't match sum → point out the math)
    - Missing cross-field validation (conflict_detected should be true → explain why)
    - Structural errors (line items in wrong format → show correct structure)

    Retries WON'T help:
    - Information not in the document ("contact billing for pricing")
      → The data literally doesn't exist in the source. Retrying doesn't add new information.
    - Information in an external document that wasn't provided
    - Genuinely ambiguous source data

    KEY EXAM CONCEPT: Identify WHETHER information is absent vs structurally wrong.
    Absent = null is correct, stop retrying.
    Wrong format = retry with specific correction feedback.
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d4-prompt-eng/retry_loop.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • Test 1 (conflicting totals): Attempt 1 may miss the conflict → validation error → Attempt 2 corrects with conflict_detected=true
    • Test 2 (missing data): All attempts return total_amount=null because the pricing data genuinely isn't in the document. Retries don't help here.

    Reference Implementation: the detected_pattern field for false positive analysis

    When Claude reviews code and developers dismiss findings, you need to know WHY. The detected_pattern field lets you systematically analyze dismissal patterns.

    # Code review finding WITH detected_pattern field
review_finding_schema = {
    "name": "report_finding",
    "description": "Report a code review finding",
    "input_schema": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
            "file":             {"type": "string"},
            "line":             {"type": "integer"},
            "severity":         {"type": "string", "enum": ["high", "medium", "low"]},
            "issue":            {"type": "string"},
            "suggestion":       {"type": "string"},
            "detected_pattern": {
                "type": "string",
                "description": (
                    "The specific code construct that triggered this finding. "
                    "Examples: 'f-string in SQL query', 'except: pass', "
                    "'hardcoded string comparison'. "
                    "Used for false positive analysis when developers dismiss findings."
                )
            }
        },
        "required": ["file", "severity", "issue", "detected_pattern"]
    }
}

# How to use: when developers dismiss a finding, log it with detected_pattern.
# After 100 reviews, analyze: which detected_patterns have high dismissal rates?
# High dismissal rate → that pattern is generating false positives → refine the criteria.

dismissed_findings = [
    {"detected_pattern": "== None comparison",  "dismissed": True},
    {"detected_pattern": "f-string in SQL",     "dismissed": False},
    {"detected_pattern": "except: pass",        "dismissed": True},
    {"detected_pattern": "f-string in SQL",     "dismissed": False},
    {"detected_pattern": "== None comparison",  "dismissed": True},
]

from collections import Counter, defaultdict

def analyze_dismissal_patterns(findings: list) -> None:
    """Find which patterns have high dismissal rates → false positive candidates."""
    by_pattern = defaultdict(lambda: {"dismissed": 0, "total": 0})
    for f in findings:
        p = f["detected_pattern"]
        by_pattern[p]["total"] += 1
        if f["dismissed"]:
            by_pattern[p]["dismissed"] += 1

    print("Pattern Dismissal Rates:")
    for pattern, counts in sorted(by_pattern.items()):
        rate = counts["dismissed"] / counts["total"] * 100
        flag = " ← HIGH FALSE POSITIVE RATE" if rate >= 50 else ""
        print(f"  '{pattern}': {rate:.0f}% dismissed ({counts['dismissed']}/{counts['total']}){flag}")

analyze_dismissal_patterns(dismissed_findings)

    Run it. High-dismissal patterns → those are your false positive sources → tighten criteria for them.

    Reference Implementation: Pydantic for validation (exam-tested concept)

    The exam appendix and Exercise 3 reference Pydantic as a tested concept. Here's how Pydantic replaces manual validation in extraction pipelines:

    # Install: pip install pydantic
from pydantic import BaseModel, validator, ValidationError
from typing import Optional, List
import json

class LineItem(BaseModel):
    description: str
    amount: float

class InvoiceExtraction(BaseModel):
    invoice_number: str
    vendor_name: str
    total_amount: Optional[float] = None
    line_items: List[LineItem] = []
    conflict_detected: bool = False

    @validator("total_amount")
    def total_must_be_positive(cls, v):
        if v is not None and v <= 0:
            raise ValueError("total_amount must be positive")
        return v

def validate_with_pydantic(raw_data: dict) -> tuple[dict | None, list]:
    """
    Validate extraction using Pydantic.
    Returns (validated_data, errors).
    Pydantic catches: wrong types, missing required fields, constraint violations.
    """
    try:
        validated = InvoiceExtraction(**raw_data)
        return validated.dict(), []
    except ValidationError as e:
        errors = [f"{err['loc'][0]}: {err['msg']}" for err in e.errors()]
        return None, errors

# Test cases
test_cases = [
    {"invoice_number": "INV-001", "vendor_name": "Acme", "total_amount": 500.0, "line_items": []},
    {"invoice_number": "INV-002", "vendor_name": "Acme", "total_amount": -100},  # Pydantic catches negative
    {"vendor_name": "Acme"},                                                       # Missing required field
    {"invoice_number": "INV-003", "vendor_name": "Acme",
     "total_amount": 300.0, "line_items": [{"description": "Widget", "amount": "not-a-number"}]},  # Type error
]

for data in test_cases:
    result, errors = validate_with_pydantic(data)
    if errors:
        print(f"VALIDATION ERRORS: {errors}")
        # → Retry with: original_doc + failed_extraction + specific_errors
    else:
        print(f"VALID: {result['invoice_number']}")

    Key distinction (frequently tested):

    • Pydantic catches schema/type errors (wrong types, missing fields)
    • Tool use with JSON schema also catches syntax errors (malformed JSON)
    • Neither catches semantic errors (line items don't sum to total)
    • Semantic errors need custom cross-field validation like conflict_detected

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens if you remove the validation function and rely only on JSON schema? (Answer: yes, the model still returns line items that don't sum to the total — schema catches syntax, not semantics)
    2. When does retrying NOT help? Consider a document that says "contact billing for pricing." Will retry 3 produce the missing data? (Answer: no — the data isn't there. Retries can't invent new information. Return null.)
    3. Why is specific error feedback critical in the retry message? Compare "try again" vs "line items sum to $300 but total_amount is $350. Set conflict_detected=true." (Answer: specific errors enable targeted correction; vague retries produce random changes)

    Validation Layers — What Each One Catches

    The exam tests your understanding of three distinct validation layers:

    Layer What it catches What it misses Example
    JSON Schema (tool_use) Syntax/structure errors Everything semantic Missing required field, wrong type, invalid enum value
    Pydantic Type constraints + basic rules Business logic String too long, number out of range, invalid date format
    Custom semantic validation Business logic + cross-field Nothing (you write the rules) Line items don't sum to total, fabricated vendor IDs, conflicting dates

    The exam trap: Candidates who think "I use JSON schema, so my output is correct" are wrong. Schema guarantees syntax (the JSON is well-formed and matches the structure). It does NOT guarantee semantics (the values are accurate, consistent, or non-fabricated).

    Nullable fields prevent fabrication: When a field is "type": ["string", "null"], the model returns null for missing data instead of inventing a value to satisfy a required field.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "extraction fails, retry doesn't help" → information is absent from the source document
    • Wrong answer trap: "Retry with a different prompt" — can't extract what isn't there
    • The correct pattern: Retries fix format/structural errors. They can't fix missing information. Return null.

    ✅ Lab 4.4 Checkpoint

    • Retry includes SPECIFIC validation errors → Claude knows exactly what to fix
    • Retries help: format errors, arithmetic errors, structural issues
    • Retries don't help: information absent from source document
    • Include original document + failed extraction + specific errors in retry prompt
    • Three validation layers: JSON Schema (syntax) → Pydantic (types) → Custom (semantics)
    • detected_pattern field: track which code constructs trigger findings → analyze dismissal rates → identify false positive sources
    • Nullable fields prevent fabrication — model returns null instead of inventing data

    LAB 4.5: BATCH PROCESSING WITH MESSAGE BATCHES API

    Domain 4 — Task Statement 4.5 50% cost savings, 24hr window, custom_id, when not to use ~20 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When do retries help vs when are they futile? (Lab 4.4)
    2. What three layers of validation catch different types of errors? (Lab 4.4)

    Why This Matters

    The problem: Your team runs two Claude-powered workflows:

    1. A pre-merge security review — developers wait for it before merging
    2. An overnight tech debt report — reviewed the next morning

    Both cost $2,000/month in API calls. Your manager proposes switching both to the Message Batches API for 50% savings. Sounds great — $1,000/month saved. Should you do it?

    The answer: only for workflow #2. The Batch API processes requests within a 24-hour window with no guaranteed latency. Developers can't wait 24 hours for a pre-merge review. But an overnight report that nobody reads until 9am? Perfect batch candidate.

    The key facts:

    • 50% cost savings vs synchronous API
    • Up to 24-hour processing window (often faster, but no SLA)
    • No multi-turn tool calling (can't run agentic loops in batch)
    • custom_id correlates each request with its result (essential for failure handling)

    Exam connection (Sample Q11): This exact scenario is tested. The wrong answer is "batch both." The right answer is "batch the overnight report, keep synchronous for blocking checks."

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > I have two workflows that each cost $2,000/month in Claude API calls:
> 1. A pre-merge security review that blocks developer PRs until complete
> 2. An overnight tech debt report reviewed the next morning
>
> My manager wants to switch both to the Message Batches API for 50% savings.
> Evaluate this decision. Consider: the Batch API has up to 24-hour processing
> with no latency SLA, and does NOT support multi-turn tool calling.
> Which workflow(s) should use batch, and why?

    Claude will correctly identify that only workflow #2 (overnight report) is a batch candidate, because developers cannot wait up to 24 hours for a blocking pre-merge review.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d4-prompt-eng/batch_processing.py

    import anthropic
import json
import time

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── BATCH API BASICS ───────────────────────────────────────
# Message Batches API:
# - 50% cost savings vs synchronous API
# - Up to 24-hour processing window
# - No guaranteed latency SLA
# - Does NOT support multi-turn tool calling within a single request

def create_batch_requests(documents: list) -> list:
    """
    Build batch request list with custom_id for correlation.
    custom_id lets you match responses back to your input documents.
    """
    return [
        {
            "custom_id": f"doc-{doc['id']}",  # YOUR identifier — must be unique in batch
            "params": {
                "model": "claude-sonnet-4-6",
                "max_tokens": 512,
                "messages": [{
                    "role": "user",
                    "content": (
                        f"Extract the vendor name and total amount from this invoice.\n"
                        f"Return JSON: {{\"vendor\": \"...\", \"total\": N}}\n\n"
                        f"{doc['text']}"
                    )
                }]
            }
        }
        for doc in documents
    ]


def submit_batch(documents: list) -> str:
    """Submit a batch and return the batch ID."""
    requests = create_batch_requests(documents)

    print(f"Submitting batch of {len(requests)} requests...")
    batch = client.beta.messages.batches.create(requests=requests)

    print(f"Batch ID: {batch.id}")
    print(f"Status: {batch.processing_status}")
    return batch.id


def poll_batch(batch_id: str, max_wait_seconds: int = 300) -> dict:
    """
    Poll until batch completes.
    In production: use a webhook or scheduled check — don't poll in a tight loop.
    """
    print(f"\nPolling batch {batch_id}...")
    start = time.time()

    while time.time() - start < max_wait_seconds:
        batch = client.beta.messages.batches.retrieve(batch_id)
        status = batch.processing_status

        print(f"  Status: {status} | "
              f"Succeeded: {batch.request_counts.succeeded} | "
              f"Errored: {batch.request_counts.errored} | "
              f"Processing: {batch.request_counts.processing}")

        if status == "ended":
            return batch

        time.sleep(5)  # Wait 5 seconds before next check

    print("Timeout waiting for batch")
    return None


def process_batch_results(batch_id: str) -> dict:
    """
    Process results and correlate via custom_id.
    Handle failures: resubmit only failed documents.
    """
    results = {}
    failures = []

    for result in client.beta.messages.batches.results(batch_id):
        custom_id = result.custom_id

        if result.result.type == "succeeded":
            text = result.result.message.content[0].text
            try:
                data = json.loads(text[text.find("{"):text.rfind("}")+1])
                results[custom_id] = {"status": "success", "data": data}
            except:
                results[custom_id] = {"status": "parse_error", "raw": text[:100]}
                failures.append(custom_id)

        elif result.result.type == "errored":
            error = result.result.error
            results[custom_id] = {"status": "error", "error": str(error)}
            failures.append(custom_id)

    return results, failures


# ── DECISION GUIDE ─────────────────────────────────────────

def batch_decision_guide():
    print("""
=== Message Batches API Decision Guide ===

USE BATCH API for:
✓ Overnight reports (don't need results until morning)
✓ Weekly audits (process 1000 documents every Sunday night)
✓ Nightly test generation (non-blocking, results ready by morning)
✓ Historical data analysis (50% cost savings on large volumes)
✓ Bulk document classification (latency-tolerant)

DO NOT USE BATCH API for:
✗ Pre-merge CI checks (blocking workflow — developer waiting for result)
✗ Real-time customer interactions (need immediate response)
✗ Multi-turn tool calling within single request (not supported)
✗ Any workflow that blocks on the API result

LATENCY SLA MATH:
If your SLA is 30 hours and batch takes up to 24 hours:
→ Submit every 4 hours (30 - 24 = 6 hour buffer, submit at 6h intervals for safety)

FAILURE HANDLING:
1. Identify failed requests by custom_id
2. For context-too-long failures: chunk the document and resubmit
3. For other errors: fix the request parameters and resubmit only failed items
4. Never resubmit the whole batch — just the failures

COST OPTIMIZATION:
1. Refine prompts on a sample set first
2. Run 10-20 test requests synchronously to validate output format
3. Only then submit the full batch
4. Avoids expensive resubmission of 10,000 requests due to prompt issue
""")

# Demo (creates real batch — comment out if you don't want to use API credits)
sample_docs = [
    {"id": "001", "text": "INVOICE #INV-001\nVendor: Acme Corp\nTotal: $500.00"},
    {"id": "002", "text": "INVOICE #INV-002\nVendor: Widget Co\nTotal: $1,200.00"},
    {"id": "003", "text": "INVOICE #INV-003\nVendor: Supply Plus\nTotal: $89.99"},
]

if __name__ == "__main__":
    batch_decision_guide()

    print("\n=== Batch API Structure Demo ===")
    requests = create_batch_requests(sample_docs)
    print(f"Sample request structure:")
    print(json.dumps(requests[0], indent=2))

    # ── SIMULATION MODE: See the full workflow without using API credits ──
    print("\n=== Simulated Batch Workflow (no API credits needed) ===\n")

    # Simulate submit
    fake_batch_id = "batch_abc123def456"
    print(f"1. submit_batch(sample_docs)")
    print(f"   → Batch ID: {fake_batch_id}")
    print(f"   → Status: in_progress")
    print(f"   → {len(sample_docs)} requests submitted\n")

    # Simulate polling
    print(f"2. poll_batch('{fake_batch_id}')")
    print(f"   Polling... status=in_progress")
    print(f"   Polling... status=in_progress")
    print(f"   Polling... status=ended ✓")
    print(f"   → request_counts: succeeded={len(sample_docs)-1}, errored=1, expired=0\n")

    # Simulate results
    print(f"3. process_batch_results('{fake_batch_id}')")
    for doc in sample_docs[:2]:
        print(f"   ✓ custom_id=doc-{doc['id']}: extracted vendor + total successfully")
    print(f"   ✗ custom_id=doc-{sample_docs[2]['id']}: ERRORED (context limit exceeded)")
    print(f"   → Resubmit doc-{sample_docs[2]['id']} with chunking\n")

    print("KEY EXAM CONCEPTS demonstrated above:")
    print("  - custom_id correlates each result with its source request")
    print("  - Failures are handled per-document, not all-or-nothing")
    print("  - Failed docs can be resubmitted with modifications (chunking, shorter prompts)")
    print("  - No multi-turn tool calling in batch (single request/response only)")
