    > Create the file lab-d4-prompt-eng/batch_processing.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • A "Message Batches API Decision Guide" listing when to use batch (overnight reports, weekly audits) vs when NOT to use it (blocking CI checks, real-time interactions)
    • A "Batch API Structure Demo" showing the JSON structure of a sample request with custom_id, model, max_tokens, and messages fields
    • Instructions for submitting, polling, and processing results (commented out to avoid using API credits)

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens if you use the Batch API for a blocking pre-merge check and it takes 6 hours to process? (Answer: developers are blocked for 6 hours. Batch has no latency SLA — unacceptable for blocking workflows)
    2. Why is custom_id essential? If 3 of 100 batch requests fail and you didn't use custom_id, how do you match results to requests? (Answer: you can't — custom_id is the only way to correlate failures with their source requests)
    3. Can you run a multi-turn agentic loop inside a batch request? (Answer: no — Batch API doesn't support multi-turn tool calling. Use synchronous API for agentic workflows)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "reduce costs" with blocking + non-blocking workflows → batch the non-blocking only
    • Wrong answer trap: "Batch everything for 50% savings" — batch has no latency SLA, blocks developers
    • Wrong answer trap: "Keep everything synchronous" — wastes money on latency-tolerant workloads
    • The correct pattern: Synchronous for blocking (pre-merge). Batch for non-blocking (overnight reports).

    ✅ Lab 4.5 Checkpoint

    • Batch API: 50% cost savings, up to 24-hour window, no latency SLA guarantee
    • custom_id: your identifier to correlate requests with responses
    • Use for: overnight reports, weekly audits, nightly test generation (latency-tolerant)
    • Don't use for: blocking workflows (pre-merge checks), real-time interactions
    • Batch API does NOT support multi-turn tool calling within a single request
    • Failure handling: resubmit only failed requests (by custom_id), not whole batch

    LAB 4.6: MULTI-INSTANCE AND MULTI-PASS REVIEW

    Domain 4 — Task Statement 4.6 Self-review limits, independent instances, per-file + integration passes ~20 minutesAdvanced | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. When is batch processing appropriate vs inappropriate? (Lab 4.5)
    2. What is the custom_id field used for in batch results? (Lab 4.5)

    Why This Matters

    Claude generates a function with a subtle off-by-one error. You ask the same session to review it. Claude says "the code looks correct" — because it just wrote it and the reasoning feels sound. The fix: use a separate, independent Claude instance for review. Fresh eyes catch what the author misses.

    Exam connection (Sample Q12): Split into per-file local analysis + cross-file integration pass. NOT bigger model, NOT require smaller PRs.

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > I have two small Python files. First, generate a function that validates
> a customer email and returns their account details from a dictionary.
> Include error handling.
>
> Now, pretend you are a DIFFERENT reviewer who has never seen the requirements.
> Review the code you just wrote with fresh eyes for: SQL injection,
> missing error handling, unvalidated inputs, and logic bugs.
> Report your confidence (0.0-1.0) for each finding.
>
> Did you catch anything you might have missed if you were reviewing
> your own code with the original context still in mind?

    This demonstrates the self-review limitation: the same session that wrote the code tends to defend its own decisions. An independent instance with no prior context catches more issues.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d4-prompt-eng/multi_instance_review.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── WHY SELF-REVIEW FAILS ──────────────────────────────────
# When the same Claude session generates code and then reviews it,
# the model retains its reasoning context — the decisions that led
# to the code feel "correct" because it just made them.
# Independent review instances have no such attachment.

def generate_code(prompt: str) -> str:
    """Generate code (simulates Claude generating an implementation)."""
    print("\n[GENERATOR INSTANCE] Generating code...")
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=512,
        messages=[{"role": "user", "content": prompt}]
    )
    return response.content[0].text


def independent_review(code: str, review_criteria: str) -> dict:
    """
    Review code with an INDEPENDENT instance.

    This is a fresh API call with no prior context.
    The reviewer has no attachment to the design decisions in the code.
    """
    print("\n[INDEPENDENT REVIEWER] Reviewing without generator context...")
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=512,
        system="""You are a code reviewer. You have not seen the requirements or
implementation decisions for this code. Review it with fresh eyes.

For each finding, report your confidence (0.0-1.0) alongside the issue.
Low confidence findings should be flagged for human review.""",
        messages=[{
            "role": "user",
            "content": f"{review_criteria}\n\nCode to review:\n```python\n{code}\n```\n\nReturn JSON array of findings."
        }]
    )
    text = response.content[0].text
    # Parse findings
    try:
        start = text.find("[")
        end = text.rfind("]") + 1
        if start >= 0 and end > start:
            findings = json.loads(text[start:end])
            return {"findings": findings, "raw": text[:200]}
    except:
        pass
    return {"findings": [], "raw": text[:200]}


def multi_pass_large_review(files: dict) -> dict:
    """
    Multi-pass review for large codebases.

    Pass 1: Per-file local analysis (bugs, security within each file)
    Pass 2: Cross-file integration analysis (data flow, interface contracts)

    WHY SPLIT:
    - "Lost in the middle" effect: reviewing 10 files in one prompt
      causes attention dilution and missed issues
    - Cross-file issues need focused attention separate from local issues
    """
    print("\n[MULTI-PASS REVIEW]")

    # Pass 1: Per-file local analysis
    per_file_results = {}
    for filename, content in files.items():
        print(f"  Pass 1: Local analysis of {filename}...")
        response = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
            max_tokens=512,
            messages=[{
                "role": "user",
                "content": (
                    f"Review ONLY for local issues in {filename} "
                    f"(bugs, security within this file — NOT cross-file concerns):\n"
                    f"```python\n{content}\n```\n"
                    f"Return JSON: [{{\"line\": N, \"issue\": \"...\", "
                    f"\"severity\": \"high/medium/low\", \"confidence\": 0.0-1.0}}]"
                )
            }]
        )
        text = response.content[0].text
        try:
            s = text.find("["); e = text.rfind("]")+1
            per_file_results[filename] = json.loads(text[s:e]) if s>=0 and e>s else []
        except:
            per_file_results[filename] = []

    # Pass 2: Cross-file integration pass
    print("  Pass 2: Cross-file integration analysis...")
    summary = json.dumps(per_file_results, indent=2)
    response = client.messages.create(
        model="claude-sonnet-4-6",
        max_tokens=512,
        messages=[{
            "role": "user",
            "content": (
                f"Review for CROSS-FILE integration issues only:\n"
                f"- Interface contract violations between files\n"
                f"- Data flow inconsistencies\n"
                f"- Missing error propagation across module boundaries\n\n"
                f"Per-file findings:\n{summary}\n\n"
                f"Files:\n" + "\n\n".join(f"# {fn}\n{fc}" for fn, fc in files.items()) +
                f"\n\nReturn JSON: [{{\"files\": [\"...\"], \"issue\": \"...\", \"severity\": \"...\"}}]"
            )
        }]
    )

    return {
        "per_file": per_file_results,
        "integration": response.content[0].text[:200]
    }


if __name__ == "__main__":
    # Demo: generate then independently review
    generated = generate_code(
        "Write a Python function that validates a customer email and returns "
        "their account details from a dictionary. Include error handling."
    )
    print(f"Generated code:\n{generated[:200]}...")

    review = independent_review(generated,
        "Review for: SQL injection, missing error handling, unvalidated inputs, logic bugs.")
    print(f"\nIndependent review findings: {json.dumps(review['findings'], indent=2)[:300]}")

    # Demo: multi-pass review
    test_files = {
        "auth.py": "def verify(token):\n    if token == 'admin': return True\n    return db.get(token)",
        "api.py":  "from auth import verify\ndef handle(req):\n    verify(req.token)\n    return data[req.user_id]"
    }
    results = multi_pass_large_review(test_files)
    print(f"\nMulti-pass results:")
    print(f"Per-file: {json.dumps(results['per_file'], indent=2)[:200]}")
    print(f"Integration: {results['integration'][:200]}")

    # === Confidence-Based Review Routing ===
    print("\n\n=== Confidence Calibration for Human Review Routing ===")

    def route_findings_by_confidence(findings: list) -> dict:
        """Route review findings based on model-reported confidence scores."""
        routed = {"auto_post": [], "spot_check": [], "human_review": []}

        for finding in findings:
            conf = finding.get("confidence", 0.5)
            if conf >= 0.9:
                routed["auto_post"].append(finding)
            elif conf >= 0.7:
                routed["spot_check"].append(finding)
            else:
                routed["human_review"].append(finding)

        return routed

    # Simulate findings with confidence scores from the review above
    sample_findings = [
        {"issue": "SQL injection in query builder", "severity": "high", "confidence": 0.95},
        {"issue": "Missing null check on user input", "severity": "medium", "confidence": 0.82},
        {"issue": "Possible race condition in cache", "severity": "high", "confidence": 0.55},
        {"issue": "Inconsistent error format", "severity": "low", "confidence": 0.73},
    ]

    routed = route_findings_by_confidence(sample_findings)
    print(f"\n  Auto-post (conf >= 0.9): {len(routed['auto_post'])} findings")
    for f in routed["auto_post"]:
        print(f"    - {f['issue']} (conf: {f['confidence']})")
    print(f"  Spot-check (0.7-0.9): {len(routed['spot_check'])} findings")
    for f in routed["spot_check"]:
        print(f"    - {f['issue']} (conf: {f['confidence']})")
    print(f"  Human review (< 0.7): {len(routed['human_review'])} findings")
    for f in routed["human_review"]:
        print(f"    - {f['issue']} (conf: {f['confidence']})")

    print("""
    CRITICAL: Confidence scores must be CALIBRATED using labeled validation sets.
    "90% confidence" means nothing until you verify it's accurate 90% of the time.

    Calibration process:
    1. Extract 200+ labeled findings (human-verified as correct or incorrect)
    2. Group by confidence bracket (0.9+, 0.8-0.9, 0.7-0.8, etc.)
    3. Measure actual accuracy in each bracket
    4. Adjust thresholds until brackets match their claimed accuracy

    Multi-instance architecture:
    Generator → Independent Reviewer → Human (for low-confidence items)
    NOT: Generator → Self-review → PR (self-review is least effective)
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d4-prompt-eng/multi_instance_review.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • A GENERATOR INSTANCE producing a Python function, followed by an INDEPENDENT REVIEWER finding issues (SQL injection, missing error handling, etc.) with confidence scores
    • A MULTI-PASS REVIEW running Pass 1 (per-file local analysis of auth.py and api.py) then Pass 2 (cross-file integration analysis for interface violations and data flow issues)
    • A "Confidence Calibration for Human Review Routing" section showing 4 sample findings routed into 3 buckets: auto-post (confidence >= 0.9), spot-check (0.7-0.9), and human-review (< 0.7)

    Code Analysis

    1. Why does self-review fail? When the same instance generates code and then reviews it, why does it miss subtle bugs? (Answer: it retains its reasoning context and doesn't question its own decisions — the reasoning that led to the code still feels "correct")
    2. Why does single-pass review degrade on large PRs? Consider a 14-file PR reviewed in one prompt. Is depth consistent across all files? (Answer: no — attention dilutes. Files at the end get superficial review. Per-file + integration passes solve this)
    3. What happens if you skip passing prior findings to the second review pass? (Answer: the second review duplicates comments the first already made, wasting developer attention)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "review misses subtle bugs" or "same instance generated and reviewed" → independent review instance
    • Wrong answer trap: "Add extended thinking" — same session still has reasoning attachment
    • Wrong answer trap: "Require smaller PRs" — shifts burden to developers
    • The correct pattern: Separate generation and review into independent instances. Multi-pass for large PRs.

    ✅ Lab 4.6 Checkpoint

    • Self-review: model retains reasoning context → less likely to question its own decisions
    • Independent instances: fresh context → no attachment to original decisions
    • Multi-pass: per-file local issues + separate cross-file integration pass
    • Confidence scores must be calibrated with labeled validation sets
    • Route low-confidence findings to human review; high-confidence to automated posting

    Quick Check — Module 4

    Q: Your extraction pipeline uses tool_use with a JSON schema. No syntax errors, but line items don't sum to the stated total and vendor IDs are fabricated for documents that lack them. What should you add?

    A) A stricter JSON schema B) Custom semantic validation + nullable fields for absent data C) More max_tokens for precision D) Few-shot examples

    Answer

    B. tool_use + schema eliminates syntax errors but NOT semantic errors (wrong sums, fabricated values). Custom validation catches cross-field issues. Nullable fields prevent fabrication by letting the model return null. (Lab 4.3, Lab 4.4)

    Q: Your manager wants to batch both a blocking pre-merge check and an overnight report for 50% savings. How should you evaluate?

    A) Batch both B) Batch the overnight report only; keep synchronous for pre-merge C) Keep both synchronous D) Batch both with a timeout fallback

    Answer

    B. The Batch API has up to 24-hour processing with no latency SLA — unsuitable for blocking checks where developers wait. Ideal for overnight jobs. (Lab 4.5, Exam Sample Q11)

    MODULE 5: CONTEXT MANAGEMENT & RELIABILITY

    Domain 5 — 15% of exam Task Statements 5.1 – 5.6

    Key Terms for Module 5

    • Case facts block: A structured section injected into each prompt containing verified transactional facts (customer ID, order number, amounts, dates). Prevents facts from being lost during progressive summarization.
    • Tool result trimming: Keeping only relevant fields from verbose tool outputs (e.g., 6 fields from a 22-field order response). Prevents token waste in context.
    • "Lost in the middle" effect: Models reliably process information at the beginning and end of long inputs but may miss findings from middle sections. Mitigate by placing summaries first.
    • Progressive summarization risk: When conversation history is summarized, specific values (amounts, dates, order numbers) can be lost. Case facts blocks prevent this.
    • Escalation triggers: Specific, verifiable criteria for when to route to a human agent. Reliable triggers: explicit customer request, policy gap, no progress. Unreliable: sentiment analysis, self-reported confidence.
    • Policy gap: When the policy is silent on the customer's situation (e.g., competitor price matching when policy only covers own-site adjustments). Different from policy violation.
    • Structured error propagation: Returning failure type, attempted query, partial results, and alternatives from subagents — not just "error occurred."
    • Scratchpad file: A file where agents write key findings during exploration. Referenced later to counteract context degradation.
    • Context degradation: In extended sessions, models start saying "typically X" instead of citing specific files/lines. A sign that context is too full of verbose tool outputs.
    • Manifest-based crash recovery: Each agent exports its state to a known location. On resume, the coordinator loads the manifest and injects state into agent prompts.
    • Stratified random sampling: Sampling a percentage of high-confidence extractions (not just low-confidence ones) to catch novel error patterns and measure ongoing accuracy.
    • Field-level confidence: Per-field certainty scores (0.0-1.0) that enable targeted human review of uncertain extractions rather than reviewing everything.
    • Claim-source mapping: Structured pairing of each claim with its source (URL, document name, excerpt, publication date). Must be preserved through synthesis — not compressed away.
    • Temporal metadata: Publication/collection dates included in structured outputs. Prevents temporal differences from being misinterpreted as contradictions.
    • Content-type rendering: Matching output format to content type: financial data → tables, news → prose, technical → structured lists. Don't convert everything to one format.

    LAB 5.1: CONTEXT MANAGEMENT ACROSS LONG INTERACTIONS

    Domain 5 — Task Statement 5.1 Case facts block, tool result trimming, "lost in the middle" ~25 minutesAdvanced | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. Why does self-review miss issues that an independent reviewer catches? (Lab 4.6)
    2. What is the multi-pass review pattern for large PRs? (Lab 4.6)

    Why This Matters

    After 15 turns, your agent says "your order was approximately $150" when the real total from turn 3 was $149.99. Progressive summarization compressed the exact value. The fix: extract transactional facts into a persistent case facts block — exact amounts, dates, order IDs — outside the summarized history.

    Exam connection: Case facts blocks and tool result trimming are core Domain 5 concepts. The exam tests whether you know that progressive summarization loses precision.

    What You'll Learn

    Long conversations accumulate verbose tool results that consume tokens disproportionately. "Lost in the middle": model misses findings in the middle of long inputs. Case facts block: persistent structured layer outside summarized history.

    Challenge Mode (optional — try before looking at the solution)

    A customer support agent has a 22-field tool result from lookup_order. Design:

    1. A case facts block that extracts only the 5 fields relevant to the current issue
    2. A tool result trimmer that reduces the 22-field result to those 5 fields before injecting into context
    3. A strategy for where to place the case facts in the prompt to avoid the "lost in the middle" effect

    Think about: which fields matter for a refund request vs a shipping inquiry? Then compare to the reference.

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > Imagine a customer support agent that just called a lookup_order tool
> and got back a response with 22 fields including: id, customer_id, items,
> total, status, tracking, warehouse_id, picker_id, packed_at, shipped_at,
> carrier, carrier_service, weight_lbs, dimensions_inches, insurance_value,
> signature_required, delivery_eta, delivery_attempts, delivery_instructions,
> return_label_url, gift_wrap, gift_message.
>
> The customer is asking about a RETURN. Which fields are relevant?
> Design a "case facts block" that extracts only the essential fields
> and a "trim function" that reduces the 22-field response to just those fields.
> Explain why this matters for context management in long conversations.

    This demonstrates two key patterns: tool result trimming (preventing context bloat) and case facts blocks (preserving exact values that progressive summarization would approximate).

    Reference Implementation: lab-d5-context-reliability/context_management.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── PATTERN 1: CASE FACTS BLOCK ───────────────────────────
# Problem: Progressive summarization loses exact numbers, dates, amounts.
# "Customer was refunded" loses the $149.99 amount.
# Solution: Extract transactional facts into a persistent block
# that's included VERBATIM in every prompt.

def build_case_facts_block(facts: dict) -> str:
    """
    Create a persistent facts block that survives summarization.

    Include: amounts, dates, order IDs, statuses, customer-stated expectations.
    These are the facts that matter — preserve them exactly.
    """
    return f"""
=== CASE FACTS (do not summarize these) ===
Customer ID:       {facts.get('customer_id', 'unknown')}
Customer Email:    {facts.get('email', 'unknown')}
Order IDs:         {', '.join(facts.get('order_ids', []))}
Refund Amounts:    {', '.join(f"${a}" for a in facts.get('refund_amounts', []))}
Dispute Amount:    ${facts.get('dispute_amount', 'N/A')}
Customer Expected: {facts.get('customer_expectation', 'not stated')}
Case Status:       {facts.get('status', 'open')}
Last Action:       {facts.get('last_action', 'none')}
=== END CASE FACTS ===
"""


# ── PATTERN 2: TOOL RESULT TRIMMING ──────────────────────
# Problem: get_customer returns 40+ fields, but we need 5.
# Each tool call accumulates verbose output → context fills fast.
# Solution: Trim tool results to only relevant fields before storing.

FULL_ORDER_RESPONSE = {
    "id": "ORD-555",
    "customer_id": "CUST-001",
    "items": ["Blue Widget x2"],
    "total": 149.99,
    "status": "shipped",
    "tracking": "1Z999AA10123456784",
    "warehouse_id": "WH-007",
    "picker_id": "EMP-1234",
    "packed_at": "2024-01-14T09:23:11Z",
    "shipped_at": "2024-01-14T14:05:22Z",
    "carrier": "UPS",
    "carrier_service": "Ground",
    "weight_lbs": 2.3,
    "dimensions_inches": "12x8x6",
    "insurance_value": 149.99,
    "signature_required": False,
    "delivery_eta": "2024-01-18",
    "delivery_attempts": 0,
    "delivery_instructions": None,
    "return_label_url": None,
    "gift_wrap": False,
    "gift_message": None,
    "created_at": "2024-01-13T11:15:00Z",
    "updated_at": "2024-01-14T14:05:22Z"
}

RETURN_RELEVANT_FIELDS = {"id", "status", "total", "items", "delivery_eta", "tracking"}

def trim_for_return_context(order: dict) -> dict:
    """Keep only return-relevant fields. Reduces 22 fields → 6 fields."""
    return {k: v for k, v in order.items() if k in RETURN_RELEVANT_FIELDS}


# ── PATTERN 3: POSITION EFFECTS ───────────────────────────
# "Lost in the middle": models reliably process beginning and end of input.
# Middle sections may be missed in long aggregated inputs.
# Solution: Place key findings summaries at the BEGINNING.

def build_aggregated_report(findings: list) -> str:
    """
    Structure aggregated results to mitigate position effects.

    Pattern: Summary first → then detailed sections with headers.
    """
    # Summary at the TOP (most reliable position)
    summary_lines = [f"- {f['file']}: {len(f['issues'])} issues" for f in findings]
    summary = "KEY FINDINGS SUMMARY:\n" + "\n".join(summary_lines)

    # Detailed sections with explicit headers
    details = []
    for f in findings:
        section = f"\n--- FILE: {f['file']} ({len(f['issues'])} issues) ---\n"
        for issue in f['issues']:
            section += f"  Line {issue['line']}: [{issue['severity']}] {issue['description']}\n"
        details.append(section)

    return summary + "\n\n" + "\n".join(details)


def demonstrate_context_patterns():
    """Demonstrate all three context management patterns."""

    print("=== Pattern 1: Case Facts Block ===\n")
    facts = {
        "customer_id": "CUST-001",
        "email": "jane@email.com",
        "order_ids": ["ORD-555", "ORD-556"],
        "refund_amounts": [149.99, 29.99],
        "dispute_amount": 149.99,
        "customer_expectation": "Full refund for damaged item, expected within 3 days",
        "status": "investigating",
        "last_action": "Refund initiated for ORD-555"
    }
    print(build_case_facts_block(facts))
    print("These facts persist across conversation — never summarized away.")

    print("\n=== Pattern 2: Tool Result Trimming ===\n")
    print(f"Full order response: {len(FULL_ORDER_RESPONSE)} fields")
    trimmed = trim_for_return_context(FULL_ORDER_RESPONSE)
    print(f"Trimmed response:    {len(trimmed)} fields")
    print(f"Kept: {list(trimmed.keys())}")
    print(f"Tokens saved: ~{(len(json.dumps(FULL_ORDER_RESPONSE)) - len(json.dumps(trimmed)))} chars per tool call")

    print("\n=== Pattern 3: Aggregated Report Structure ===\n")
    sample_findings = [
        {"file": "auth.py",   "issues": [{"line": 5, "severity": "high", "description": "hardcoded token"}]},
        {"file": "api.py",    "issues": [{"line": 12, "severity": "medium", "description": "missing null check"},
                                          {"line": 34, "severity": "low",    "description": "unused import"}]},
        {"file": "utils.py",  "issues": []},
    ]
    report = build_aggregated_report(sample_findings)
    print(report)
    print("\nSummary at top → reliably read. Details below with section headers.")

    print("\n=== Pattern 4: Context Budget for Multi-Agent Systems ===")
    print("""
    When downstream agents have limited context budgets:
    → Upstream agents return structured data, NOT verbose reasoning chains

    WRONG (upstream returns):
    "After carefully analyzing the document, I considered several interpretations.
     The vendor name appears to be Widget Corp based on the letterhead, though
     there is also a reference to Widget Corporation in the body text..."

    CORRECT (upstream returns):
    {"vendor_name": "Widget Corp", "confidence": 0.85,
     "source": "invoice-header", "alternative": "Widget Corporation (body text)"}

    The downstream agent gets facts, not reasoning. Context preserved for its own work.
    """)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    demonstrate_context_patterns()

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d5-context-reliability/context_management.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • "Pattern 1: Case Facts Block" showing a formatted block with customer ID, email, order IDs, refund amounts, dispute amount, and case status that persists across conversation turns
    • "Pattern 2: Tool Result Trimming" showing a full order response (22 fields) reduced to 6 relevant fields, with character savings calculated
    • "Pattern 3: Aggregated Report Structure" showing a KEY FINDINGS SUMMARY at the top followed by detailed per-file sections with severity-tagged issues
    • "Pattern 4: Context Budget" explaining why upstream agents should return structured data (not verbose reasoning chains) to downstream agents

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens without a case facts block? After 15 turns, does the agent remember the exact order total from turn 3? (Answer: no — progressive summarization compresses "$149.99" into "approximately $150")
    2. What is the cost of keeping all 22 fields from the order lookup instead of trimming to 6? (Answer: context fills with irrelevant fields after multiple tool calls, crowding out important conversation history)
    3. Why does the report structure place the summary at the TOP? What happens if key findings are in the middle of a long aggregated report? (Answer: "lost in the middle" effect — models reliably process information at the beginning and end of long inputs but may miss findings from middle sections)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "agent says 'approximately $150' instead of '$149.99'" → case facts block
    • Wrong answer trap: "Increase context window" — delays but doesn't prevent summarization loss
    • Wrong answer trap: "Use progressive summarization" — summarization IS the cause of the problem
    • The correct pattern: Extract exact values into a persistent case facts block outside summarized history

    ✅ Lab 5.1 Checkpoint

    • Case facts block: extract transactional facts (amounts, dates, IDs) into persistent structured layer
    • Tool result trimming: keep only relevant fields before accumulating in context
    • "Lost in the middle": place key summaries at beginning, use section headers
    • Pass complete conversation history in every API request for coherence
    • Upstream agents return structured data (not reasoning chains) when downstream has limited context

    LAB 5.2: ESCALATION AND AMBIGUITY RESOLUTION

    Domain 5 — Task Statement 5.2 Escalation triggers, explicit request honoring, policy gaps ~20 minutesIntermediate | Requires: Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What is the "case facts block" pattern? (Lab 5.1)
    2. Why does progressive summarization lose exact values? (Lab 5.1)

    Why This Matters

    Your agent escalates 45% of cases (target: 20%). It escalates simple damage replacements while attempting policy exceptions. The problem: no clear escalation criteria. The fix: explicit categorical triggers — customer requests human, policy gap exists, no progress after 3 attempts.

    Exam connection (Sample Q3): Explicit criteria + few-shot beats sentiment analysis and self-reported confidence. Sentiment doesn't correlate with case complexity.

    See It In Action

    Try this in Claude Code:

    > You are a customer support agent. I'm going to give you 4 customer messages.
> For each, decide: should you escalate to a human agent or handle it yourself?
> Use these criteria:
> - Escalate IMMEDIATELY if customer explicitly requests a human
> - Escalate if the issue falls outside your policy (policy gap)
> - DO NOT escalate just because the customer is frustrated — if you can resolve it, do so
>
> Message 1: "I want to speak to a manager right now."
> Message 2: "I found the same item at Amazon for $20 less. Will you match it?"
> Message 3: "I'm really frustrated! My order has been processing for 3 days!"
> Message 4: "I'm John Smith and I have a problem with my account."
>            (Assume 3 customers named John Smith exist in the system)
>
> Explain your reasoning for each decision.

    Notice how explicit criteria produce consistent, correct escalation decisions. Message 1 is always escalated (explicit request). Message 2 is escalated (policy gap). Message 3 is handled (frustration alone is not an escalation trigger). Message 4 requires disambiguation (ask for email/order number).

    Reference Implementation: lab-d5-context-reliability/escalation_patterns.py

    import anthropic
import json

client = anthropic.Anthropic()

# ── ESCALATION CRITERIA WITH FEW-SHOT EXAMPLES ────────────
# Vague escalation criteria produce unreliable behavior.
# Specific criteria + few-shot examples demonstrate edge cases.

SYSTEM_PROMPT = """You are a customer support agent.
Resolve issues autonomously when possible. Use escalate_to_human only when needed.

ESCALATION CRITERIA (with examples):

1. Customer explicitly requests human — ESCALATE IMMEDIATELY
   Example: "I want to speak to a manager" → escalate NOW
   Example: "Let me talk to a real person" → escalate NOW
   Do NOT: investigate first; do NOT say "let me try to help first"

2. Policy gap — ESCALATE
   Example: Customer asks for competitor price match
     → Our policy covers own-site adjustments only; policy is silent on competitors
     → This is a policy GAP, not a policy violation → escalate
   Example: Customer asks for refund on a digital item opened 45 days ago
     → Policy only covers 30 days → policy GAP for special circumstances → escalate
   Do NOT escalate for: standard returns within policy, normal refunds

3. Unable to make progress — ESCALATE
   Example: Same issue persists after 3 tool calls → escalate
   Do NOT escalate for: first attempt, trying one approach

DO NOT ESCALATE for (handle autonomously):
- Standard returns within 30 days → process the refund
- Frustrated customer where issue is within your capability
  → Acknowledge frustration, offer resolution, escalate ONLY if they repeat request

AMBIGUITY: Multiple customer matches
- If lookup returns multiple accounts for same name → ask for additional identifier
  (email, order number, phone)
  Do NOT select based on heuristics (most recent, closest match)"""

tools = [
    {
        "name": "get_customer",
        "description": "Gets customer by email or name. Returns customer data. If multiple matches by name, returns list requiring disambiguation.",
        "input_schema": {"type": "object",
                         "properties": {
                             "email": {"type": "string", "description": "Customer email (preferred identifier)"},
                             "name": {"type": "string", "description": "Customer name (may return multiple matches)"}
                         }}
    },
    {
        "name": "escalate_to_human",
        "description": "Escalates to human agent. Use per criteria above.",
        "input_schema": {
            "type": "object",
            "properties": {
                "reason": {"type": "string",
                           "enum": ["explicit_customer_request", "policy_gap",
                                    "no_progress", "high_value_case"]},
                "customer_id": {"type": ["string", "null"]},
                "summary": {"type": "string"},
                "root_cause": {"type": "string"},
                "recommended_action": {"type": "string"}
            },
            "required": ["reason", "summary", "recommended_action"]
        }
    }
]

CUSTOMERS = {
    "jane@email.com": {"id": "CUST-001", "name": "Jane Smith"},
    "john@email.com": {"id": "CUST-003", "name": "John Smith"},
}

# Name-based lookup returns MULTIPLE matches (ambiguity scenario)
CUSTOMERS_BY_NAME = {
    "John Smith": [
        {"id": "CUST-003", "name": "John Smith", "email": "john@email.com"},
        {"id": "CUST-004", "name": "John Smith", "email": "jsmith@corp.com"},
        {"id": "CUST-005", "name": "John Smith", "email": "j.smith@gmail.com"},
    ]
}

def execute_tool(name, input_data):
    if name == "get_customer":
        email = input_data.get("email", "")
        if email:
            c = CUSTOMERS.get(email)
            return json.dumps(c) if c else json.dumps({"isError": False, "results": None,
                                                        "message": "No customer found"})
        # Name-based lookup → may return multiple matches
        cname = input_data.get("name", "")
        matches = CUSTOMERS_BY_NAME.get(cname, [])
        if len(matches) > 1:
            return json.dumps({"multiple_matches": True, "count": len(matches),
                               "matches": [{"id": m["id"], "name": m["name"],
                                            "email_hint": m["email"][:3] + "***"} for m in matches],
                               "message": "Multiple customers found. Ask for additional identifier."})
        elif len(matches) == 1:
            return json.dumps(matches[0])
        return json.dumps({"isError": False, "results": None, "message": "No customer found"})
    if name == "escalate_to_human":
        print(f"\n  → ESCALATED: reason={input_data.get('reason')}")
        print(f"    Summary: {input_data.get('summary', '')[:80]}")
        return json.dumps({"escalated": True, "ticket_id": "TKT-1234"})
    return json.dumps({"error": f"Unknown: {name}"})

def run_escalation_test(scenario: str, message: str):
    print(f"\n{'='*60}")
    print(f"SCENARIO: {scenario}")
    print(f"MESSAGE:  {message}")
    print(f"{'='*60}")

    messages = [{"role": "user", "content": message}]

    while True:
        r = client.messages.create(
            model="claude-sonnet-4-6", max_tokens=512,
            system=SYSTEM_PROMPT, tools=tools, messages=messages
        )
        print(f"  stop_reason: {r.stop_reason}")

        if r.stop_reason == "end_turn":
            text = "".join(b.text for b in r.content if hasattr(b, "text"))
            print(f"  Response: {text[:200]}")
            break
        elif r.stop_reason == "tool_use":
            messages.append({"role": "assistant", "content": r.content})
            results = []
            for b in r.content:
                if b.type == "tool_use":
                    print(f"  Tool: {b.name}")
                    results.append({"type": "tool_result", "tool_use_id": b.id,
                                    "content": execute_tool(b.name, b.input)})
            messages.append({"role": "user", "content": results})
        else:
            break


if __name__ == "__main__":
    run_escalation_test(
        "Explicit human request — must escalate immediately",
        "I want to speak to a manager right now, not a chatbot."
    )

    run_escalation_test(
        "Policy gap — competitor price match",
        "I'm jane@email.com. I bought the same item at Amazon for $20 less. Will you match it?"
    )

    run_escalation_test(
        "Frustrated but within capability — should offer to help",
        "I'm jane@email.com. I'm really frustrated! I've been waiting 3 days for my order."
    )

    run_escalation_test(
        "Multiple matches — should ask for more info",
        "I'm John Smith and I have a problem with my account."
    )

    print("\n\n=== Escalation Patterns Summary ===")
    print("""
    Unreliable escalation triggers (don't use):
    - Sentiment analysis ("escalate when customer is frustrated")
    - Self-reported confidence ("escalate when I'm not confident")
    - Complexity estimate ("escalate complex cases")

    Reliable escalation triggers:
    - Explicit customer request (binary — did they ask or not?)
    - Policy gap (verifiable — does policy cover this?)
    - No progress (measurable — N tool calls with no resolution)

    The frustration nuance:
    Frustrated + issue within capability → offer resolution
    Frustrated + repeats human request → THEN escalate
    Not: frustration alone → immediate escalation
    """)

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    > Create the file lab-d5-context-reliability/escalation_patterns.py with the code above and run it.

    What you should see:

    • Test 1 ("speak to a manager"): → ESCALATED immediately, reason=explicit_customer_request. No investigation attempted.
    • Test 2 ("competitor price match"): → ESCALATED, reason=policy_gap. Agent recognizes policy is silent on this.
    • Test 3 ("really frustrated, order still processing"): → Agent acknowledges frustration AND offers to help. Does NOT escalate (issue is within capability).
    • Test 4 ("John Smith, problem with account"): → Agent should ask for email or order number to disambiguate (multiple_matches returned). Does NOT guess which John Smith.

    Key exam distinction (from Sample Question 3): Sentiment-based escalation is unreliable. Explicit categorical criteria (customer request, policy gap, no progress) are reliable.

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens if you remove escalation criteria entirely? (Answer: the agent guesses when to escalate — inconsistent behavior, high false escalation rate)
    2. Why is sentiment analysis an unreliable escalation trigger? (Answer: frustrated but simple cases get escalated unnecessarily — sentiment doesn't correlate with case complexity. A customer can be furious about a standard return that the agent can easily process)
    3. What goes wrong if the agent picks from multiple customer matches using heuristics (e.g., "most recent account")? (Answer: agent picks wrong customer — could process a refund to the wrong person. Always ask for an additional identifier to disambiguate)

    Policy Gap vs Complex Case vs Explicit Request

    The exam tests three distinct escalation triggers. Don't confuse them:

    Scenario Action Why
    Customer says "I want to speak to a manager" Escalate immediately Explicit human request — don't investigate first, don't try to resolve
    Customer wants competitor price match; your policy only covers own-site adjustments Escalate Policy gap — the policy is silent on this situation, agent has no authority
    Customer wants return on damaged item with photo evidence Resolve Complex but within policy — standard damage replacement process exists
    Customer is furious about a delayed order Offer resolution first Frustrated but issue is within capability — acknowledge frustration, then help
    Agent has tried 3 different tool calls with no progress Escalate No progress — further attempts are wasting the customer's time

    The exam trap: Confusing "complex" with "needs escalation." A complex case that's within policy should be resolved. A simple-sounding case where policy is silent (competitor price match) should be escalated. The trigger is policy coverage, not case complexity.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "poor escalation calibration" → explicit criteria + few-shot examples
    • Wrong answer trap: "Sentiment analysis" — sentiment doesn't correlate with case complexity
    • Wrong answer trap: "Self-reported confidence" — model is poorly calibrated on its own confidence
    • The correct pattern: Explicit triggers (customer request, policy gap, no progress) + few-shot demonstrations

    ✅ Lab 5.2 Checkpoint

    • Explicit human request → escalate immediately (no investigation first)
    • Policy gap (policy silent on the situation) → escalate
    • Complex but within policy → resolve (agent has the tools and authority)
    • Frustrated but issue within capability → offer resolution first
    • No progress after 3 tool calls → escalate
    • Multiple customer matches → ask for additional identifier (not heuristic selection)
    • Sentiment/confidence → unreliable escalation proxy; explicit criteria is reliable

    LAB 5.3: ERROR PROPAGATION IN MULTI-AGENT SYSTEMS

    Domain 5 — Task Statement 5.3 Structured error context, partial results, coordinator recovery ~20 minutesIntermediate

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. Name the three explicit escalation triggers. (Lab 5.2)
    2. What's the difference between a policy gap and a complex case? (Lab 5.2)

    Why This Matters

    Your search subagent times out. It returns "error occurred" to the coordinator. The coordinator can't decide: retry? use backup? proceed without? Generic errors force blind decisions. The fix: structured error context with failure type, what was attempted, partial results, and alternatives.

    Exam connection (Sample Q8): Structured error context enables intelligent recovery. Generic status hides context. Silent suppression and full termination are both anti-patterns.

    See It In Action

    Ask Claude Code about error handling:

    When you call a tool and it fails, what do you do? Do you retry? How do you decide whether to try a different approach or give up?

    Watch how Claude Code describes its own error handling strategy. Compare this to the anti-patterns in the reference code below: silent suppression (returning empty as success) and full termination (aborting on first failure). Claude Code itself uses structured error context — failure type, what was attempted, partial results — to make recovery decisions.

    Reference Implementation: lab-d5-context-reliability/error_propagation.py

    import json

# ── ANTI-PATTERNS vs CORRECT PATTERNS ────────────────────

def demonstrate_error_patterns():
    """Show anti-patterns vs correct error propagation."""

    print("=== Anti-Pattern 1: Silent Suppression ===\n")
    # WRONG: Subagent returns empty results as if search succeeded
    def search_subagent_bad(query: str) -> dict:
        try:
            # raise ConnectionError("Search service unavailable")
            raise ConnectionError("Simulated failure")
        except ConnectionError:
            # BAD: silently return empty as if search worked
            return {"results": [], "total": 0}

    result = search_subagent_bad("EV market trends")
    print(f"Bad error response: {result}")
    print("Problem: Coordinator thinks search found nothing vs search FAILED.")
    print("Coordinator can't distinguish 'no results' from 'search broken'.\n")

    print("=== Anti-Pattern 2: Workflow Termination ===\n")
    print("""
    WRONG: Subagent raises exception → coordinator catches → aborts entire workflow
    Problem: One source failure shouldn't abort a multi-source research task.
    """)

    print("=== Correct Pattern: Structured Error Context ===\n")
    # CORRECT: Return structured error with context for coordinator recovery
    def search_subagent_good(query: str, source: str) -> dict:
        try:
            raise ConnectionError(f"Connection timeout to {source}")
        except ConnectionError as e:
            # GOOD: Structured error with full context
            return {
                "success": False,
                "error": {
                    "failure_type": "connection_timeout",
                    "attempted_query": query,
                    "source": source,
                    "partial_results": None,  # or any partial data retrieved
                    "what_was_attempted": f"HTTP GET to {source}/search?q={query}",
                    "alternative_approaches": [
                        f"Try cached version of {source}",
                        "Use backup source: archive.org",
                        "Proceed with available sources and note gap"
                    ],
                    "is_retryable": True,
                    "results_may_exist": False
                }
            }

    error = search_subagent_good("EV market trends", "reuters.com")
    print(f"Good error response:\n{json.dumps(error, indent=2)}")

    print("\n\n=== Access Failure vs Valid Empty Result ===\n")
    print("""
    Access failure (success: False):
      Service didn't respond, timed out, threw exception
      → Coordinator: decide whether to retry, use backup source, or proceed without

    Valid empty result (success: True, results: []):
      Search executed successfully; genuinely no matches
      → Coordinator: this topic has no data at this source (not a failure)

    This distinction enables intelligent recovery:
    Access failure → retry or use backup
    Empty result   → note gap in coverage, continue with other sources
    """)


def demonstrate_partial_results():
    print("=== Partial Results Pattern ===\n")

    def analysis_subagent_with_partial(docs: list) -> dict:
        """Process documents, return partial results if some fail."""
        results = []
        errors = []

        for doc in docs:
            try:
                if doc["id"] == "doc-3":  # Simulate one failure
                    raise ValueError(f"Document {doc['id']} is corrupted")
                results.append({
                    "doc_id": doc["id"],
                    "findings": f"Analysis of {doc['text'][:20]}...",
                    "confidence": 0.85
                })
            except ValueError as e:
                errors.append({
                    "doc_id": doc["id"],
                    "error": str(e),
                    "is_retryable": False
                })

        return {
            "success": True,  # Overall operation didn't fail
            "partial_results": results,
            "errors": errors,
            "coverage": f"{len(results)}/{len(docs)} documents processed",
            "note": "Coordinator should note which documents have gaps"
        }

    docs = [
        {"id": "doc-1", "text": "EV market growth report"},
        {"id": "doc-2", "text": "Battery cost analysis"},
        {"id": "doc-3", "text": "Corrupted file"},
        {"id": "doc-4", "text": "Regional adoption data"},
    ]

    result = analysis_subagent_with_partial(docs)
    print(f"Partial results response:\n{json.dumps(result, indent=2)}")

    print("""
    Coordinator receives partial results:
    - Knows doc-3 failed (and can note gap in synthesis)
    - Has 3/4 documents analyzed (useful, not all-or-nothing)
    - Can decide: proceed with 3 docs, or retry doc-3 with different approach
    """)


def demonstrate_coverage_annotations():
    print("\n=== Coverage Annotations in Synthesis Output ===\n")
    print("""
    Synthesis output should annotate which findings are well-supported
    vs which have gaps due to unavailable sources:

    {
      "topic": "EV market growth",
      "findings": [
        {
          "claim": "35% market growth in 2023",
          "sources": ["iea.org", "bloomberg.com"],
          "confidence": "high",
          "coverage": "well_supported"
        },
        {
          "claim": "Regional breakdown by country",
          "sources": ["partial - reuters.com unavailable"],
          "confidence": "low",
          "coverage": "gap - reuters search failed, data may be incomplete"
        }
      ],
      "overall_coverage": "3 of 4 sources available; regional data has gaps"
    }

    Coordinator then: includes coverage annotation in final report
    User understands: "regional data may be incomplete because one source was unavailable"
    """)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    demonstrate_error_patterns()
    demonstrate_partial_results()
    demonstrate_coverage_annotations()

    Try It

    Ask Claude Code to create and run this file:

    Create lab-d5-context-reliability/error_propagation.py with the code above and run it

    What you should see:

    • "Anti-Pattern 1" showing a bad error response ({"error": "Search failed"}) that is indistinguishable from an empty result, followed by the correct structured error with failure_type, attempted_query, alternative_approaches, and is_retryable fields
    • "Partial Results Pattern" showing 4 documents processed where doc-3 fails but 3/4 succeed, returning partial_results alongside errors with per-document coverage
    • "Coverage Annotations in Synthesis Output" showing how the final synthesis tags each claim as well_supported or flags gaps due to unavailable sources

    Code Analysis

    1. What happens if a subagent returns a generic "error" string? The coordinator can't decide recovery strategy — retry, backup, or skip are all blind guesses without structured error context.
    2. What happens if errors are suppressed and empty results returned as success? Downstream agents produce incomplete reports without knowing data is missing — this is silent failure, the worst anti-pattern.
    3. What happens if the entire workflow terminates on one subagent failure? It wastes all other subagents' completed work — one source failure shouldn't abort a multi-source task.

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "subagent fails, coordinator can't recover" → structured error context
    • Wrong answer trap: "Return empty result as success" — silent suppression hides failures
    • Wrong answer trap: "Terminate entire workflow" — wastes completed work from other subagents
    • The correct pattern: Structured error with failure_type, partial_results, alternatives, is_retryable

    ✅ Lab 5.3 Checkpoint

    • Never silently suppress errors — return structured error context
    • Never terminate entire workflow on single subagent failure
    • Structured error: failure_type, attempted_query, partial_results, alternatives, is_retryable
    • Distinguish access failure (service down) from valid empty result (no data)
    • Return partial results with coverage annotations — all-or-nothing is an anti-pattern
    • Coordinator uses error context to decide: retry, use backup, or proceed with gap noted

    LAB 5.4: CONTEXT MANAGEMENT IN LARGE CODEBASE EXPLORATION

    Domain 5 — Task Statement 5.4 Scratchpad files, subagent delegation, crash recovery ~20 minutesGuided | Claude Code

    Quick Recall (from previous labs)

    1. What two anti-patterns should you avoid in multi-agent error handling? (Lab 5.3)
    2. What does structured error context enable the coordinator to do? (Lab 5.3)

    Why This Matters

    Two hours into exploring a 50-file codebase, you ask Claude about the first class it found. It says "the codebase typically uses the factory pattern" instead of naming the specific class from turn 3. This is context degradation — and scratchpad files, subagent delegation, and crash recovery manifests are the fixes.

    Exam connection: The exam tests scratchpad files for persistence, subagent delegation for isolation, and manifest-based recovery for crash resilience.

    What You'll Learn

    Extended sessions cause context degradation — model references "typical patterns" not specific code. Scratchpad files persist findings across context boundaries. Manifest-based crash recovery enables graceful resume after failures.

    See It In Action

    Ask Claude Code to explore this project:

    Give me a high-level overview of what this project contains — what are the main files and directories?

    Notice how Claude Code uses Glob to find files, then Read to inspect key ones, building understanding incrementally. In a long session, it would start referencing "typical patterns" instead of specific findings — that's context degradation, and the scratchpad pattern in this lab fixes it.

    Step 1: Practice the scratchpad pattern

    In Claude Code, start a codebase exploration session:

    > Let's explore the lab codebase. Create a scratchpad file at
  /tmp/exploration-scratchpad.md to record key findings as we go.
  Start by mapping the directory structure.

    As Claude explores, it will write to the scratchpad:

    # Exploration Scratchpad

## Directory Structure
- lab-d1-agentic-arch/: Agentic loop labs (basic_loop.py, multi_agent.py...)
- lab-d2-tool-design/: Tool quality and MCP integration
...

## Key Patterns Found
- All agents use while True + stop_reason pattern
- Error responses use isError/errorCategory/isRetryable structure
...

    Later, when asking about earlier findings:

    > What was the key pattern in the agentic loop files?

    Instead of relying on fading context memory:

    > Read /tmp/exploration-scratchpad.md and tell me about the agentic loop pattern

    Why this matters: After 2+ hours of exploration, the model may say "typically agents use X pattern" instead of "the lab files use Y pattern at line Z" — it's using generic knowledge instead of specific findings.

    Step 2: Create a crash recovery manifest

    Create lab-d5-context-reliability/crash_recovery.py:

    import json
import os
from pathlib import Path
import tempfile

# ── SCRATCHPAD PATTERN ───────────────────────────────────
# In long sessions, write key findings to a file as you go.
# When context degrades, read the scratchpad to restore knowledge.

SCRATCHPAD_PATH = Path(tempfile.gettempdir()) / "exploration-scratchpad.md"

def demonstrate_scratchpad():
    print("=== Scratchpad Pattern Demo ===\n")

    # Simulate finding things during exploration
    findings = [
        ("Turn 3", "Found agentic loop in basic_loop.py — uses stop_reason for control flow"),
        ("Turn 7", "Coordinator in multi_agent.py delegates to 3 subagents"),
        ("Turn 12", "Error handling uses 4 categories: transient, validation, business, permission"),
        ("Turn 18", "Session management: --resume for continuation, fork_session for branching"),
        ("Turn 25", "Human review uses stratified sampling + field-level confidence scores"),
    ]

    # Write findings to scratchpad as we discover them
    scratchpad_content = "# Exploration Scratchpad\n\n"
    for turn, finding in findings:
        scratchpad_content += f"## {turn}\n{finding}\n\n"
        print(f"  [{turn}] Found: {finding[:60]}...")
        print(f"  [{turn}] → Written to scratchpad")

    SCRATCHPAD_PATH.write_text(scratchpad_content)
    print(f"\n  Scratchpad saved: {SCRATCHPAD_PATH}")

    # Now simulate context degradation
    print("\n  --- After 30 turns, context is degraded ---")
    print("  Without scratchpad: 'The codebase typically uses standard patterns...'")
    print("  With scratchpad:")

    # Read scratchpad to restore knowledge
    restored = SCRATCHPAD_PATH.read_text()
    lines = [l for l in restored.split("\n") if l.startswith("Found") or l.startswith("Coordinator") or l.startswith("Error") or l.startswith("Session") or l.startswith("Human")]
    for line in lines[:3]:
        print(f"    → {line}")
    print(f"  ✓ Specific findings restored from scratchpad (not 'typical patterns')")


# ── MANIFEST-BASED CRASH RECOVERY ────────────────────────
# Each agent exports its state to a known location.
# On resume, coordinator loads the manifest and injects state into agent prompts.

MANIFEST_PATH = Path(tempfile.gettempdir()) / "research-manifest.json"

def save_agent_state(agent_name: str, state: dict):
    """Save agent state to manifest for crash recovery."""
    manifest = {}
    if MANIFEST_PATH.exists():
        manifest = json.loads(MANIFEST_PATH.read_text())

    manifest[agent_name] = {
        **state,
        "saved_at": "2024-01-15T10:30:00Z",
        "completed": state.get("completed", False)
    }
    MANIFEST_PATH.write_text(json.dumps(manifest, indent=2))
    print(f"[{agent_name}] State saved to manifest")


def load_manifest() -> dict:
    """Load manifest on resume."""
    if not MANIFEST_PATH.exists():
        return {}
    return json.loads(MANIFEST_PATH.read_text())


def build_resume_prompt(agent_name: str, manifest: dict, new_task: str) -> str:
    """Build prompt that injects prior state for resumed agent."""
    prior_state = manifest.get(agent_name, {})

    if not prior_state:
        return f"New task: {new_task}"

    return f"""
RESUMED SESSION: Prior work context below.

Prior state for {agent_name}:
- Last saved: {prior_state.get('saved_at')}
- Completed steps: {prior_state.get('completed_steps', [])}
- Key findings: {json.dumps(prior_state.get('findings', []), indent=2)}
- In progress at crash: {prior_state.get('in_progress_step', 'unknown')}

Continue from where the session was interrupted.
Do NOT re-do completed steps listed above.

Remaining task: {new_task}
"""


def demonstrate_crash_recovery():
    print("=== Crash Recovery Demo ===\n")

    # Simulate agent saving state mid-execution
    print("Simulating research agent running...")
    save_agent_state("search_agent", {
        "completed_steps": ["search_iea", "search_bloomberg"],
        "findings": [
            {"claim": "35% growth", "source": "iea.org"},
            {"claim": "$139/kWh", "source": "bloomberg.com"}
        ],
        "in_progress_step": "search_reuters",
        "completed": False
    })

    save_agent_state("analysis_agent", {
        "completed_steps": ["analyze_doc_1", "analyze_doc_2"],
        "findings": [
            {"claim": "Regional variation 3x", "doc": "report.pdf", "page": 12}
        ],
        "in_progress_step": "analyze_doc_3",
        "completed": False
    })

    print("\nSimulating crash...\n")

    # Resume: load manifest, build resume prompts
    print("Resuming after crash...")
    manifest = load_manifest()
    print(f"Manifest loaded: {list(manifest.keys())} agents with saved state\n")

    resume_prompt = build_resume_prompt(
        "search_agent",
        manifest,
        "Continue searching: reuters.com, ft.com, wsj.com"
    )
    print("Resume prompt for search_agent:")
    print(resume_prompt[:400])

    # Clean up
    if MANIFEST_PATH.exists():
        MANIFEST_PATH.unlink()


if __name__ == "__main__":
    demonstrate_scratchpad()
    print()
    demonstrate_crash_recovery()

    print("\n\n=== Context Degradation Patterns ===")
    print("""
    Symptoms of context degradation (extended sessions):
    - Model says "typically X" instead of "in file Y at line Z"
    - Model references "standard patterns" not the specific code
    - Answers become less specific over time
    - Model contradicts its own earlier findings

    Solutions:
    1. Scratchpad files: write key findings → reference later
    2. /compact: summarize context when full of verbose tool outputs
    3. Subagent delegation: spawn subagent for deep exploration,
       get summary back, keep main context clean
    4. Start fresh: when prior tool results are stale (code changed),
       start new session with injected summary

    Subagent delegation pattern:
    "Spawn a subagent to find all test files and count test cases per module.
     Return only a summary — I don't need the raw file contents."
    → Verbose exploration happens in isolated context
    → Main session only receives the summary
    """)

    # === Subagent Delegation Pattern (executable) ===
    print("\n=== Subagent Delegation Pattern ===")

    def simulate_subagent_exploration(task: str) -> dict:
        """
        Simulates a subagent doing verbose exploration and returning only a summary.
        In production, this would be a separate Claude instance via Task tool.
        """
        # Simulate verbose exploration (would be 20+ file reads in reality)
        verbose_output = [
            "Read lab-d1-agentic-arch/basic_loop.py → found agentic loop with stop_reason",
            "Read lab-d1-agentic-arch/multi_agent.py → found coordinator pattern",
            "Read lab-d2-tool-design/structured_errors.py → found 4 error categories",
            "Read lab-d3-claude-code/refinement_demo.py → found interview pattern",
            "Read lab-d4-prompt-eng/structured_output.py → found tool_use + JSON schema",
            "Read lab-d5-context-reliability/human_review.py → found stratified sampling",
        ]
        print(f"  [Subagent] Exploring: {task}")
        print(f"  [Subagent] Read {len(verbose_output)} files (verbose output isolated)")

        # Return ONLY the summary — not all the verbose file contents
        return {
            "summary": f"Found {len(verbose_output)} relevant files across 5 domains",
            "key_patterns": [
                "Agentic loop: stop_reason-based control flow",
                "Multi-agent: hub-and-spoke coordinator",
                "Error handling: 4 categories with isRetryable",
                "Structured output: tool_use + JSON schema",
                "Human review: stratified sampling + confidence calibration",
            ],
            "files_examined": len(verbose_output),
            # NOTE: Raw file contents NOT included — saves context budget
        }

    def main_agent_with_delegation():
        """Main agent delegates verbose exploration, preserves context."""
        print("\n  [Main Agent] I need to understand the full lab codebase...")
        print("  [Main Agent] Delegating exploration to subagent...")

        # Subagent does the heavy lifting in isolated context
        summary = simulate_subagent_exploration("Map all lab files and identify patterns")

        # Main agent only sees the summary — context stays clean
        print(f"\n  [Main Agent] Received summary: {summary['summary']}")
        print(f"  [Main Agent] Key patterns: {len(summary['key_patterns'])} found")
        print(f"  [Main Agent] My context is still clean — ready for the next task")

        return summary

    result = main_agent_with_delegation()
    print(f"\n  Benefit: Main agent context consumed ~200 tokens (summary)")
    print(f"  Without delegation: would have consumed ~10,000 tokens (all file contents)")

    • Crash recovery: agent state saved to manifest → simulated crash → manifest loaded → resume prompt built with prior state injected
    • Context degradation symptoms listed (says "typically" instead of specific findings)

    2. Don't use the manifest for crash recovery. Restart mid-exploration. What's lost? (Answer: everything since last checkpoint — manifest-based recovery is the only way to resume gracefully)
    3. Run verbose exploration in the main context instead of delegating to a subagent. What fills up? (Answer: your context window — verbose discovery output crowds out the actual work. Subagent delegation keeps main context clean)

    Exam Essentials

    • If the exam says "agent says 'typically uses X' instead of naming specific code" → context degradation
    • Wrong answer trap: "Increase context window" — doesn't fix progressive summarization
    • The correct pattern: Scratchpad files persist findings. Subagent delegation isolates verbose output. /compact reduces context usage.

    ✅ Lab 5.4 Checkpoint

    • Scratchpad files persist findings across context boundaries (write-as-you-go)

    • Subagent delegation: verbose exploration in isolated context, summary returned to main
    • Crash recovery: manifest-based state export → coordinator loads on resume
